(قَوْلُهُ: عَنْ أَعْرَاضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ) الحَاصِلُ: أنَّ العَرَضَ إمَّا ذَاتِيٌّ، وإمَّا غَرِيبٌ، فَالعَرَضُ الذَّاتِيُّ؛ مَا يَلْحَقُ الشَّيْءَ لِذَاتِهِ؛ أيْ: بِلَا وَاسِطَةٍ، وَذَلِكَ كَالتَّعَجُّبِ؛ أيْ: قَوْلِهِم: الإِنْسَانُ مُتَعَجِّبٌ، وَلَيْسَ المُرَادُ التَّعَجُّبَ اللَّازِمَ لِلْمُتَكَلِّمِ، كَمَا قِيلَ. العَطَّارُ: عَبْدُ الحَكِيمِ بَعْدَ أنْ نَقَلَ خُلَاصَةَ كَلَامِ الدَّوَّانِيِّ الَّذِي بَسَطْنَاهُ؛ مُوَضِّحاً: وَلَا يَخْفَى عَلَيْكَ أنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ حِينَئِذٍ؛ أيْ: حِينَ إذْ فُصِّلَ الإجْمَالُ بِهَذَا التَّفْصِيلِ دُخُولُ العِلْمِ الجُزْئِيِّ في العِلْمِ الكُلِّيِّ كَعِلْمِ الكُرَةِ المُتَحَرِّكَةِ في عِلْمِ الكُرَةِ، وَعِلْمِ الكُرَةِ في العِلْمِ الطَّبِيعِيِّ؛ لأنَّهُ يُبْحَثُ فِيهَا؛ أيْ: في تِلْكَ العُلُومِ عَنِ العَوَارِضِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ لِنَوْعِ الكُرَةِ أو لِلجِسْمِ الطَّبِيعِيِّ، أو لِعَرَضِهِ الذَّاتِيِّ، أو لِنَوْعِ عَرَضِهِ الذَّاتِيِّ، وَالَّذِي اخْتَارَهُ ذَلِكَ الفَاضِلُ في دَفْعِ الإشْكَالِ؛ أنَّ مَعْرِفَةَ الجُزْئِيَّاتِ بِخُصُوصِهَا لَمَّا كَانَتْ مُتَعَذِّرَةً؛ أَخَذُوا المَفْهُومَاتِ الكُلِّيَّةَ الصَّادِقَةَ عَلَيْهَا؛ ذَاتِيَّةً كَانَتْ أو عَرَضِيَّةً، وَبَحَثُوا عَنْ أَحْوَالِهَا مِنْ حَيْثُ انْطِبَاقُهَا عَلَيْهَا، وَلَمَّا كَانَتْ تِلْكَ الأَحْوَالُ مُتَكَثِّرَةً مُنْتَشِرَةً، وَضَبْطُهَا عَلَى هَذَا الوَجْهِ عَسِرٌ؛ اعْتَبَرُوا الأَحْوَالَ الذَّاتِيَّةَ لِمَفْهُومٍ مَفْهُومٍ، وَجَعَلُوهَا عِلْماً مُنْفَرِداً بِالتَّدْوِينِ، وَعَمَّمُوا الأَحْوَالَ الذَّاتِيَّةَ وَفَسَّرُوهَا بِمَا يَكُونُ مَحْمُولاً عَلَى ذَلِكَ المَفْهُومِ؛ إمَّا لِذَاتِهِ أو لِجُزْئِهِ الأَعَمِّ أو المُسَاوِي، فَإنَّ لَهُ اخْتِصَاصاً بِالشَّيْءِ مِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنُهُ مِنْ أَحْوَالٍ مُقَوِّمَةٍ، أو الخَارِجِ المُسَاوِي لَهُ؛ سَوَاءٌ كَانَ شَامِلاً لِجَمِيعِ
The subject of the science of logic is [concerned with] their essential attributes, and what is investigated in a science concerning its essential attributes, that is the subject of the science. Dassuqi: (His saying: concerning their attributes) i.e., their states, and the meaning of investigating in it concerning their states is that its subject is made the subject of its questions, and those attributes are predicated upon it, such as saying: 'the rational animal' is a definition, or 'animal' is a genus, or 'rational' is a differentia, or 'man' is a species. Accordingly, what is meant by 'attributes' are the essential [attributes]: generic, specific, and differential, and so on, so ponder. (His saying: concerning its essential attributes) The result: that an attribute is either essential or accidental (gharib). The essential attribute is what attaches to a thing in itself, i.e., without mediation, such as 'laughing'; i.e., their saying: 'Man is laughing', and what is meant is not the laughter which is necessary for the speaker, as was said. Al-Attar: Abd al-Hakim, after he transmitted the summary of Dawani's speech which we have expanded, clarifying: It is not hidden from you that it follows then, i.e., when the summary was detailed with this detail, the entry of the particular science into the universal science, like the science of the moving sphere into the science of the sphere, and the science of the sphere into natural science; because in those sciences one investigates the essential attributes of the type of sphere or of the natural body, or of its essential attribute, or of the type of its essential attribute. And what that virtuous scholar chose in repelling the problem is that since knowledge of particulars in their specificity is impossible, they took the universal concepts that are true of them, whether essential or accidental, and investigated their states in terms of their applicability to them. And since those states are numerous and scattered, and controlling them in this manner is difficult, they considered the essential states of a concept as concept, and made them a separate science by codification. And they generalized the essential states and interpreted them as what is predicated of that concept, either in itself or by its more general or coextensive part, for it has a specificity to the thing in terms of being from the constituent states, or the coextensive external [attribute]; whether it includes all of
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the definition of the subject of logic. It begins by stating that the subject of logic is 'their' essential attributes—referring to the two items (likely concepts and judgments). Then it clarifies: what is investigated in any science concerning its essential attributes is precisely the subject of that science. Dassuqi explains that the subject is made the topic of the questions, and attributes are predicated upon it. He gives examples from logic itself: 'rational animal' as definition, 'animal' as genus, 'rational' as differentia, 'man' as species—all are examples of essential attributes (generic, specific, differential). He then distinguishes essential attributes from accidental (gharib): essential attributes attach without mediation, like 'laughing' attaches to man. Al-Attar then addresses a problem: if we define subject as that which is investigated through its essential attributes, then particular sciences (e.g., the science of the moving sphere) would fall under more general sciences (science of spheres, natural science), leading to overlap. The solution, following Dawani, is that since particulars cannot be known in their specificity, we take universal concepts true of them and investigate those concepts' states. Because these states are many, we isolate the essential states of each concept and make them a distinct science. The essential states are defined as predicates that apply to the concept either directly, or through a more general or coextensive part, provided they are constitutive (muqawwima) or coextensive external attributes. This explains how sciences are demarcated without inappropriate overlap.

العلم الكلي

الأحوال الذاتية

Checkpoint
According to al-Attar's explanation, what is the reason that particular sciences would overlap with universal sciences when each is defined by its essential attributes?
A) The particular sciences study the same essential attributes as the universal sciences.
B) The particular sciences are too narrow to stand alone.
C) The essential attributes of the particular subject are contained within the essential attributes of the universal subject.
D) The universal sciences were established first.

Key Takeaway
The demarcation of sciences relies on universal concepts and the study of their essential states as separate disciplines, not on the particulars themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the science of hadith (mustalah al-hadith) and the science of fiqh. The subject of hadith science is the concept 'hadith' (report) and its essential states (e.g., chain authenticity, narrators). The subject of fiqh is the concept 'action' and its rulings. Using the solution, explain how these two sciences are demarcated without overlap, even though hadith provides evidence for fiqh.
?
Suppose a new science called 'the science of fasting' is proposed. Using the Dawani solution, identify what universal concept would be its subject, and what essential states would be investigated to make it a distinct science from fiqh.

What Is Mantiq?

Essential Accidents and the Subject of a Science
Al-Dasooqi defines essential accidents and explains their role in constituting the subject of a science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the essential accident ('arad dhati) in a given definition and explain how it constitutes the subject matter of a science.

Core Idea
The subject of a science is defined by its essential accidents, not by its individuals.

This passage from Al-Dasooqi's commentary explains a foundational concept: what makes a science a distinct field of inquiry is its subject matter, which is determined by the essential accidents that belong to that subject by virtue of itself. For example, logic investigates concepts and judgments not as isolated entities but through their essential accidents like universality and particularity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly defining any Islamic science, from usul al-fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of essential accident
Find where the author defines 'arad dhati: 'al-‘arad al-dhati ma yalhaqu al-shay’ li-dhatihi, ay bi-la wasita' (the essential accident is what attaches to a thing by virtue of itself, without intermediary).
2
Identify the example of essential accident
The passage gives 'ta'ajjub' (wonder) attaching to humans because of their rationality. This shows how an accident can be essential without being part of the definition.
3
Distinguish essential from foreign accidents
Contrast 'arad dhati with 'arad gharib (foreign accident), which attaches through an intermediary. For example, laughter attaches to humans because of wonder, not directly.
4
Apply the principle to a science
For logic, the essential accidents of concepts and judgments (e.g., universality, particularity) define its subject matter. The same applies to other Islamic sciences.
5
Resolve the problem of particular sciences
Al-Dasooqi explains that essential accidents can be considered at different levels of generality, so particular sciences fall under universal ones without losing their distinctness.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : (تعريف) أي موصل توصيلاً قريباً. و(قَوْلُهُ : أو الحيوان جنس... إلخ) أي : موصل : توصيلاً بعيداً فالبحث عن التعاريف والكليات من حيث التوصيل بقسميه القريب والبعيد، وكذا يقال في الحجج والقضايا وأحكامها ، وسيوضّح ذلك نقلاً عن شرح المطالع . ا . هـ الشَّرنوبي .
1
أفراد ذلك المفهوم على الإطلاق، أو مَعَ مُقابلة التّضاد أو العدمِ والمَلكة دونَ مقابلة السَّلْبِ والإيجاب؛ إذ المتقابِلَانِ تَقابُلَ الإيجاب والسَّلْبِ لا اختصاص لَهُما بمفهوم دونَ مَفهوم ضبطاً للانتشار بقدر الإمكان، فأثبتوا الأحوالَ الشَّامِلَةَ على الإطلاقِ لِنَفْسِ الموضوع، والشَّامِلَةَ معَ مُقابلها لأنواعه واللاحقة للخارج المساوي لأعراضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ، ثمَّ إِنَّ تِلكَ العوارض الذَّاتِيَّةَ لَهَا عوارض ذائيَّةٌ شاملة لها على الإطلاقِ أو على التّقابل، فأثبتوا العوارضَ الشَّاملةَ على الإطلاقِ لِنَفْسِ الأعراض الذَّاتِيَّةِ والشَّاملة على التَّقابُلِ لأنواع تلكَ الأعراض، وكذلك عوارض تلك العوارض، وهذه العوارض في الحقيقة قيود للأعراض المثبتة للموضوعِ أو لأنواعِهِ، إلا أنَّها لِكَثرةِ مَبَاحثِها ؛ جُعِلَتْ مَحمولاتٍ على الأعراض، وهذا تفصيلُ ما قالُوا : معنى البحث عن الأعراض الذاتية أن تثبت تلك الأعراضُ لِنَفْسِ الموضوع أو لأنواعِهِ أو لأعراضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ أو لأنواعها أو أعراض أنواعها، وبِمَا ذكرْنَا اندفعَ مَا قِيْلَ إِنَّهُ مَا مِنْ عِلْمٍ إِلَّا ويبحث فيهِ عَن أحوالِهِ المختصَّةِ بأنواعِهِ، فيكون بحثاً عن الأعراض الغريبة للحوقها بواسطة أمرٍ أخص، كما يبحث في الطَّبيعيّ عَن الأحوال المختصة بالمعادنِ والنَّباتِ والحيوان، وذلك لأنَّ المبحوث عنه في الطَّبيعيّ؛ أنَّ الجسم إمَّا ذو طبيعة أو ذو نَفْسٍ، آلي أو غيرُ اليّ، وهِيَ مِن عوارضهِ الذَّاتية، والبحثُ عَن الأحوال المختصة بالعناصر وبالمركَّبَاتِ التَّامَّةِ أو غيرِ التَّامَّةِ كُلُّهَا تَفصيل لهذهِ العوارض وقيودٌ لَهَا .ا.هـ. وهو كلامٌ مُحرَّر؛ إِلَّا أَنَّ فِيهِ خفاءٌ نُوضحه لك، وهو أنَّ معنى قولِهِ : سواءٌ كَانَ شَامِلاً لجميع أفراد ذلك المفهوم . . . إلخ ؛ انه اعتبر في العَرَضِ الذَّاتي شموله لجميع أفراد الموضوع؛ إمَّا على الانفرادِ أو على سبيل التَّقابُل ؛ فكلّ محمولات المسائل مع مقابلاتها ؛ أعني :
The subject matter of the science of logic is about the essential accidents of its two [objects: concepts and judgments]. And what is investigated in a science concerning its essential accidents is the subject of that science. Al-Dasooqi: His statement: 'about their accidents' means their states. The meaning of investigating their states is that the subject is made the subject of its issues and those accidents are predicated upon it, such as saying: 'rational animal' is a definition, or 'animal' is a genus, or 'rational' is a differentia, or 'human' is a species. Therefore, what is meant by accidents here are the essential ones: generic, specific, and differentiative, and so on. So reflect. His statement: 'about its essential accidents' the gist: an accident is either essential or foreign. The essential accident is what attaches to a thing by virtue of itself, i.e., without any intermediary, such as wonder, i.e., al-'Attar: 'Abd al-Hakim after summarizing al-Dawwani's speech which we have expanded, clarifying: It is not hidden from you that then, i.e., when the summary is detailed in this way, there follows the entry of a particular science into a universal science, like the science of a moving sphere into the science of the sphere, and the science of the sphere into natural science, because in those sciences they investigate the essential accidents of the type of sphere or of the natural body, or of its essential accident, or of the type of its essential accident. What that virtuous one chose to repel the difficulty is that since knowledge of particulars in their specificity is impossible, they took universal concepts that are true of them, whether essential or accidental, and investigated their states in terms of their applicability to them. And since those states are multiple and scattered, and controlling them in this way is difficult, they considered the essential states of a concept of a concept, and made it an independent science by codification. And they generalized the essential states and interpreted them as what is predicated of that concept: either by virtue of itself or by virtue of its more general part or its equal, for it has a specificity to the thing by virtue of being from its constitutive states or from the external equal to it, whether it includes all individuals of that concept absolutely, or along with the opposition of contrariety or privation and possession, without the opposition of negation and affirmation, since two things in the opposition of affirmation and negation have no specificity to one concept over another, as a control of dispersal as much as possible. So they established the states that include absolutely the subject itself, and the states that include with their opposite for its types, and the states that attach to the external equal to its essential accidents. Then those essential accidents have essential accidents that include them absolutely or in opposition. So they established the accidents that include absolutely the essential accidents themselves, and the accidents that include in opposition for the types of those accidents, and likewise the accidents of those accidents. And these accidents are in reality qualifications (quyud) for the accidents established for the subject or its types, except that due to the abundance of their discussions they were made as predicates on the accidents. This is the elaboration of what they said: the meaning of investigating essential accidents is to establish those accidents for the subject itself, or for its types, or for its essential accidents, or for their types, or for the accidents of their types. And by what we have mentioned, there is repelled what was said: 'There is no science except that it investigates the states specific to its types, so it would be an investigation of foreign accidents because they attach by means of a more specific thing', as it investigates in natural science states specific to minerals, plants, and animals. That is because what is investigated in natural science is that the body is either possessing a nature or possessing a soul, mechanical or non-mechanical, and these are among its essential accidents. And investigation of states specific to elements and to complete or incomplete compounds are all a detailing of these accidents and qualifications for them. End quote. It is well-composed speech, except that there is obscurity in it which we clarify for you: the meaning of his statement 'whether it includes all individuals of that concept... etc.' is that he considered in the essential accident its inclusion of all individuals of the subject: either individually or by way of opposition. So all predicates of the issues along with their opposites, I mean:
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the subject matter of logic and the concept of essential accidents ('arad dhati). The author, al-Dasooqi, begins by stating the fundamental principle: the subject of any science is defined by what it investigates regarding essential accidents. He then clarifies that 'essential accidents' here refer to the states (ahwal) that belong to the subject by virtue of itself, such as genus, species, and differentia. The example given is that of 'rational animal' as a definition, where 'animal' is genus and 'rational' is differentia. 

Al-Dasooqi then elaborates, citing al-'Attar, that an accident is either essential (dhati) or foreign (gharib). An essential accident is what attaches to a thing without any intermediary, like 'wonder' (ta'ajjub) that attaches to humans by virtue of their rational nature. The discussion then addresses a potential problem: how can a particular science, like the science of a moving sphere, be contained within a universal science, like natural science? The solution is that since direct knowledge of particulars is impossible, scholars take universal concepts that apply to those particulars and investigate their states in terms of applicability. However, due to the multiplicity of states, they restrict investigation to essential states of a concept, thereby defining a distinct science.

Furthermore, the author explains that essential accidents are considered with respect to their scope: they may include all individuals of the subject absolutely, or only in conjunction with their opposites (like contrariety or privation), but not with the opposition of affirmation and negation, because such opposites have no specificity. This leads to a hierarchy: the subject itself has essential accidents, those accidents have their own essential accidents, and so on, forming a chain of predicates that are in reality qualifications (quyud) for the subject. The passage concludes with an example from natural science, where investigating the body's possession of nature or soul is an essential accident, and further subdivisions (minerals, plants, animals) are merely detailing these essential accidents. A final note clarifies that the essential accident's inclusion of all individuals can be either individually or via opposition.

Key Terms
العرض الذاتي (al-‘arad al-dhati): An accident that attaches to a thing by virtue of itself, without any intermediary, such as wonder attaching to humans by virtue of their rationality. [Note: No significant shift; this term remains stable across the tradition.]
موضوع العلم (mawdu‘ al-‘ilm): The subject matter of a science is that which the science investigates in terms of its essential accidents. [Note: In later texts, the definition of subject matter may expand to include accidental properties, but here it is strictly essential.]
توصيل قريب/بعيد (tawsil qarib/ba‘id): A near or remote connection: in the footnote, 'definition' is a near connection (tawsil qarib), while genus, differentia, etc., are remote connections (tawsil ba‘id) in conveying the essence. [Note: This distinction appears in later commentaries; it is specific to the Sharh al-Matali‘ tradition.]

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'essential accident' ('arad dhati)?
A) Any property that a thing has.
B) A property that attaches to a thing by virtue of itself, without intermediary.
C) A property that defines the thing.
D) A property that is always present in the thing.

Key Takeaway
An essential accident is what attaches to a subject by virtue of itself, and the subject of any science is defined by its essential accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
10 min
The Subject of Logic: Essential Accidents of Knowledge Leading to the Unknown
Author defines logic's subject as essential accidents of concepts and assents leading to unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter of logic according to this passage and explain why it focuses on essential accidents rather than strange ones.

Core Idea
Logic studies the intrinsic guiding function of concepts and judgments, not their accidental attributes.

The passage you are about to read defines the very subject matter of logic (mawdu' al-mantiq). It is not a random collection of mental operations; it is a focused investigation into the essential accidents (al-a'rad al-dhatiyya) of conceptual and assent-based knowledge, considered from the specific angle of 'leading to the unknown' (al-isaal ila al-majhul). As you work through this lesson, you will dissect this definition and see how the commentators Dasuqi and Attar clarify what makes an accident 'essential' and what 'leading' means in both near and far senses.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence in the Arabic that states the subject matter of logic. It begins with 'مَوْضُوعُ عِلْمِ الْمَنْطِقِ' and continues with the investigation of essential accidents. Write it down word for word.
The core definition is the first sentence of the passage.
2
Distinguish essential vs strange accidents
Read Dasuqi's commentary on 'al-a'rad al-dhatiyya'. Explain the difference using the water-fire example: heat in water is a strange accident because it comes from fire, not from water's essence. Essential accidents are inseparable from the thing's nature.
Essential accidents are genuine states of the subject; strange accidents belong to another subject.
3
Understand 'leading' (al-isaal)
Identify the two modes of leading: near (qareeb) and far (ba'eed). Near leading is without intermediary (e.g., a definition directly conveys a concept). Far leading requires an intermediary (e.g., a term being universal/particular is not enough until combined with another term). Give an example of each from the text.
Near leading includes definition and description; far leading includes classification as universal/particular, genus/species.
4
Apply the definition to an objection
The passage anticipates an objection: the actual predicates in logical problems are things like 'genus' or 'definition', not 'leading'. Find the answer given: these predicates imply conveying to the unknown. For example, 'rational animal is a definition' means it leads to the concept directly.
Thus, the subject matter is indirectly about leading, even when the term 'isaal' is not explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَوْضُوعُ عِلْمِ الْمَنْطِقِ
وإنَّما قُلْنا : يُبحَثُ في المنطقِ عن الأعراض الذاتية للمعلومِ التَّصَوُّرِيِّ والتصديقي ؛ لأنَّ المنطقي يبحث عنهما من حيث الإيصال إلى مجهول تصوري أو تصديقي
الدسوقي
اللاحقة للماءِ بواسطةِ النَّار، وبينَ الماءِ والنَّار تباين وإنَّما سُمِّيت غريبة ؛ لأنَّها ؛ وإن كانت عارضة للمعروض ليسَتْ مُستندة لذاته، فهي غريبة وبعيدة عن ذاتِهِ . وإِنَّما كان يبحث في الفنّ عن الأعراض الذَّاتِيَّةِ لِلشَّيء دون أعراضه الغريبة؛ لأنَّ أعراضَهُ الذَّاتيّةَ أحوال له في الحقيقةِ، فَلِذا يبحث في الفنّ المتعلقِ بِهِ عنها بخلافِ أعراضِهِ الغريبةِ، فإنَّها في الحقيقة ليست أحوالاً له ، وإِنَّما هي أحوال للغيرِ الذي ثبتَتْ لِذلكَ الشَّيء بِسببه، فَلَا يبحث عنها في الفنّ المتعلّق بذلكَ الشَّيء، وإنَّما يبحث عنها في الفنّ المتعلّق بذلك الغير ؛ لأنَّ المقصود في كلِّ علم إنَّما البحث عن أحوالِ موضوعِهِ الحقيقية . (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا . . . إِلَخ) قصده بهذا : بيان كون المعلوماتِ التَّصوريَّةِ والتصديقيَّةِ يبحث عن عوارضهما الذاتية.
العطار
البحث عن التصوراتِ من حيثُ الإيصال للمجهول؛ إما أن يكونَ مِن حيثُ الإيصال القريب؛ أي: الإيصال بلا واسطة الذي هو فَرْدٌ مِنْ أفرادِ مُطلَقِ جسمٍ، فَيُقَالُ لِلمسائلِ الَّتي موضوعُها الكرةُ : عِلْمٌ جزئي باعتبار اندراجها تحتَ المسائل الباحثةِ عَنِ الجسم مِنْ حيثُ هو، وكَذَلِكَ الحال في الكُرَةِ المتحرّكةِ ؛ فَهذهِ عُلُومٌ ثلاثةٌ ؛ كُلُّ واحدٍ مِنهَا أَعمُ مِمَّا تحتَهُ؛ باعتبار اندراج بعضها في بعض، فالمندرج فيهِ عِلْمٌ كُلّي، والمندرج عِلْمٌ جُزئي، والمرادُ بِالعِلْمِ هَهُنَا : التصديقات المتعلقة بتلك المسائل؛ لا نَفْسُ الإدراك ولا الملكة كما قد يُتَوهَّمُ.
الدسوقي
ضميمةٍ كَالحدّ والرَّسم أو البعيدُ كَكَونِهَا : كُلِّيَّةً وجزئيّة، وذاتيَّةٌ وعَرَضيَّةً، وجنساً وفصلاً ، فإنَّ مُجرَّدَ أمر من هذه الأمور لا يوصل إلى التصورِ؛ ما لم ينضم إليه أمرٌ آخَرُ يحصل منهما الحدّ والرَّسم .
والبحث عن التصديقات من حيثُ الإيصالُ لِمجهولٍ ؛ إما من حيثُ يوصل إلى تصديق مجهول إيصالاً قريباً كالقياس والاستقراء والتَّمثيل، أو بعيداً كَكونها قضيَّةٌ و عكس قضيَّةٍ ونقيض قضيَّة، فإنَّها ما لم ينضم إليها قضيَّة؛ لا تُوصل إلى التصديق. ويبحث عن التصوراتِ مِن حيثُ إِنَّها توصل إلى تصديق إيصالاً أَبْعَدَ؛ كَكَونِها موضوعاتٍ أو محمولات، فإنَّها إنَّما توصل إليه إذا انضم إليه أمر آخر يحصل منهما قضيَّة، ثم انضم إليهما ضميمةٌ أُخرى حتّى يحصل القياس والاستقراءُ والتَّمثيل، ثمَّ لا يخفى أنَّ معنى البحثِ عَنِ المعلومين من حيثُ الإيصال المذكورُ؛ إثباتُ الإيصال لهما بحملِهِ عليهما، فيقتضي أنَّ الإيصالُ يُحملُ عليهما ، كَأَنْ يُقال: الحيوانُ النَّاطِقُ مُوصلٌ لِمَطلوب تصوري، والعالمُ مُتغيّر، وكلُّ مُتغيّر حادث ؛ موصل لمطلوب تصديقي، مع أن الذي يقعُ محمولاً في المسائل غير الإيصال المذكور ، كَالمحمول في قولنا : الحيوان جنس والنَّاطقُ فصل، والحيوانُ النَّاطِقُ حد، والحيوانُ الضَّاحكُ رسم ، والعالَمُ مَوضوع ، ومُتغيّرُ مَحمول، والعالَمُ مُتغيّرُ قضيَّة، والعالَمُ مُتغيّر، وكلُّ مُتغيّر حادث قياس، وهكذا .
أجيب : بأَنَّهُ إذا حكم على المعلوم النّصوريِّ بأَنَّهُ حدٌ أو رسمٌ؛ كَانَ مَعْناهُ أَنَّهُ موصل للمطلوب التَّصوري بلا واسطة، وإذا حُكم عليه بأنَّهُ كُلّيّ أو جنس أو فصل
The subject of the science of logic: It is investigated in logic about the essential accidents of conceptual knowledge and assent-based knowledge from the aspect of leading to an unknown conceptual or assent-based [thing]. And we only said: It is investigated in logic about the essential accidents of conceptual knowledge and assent-based knowledge because the logician investigates them from the aspect of leading to an unknown conceptual or assent-based [thing]. Al-Dasuqi: [They are] like those attaching to water by means of fire, and between water and fire there is opposition, and they are called 'strange' because even though they are accidental to the subject, they are not based on its essence, so they are strange and distant from its essence. And the investigation in an art is only about the essential accidents of a thing, not its strange accidents, because its essential accidents are its states in reality; therefore one investigates them in the art pertaining to it, unlike its strange accidents, for they are in reality not its states, but rather they are states of the other due to which the thing acquired them, so they are not investigated in the art pertaining to that thing, but are investigated in the art pertaining to that other, because the purpose in every science is only the investigation of the real states of its subject. (His statement: 'And we only said... etc.' His intent by this is to explain that conceptual and assent-based knowledge are investigated for their essential accidents. Al-'Attar: The investigation of concepts from the aspect of leading to the unknown: either it is from the aspect of near leading, i.e., leading without intermediary, which is an individual of the absolute 'body', so it is said of the problems whose subject is the sphere: partial science, considering their being subsumed under the problems investigating body qua body, and similarly the case for the moving sphere; so these are three sciences, each one more general than what is beneath it, considering the subsumption of some under others, so what subsumes is a universal science, and what is subsumed is a partial science, and what is meant by 'science' here is the assents pertaining to those problems, not perception itself nor the faculty as might be understood. Al-Dasuqi: As an appendage like definition and description, or far like being universal and particular, essential and accidental, genus and species, for a mere one of these matters does not lead to conceptualization unless another matter is joined to it from which definition and description result. And the investigation of assents from the aspect of leading to the unknown: either from the aspect of leading to an unknown assent through near leading like syllogism, induction, and analogy, or far like being a proposition, its converse, or its contradictory, for they do not lead to assent unless a proposition is joined to them. And it investigates concepts from the aspect that they lead to assent through a farther leading, like being subjects or predicates, for they only lead to it when another matter is joined to them from which a proposition results, then another appendage is joined to them until syllogism, induction, and analogy result. Then it is not hidden that the meaning of investigating the two known things from the aspect of the mentioned leading is establishing leading for them by predicating it of them, so it requires that leading is predicated of them, like saying: 'Rational animal leads to a conceptual demand, and the universe is changing, and everything changing is originated' leads to an assent-based demand, while what actually occurs as predicate in the problems is not the mentioned leading, like the predicate in our saying: 'Animal is a genus, Rational is a difference, Rational animal is a definition, Laughing animal is a description, The universe is a subject, Changing is a predicate, The universe is changing is a proposition, The universe is changing and everything changing is originated is a syllogism', and so on. The answer: If it is judged of a conceptual known that it is a definition or description, its meaning is that it leads to the conceptual demand without intermediary; and if it is judged that it is universal or genus or difference
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter of logic (mawdu' ilm al-mantiq) and expands on why logic specifically investigates the essential accidents ('arad dhatiyya) of conceptual and assent-based knowledge. The commentator Dasuqi begins by explaining the distinction between essential and strange (ghariba) accidents. He uses the example of water heated by fire: the heat is an accidental attribute, but it is not essential to water because it derives from an external cause (fire), whereas essential accidents are inseparable from the essence. Logic focuses only on essential accidents because they are genuine states of the subject, while strange accidents belong to another subject. Thus, logic examines what is intrinsic to concepts and assents in terms of their ability to lead (isaal) to the unknown. Al-'Attar then clarifies that investigation of concepts is either for near conveyance (e.g., definition or description) or far conveyance (e.g., being universal/particular). Similarly, assents are investigated for near conveyance (syllogism, induction, analogy) or far conveyance (proposition, converse, contradictory). The passage concludes by addressing a potential objection: although the actual predicates in logical problems are terms like 'genus' or 'definition', the underlying meaning is that these predicates imply conveyance to the unknown. For example, 'rational animal is a definition' means it conveys the concept directly. Thus, logic's subject is properly the essential accidents of knowledge regarding their guiding function.

A) Because essential accidents are more numerous than accidental ones.
B) Because essential accidents are the real states of the subject and directly relate to its guiding function.
C) Because accidental accidents are impossible to study.
D) Because logic is only concerned with assent-based knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The subject of logic is the essential accidents of conceptual and assent-based knowledge considered specifically in their capacity to lead the mind to the unknown.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
5 min
The Three Degrees of Leading to the Unknown
Defines the three degrees of leading (isal) for both conceptions and assents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three degrees of leading (isal) for both conceptions and assents, and explain how logical terms like genus and syllogism function as essential accidents indicating these degrees.

Core Idea
Leading (isal) is the aspect, not the predicate; terms like 'genus' and 'syllogism' are essential accidents that indicate the degree of leading.

The logician does not study just any property of known things; he studies them specifically under the aspect of how they lead the mind to the unknown. This passage unpacks the meaning of 'leading' (isal) and shows that when we call something a 'genus' or a 'syllogism', we are really indicating how directly or indirectly it leads to a desired conception or assent. Let's work through the three degrees for each type of known thing and understand the elegant answer to a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of known things
Identify that the logician investigates two categories: conceptions (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat). Each can lead to an unknown of the same type.
Conceptions are concepts; assents are judgments.
2
Find the three degrees of leading for conceptions
A conception can lead without intermediary (e.g., a complete definition like 'rational animal'), with one intermediary (e.g., 'genus' when combined with differentia), or with two intermediaries (e.g., being a subject or predicate that forms a proposition and then a syllogism).
The passage labels these as bila wasita, bi wasita wahida, bi wasitatayn.
3
Find the three degrees of leading for assents
An assent can lead without intermediary (e.g., a syllogism itself), with one intermediary (e.g., a proposition, its converse, or its contradictory), or with two intermediaries (e.g., being an antecedent or consequent in a conditional).
The same three-tier structure applies symmetrically.
4
Understand the objection and the answer
The objection: if the logician studies leading, why do the predicates in logical works say 'genus' or 'syllogism' instead of 'leads'? The answer: these predicates are essential accidents ('awarid dhatiyya) that mean 'leads with a certain degree'. When we say 'animal is genus', it means 'animal leads to the desired conception with one intermediary'.
The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'min haythu al-isal' refers to the aspect of leading, not the essence of leading itself.
5
Contrast with the alternative explanation
Note that the idafa (annexation) in 'min haythu al-isal' could be bayaniyya (explicative) or haqiqiyya (real). The real interpretation simply means 'from the aspect that leads', which is sufficient without the elaborate answer.
The upshot is that the essential accidents (like jinsiyya, haddiyya) are that aspect.

ضميمةٍ كَالحدّ والرَّسم أو البعيدُ كَكَونِهَا : كُلِّيَّةً وجزئيّة، وذاتيَّةٌ وعَرَضيَّةً، وجنساً وفصلاً ، فإنَّ مُجرَّدَ أمر من هذه الأمور لا يوصل إلى التصورِ؛ ما لم ينضم إليه أمرٌ آخَرُ يحصل منهما الحدّ والرَّسم .
والبحث عن التصديقات من حيثُ الإيصالُ لِمجهولٍ ; إما من حيثُ يوصل إلى تصديق مجهول إيصالاً قريباً كالقياس والاستقراء والتَّمثيل، أو بعيداً كَكونها قضيَّةٌ و عكس قضيَّةٍ ونقيض قضيَّة، فإنَّها ما لم ينضم إليها قضيَّة؛ لا تُوصل إلى التصديق. ويبحث عن التصوراتِ مِن حيثُ إِنَّها توصل إلى تصديق إيصالاً أَبْعَدَ؛ كَكَونِها موضوعاتٍ أو محمولات، فإنَّها إنَّما توصل إليه إذا انضم إليه أمر آخر يحصل منهما قضيَّة، ثم انضم إليهما ضميمةٌ أُخرى حتّى يحصل القياس والاستقراءُ والتَّمثيل، ثمَّ لا يخفى أنَّ معنى البحثِ عَنِ المعلومين من حيثُ الإيصال المذكورُ; إثباتُ الإيصال لهما بحملِهِ عليهما، فيقتضي أنَّ الإيصال يُحملُ عليهما ، كأَنْ يُقال: الحيوانُ النَّاطِقُ مُوصلٌ لِمَطلوب تصوري، والعالمُ مُتغيّر، وكلُّ مُتغيّر حادث ; موصل لمطلوب تصديقي، مع أن (۱) الَّذي يقعُ محمولاً في المسائل غير الإيصال المذكور ، كَالمحمول في قولنا : الحيوان جنس والنَّاطقُ فصل، والحيوانُ النَّاطِقُ حد، والحيوانُ الضَّاحكُ رسم ، والعالَمُ مَوضوع ، ومُتغيّرُ مَحمول، والعالَمُ مُتغيّرُ قضيَّة، والعالَمُ مُتغيّر، وكلُّ مُتغيّر حادث قياس، وهكذا .
أجيبُ : بأَنَّهُ إذا حكم على المعلوم النّصوريِّ بأَنَّهُ حدٌ أو رسمٌ; كَانَ مَعْناهُ أَنَّهُ موصل للمطلوب التَّصوري بلا واسطة، وإذا حُكم عليه بأنَّهُ كُلّيّ أو جنس أو فصل أو خاصَّة ; كانَ مَعناهُ أنَّهُ مُوصل للمطلوب النَّصوري بواسطة، وإذا حكم عليه بأنَّهُ موضوع أو محمول ; كانَ مَعناهُ أنَّهُ مُوصل للمطلوبِ التّصديقي بواسطتين، وهذهِ الأحوالُ الثَّلاثةُ الثَّابتةُ لِلمعلومِ التَّصوري هي المعبر عنها بأعراضه الذاتية . وإذا حُكِمَ على معلوم تصديقي بأنَّهُ قياس أو استقراء أو تمثيل ; كانَ مَعناهُ أَنَّهُ مُوصل للمطلوبِ التّصديقي بلا واسطة وإذا حكم عليه بأنَّهُ قضيَّةٌ، أو عكس قضيَّة، أو نقيضُ قضيَّة ; كان معناه أنَّهُ مُوصل للمطلوب التصديقي بواسطة. وإذا حكم على المعلوم التصديقي بأنَّهُ مقدَّم أو تالي; كانَ معناهُ أَنَّهُ مُوصل للمطلوبِ التصديقي بواسطتين، وهذه الأحوالُ الثَّلاثةُ الثَّابتةُ لِلمعلوم التصديقي هي المعبر عنها بأعراضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّة، فَقَوْلُ الشَّارح : لأنَّ المنطقي يبحثُ عَنهُمَا مِن حيثُ الإيصال; أي: مِن حيثُ ما هو بمعنى الإيصال ; أي: مِن حيثُ الشَّيءُ الَّذِي مَعناه الإيصال كالحدّيَّة، والجنسيَّة، والفصلية ... إلخ، وقد يُقالُ : لا داعي لذلكَ السُّؤال والجواب عنهُ بِمَا ذكر، إلا جعل الإضافةِ فى قوله : (من حيثُ الإيصال) بيانية، وليس بمتعيّن; لجواز جعلها حقيقيَّة; أي: إلا من جهة الإيصال; أي: إلَّا من الجهةِ الَّتي يكون بها الإيصال للمطلوبِ كَالجنسيَّةِ والحدّيَّةِ ... إلخ، والحاصل أنَّ قولَه : (مِن حيثُ الإيصال); أي: من الجهةِ الَّتي توصل للمطلوبِ; تَكون المعلوم التّصوري جنساً، أو فصلاً، أو عَرَضاً عاما، أو حدا ، أو رسماً، وكون المعلوم التصديقي قضيَّةً، أو عكس قضيَّة، أو نقيض قضيَّة، فَالعوارض تلك الجهةُ لَا نَفْسُ الإيصال .
a complement like the definition (hadd) or description (rasm), or distant like being: universal and particular, essential and accidental, genus and species. For a mere matter among these matters does not lead to conception (tasawwur) unless another matter joins it, from which the definition and description are obtained. And the investigation of assents (tasdiqat) from the perspective of leading (isal) to the unknown: either from the perspective that it leads to an unknown assent with a near leading like syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and example (tamthil); or with a distant leading like being a proposition ('qadiyya') and the converse of a proposition ('aks qadiyya') and the contradictory of a proposition (naqid qadiyya). For indeed, unless a proposition joins them, they do not lead to assent. And he investigates conceptions (tasawwurat) from the perspective that they lead to an even more distant leading, like being subjects (mawdu'at) or predicates (mahmulat). For indeed, they only lead to it when another matter joins them, from which a proposition is obtained; then another complement joins them until the syllogism, induction, and example are obtained. Then it is no secret that the meaning of the investigation of the two known things (al-ma'lumayn) from the perspective of the mentioned leading is the affirmation of leading for them by predicating it of them, so it necessitates that leading is predicated of them, as if it is said: 'Rational animal leads to a desired conception', and 'The world is changing, and every changing thing is temporally originated' leads to a desired assent. Despite that which occurs as a predicate in the issues is other than the mentioned leading, like the predicate in our saying: 'Animal is genus, and Rational is species, and Rational animal is definition, and Laughing animal is description, and The world is subject, and Changing is predicate, and The world is changing is a proposition, and The world is changing, and every changing thing is temporally originated is a syllogism', and so on. The answer: That when it is judged of the conceptual known thing that it is a definition or description, its meaning is that it leads to the desired conception without an intermediary. And when it is judged of it that it is universal, genus, species, or property (khassa), its meaning is that it leads to the desired conception with an intermediary. And when it is judged of it that it is subject or predicate, its meaning is that it leads to the desired assent with two intermediaries. And these three states that are constant for the conceptual known thing are what are expressed as its essential accidents ('awarid dhatiyya). And when it is judged of an assertoric known thing (ma'lum tasdiqi) that it is a syllogism, induction, or example, its meaning is that it leads to the desired assent without an intermediary. And when it is judged of it that it is a proposition, or the converse of a proposition, or the contradictory of a proposition, its meaning is that it leads to the desired assent with an intermediary. And when it is judged of the assertoric known thing that it is an antecedent (muqaddam) or consequent (tali), its meaning is that it leads to the desired assent with two intermediaries. And these three states that are constant for the assertoric known thing are what are expressed as its essential accidents. So the statement of the commentator: 'Because the logician investigates them from the perspective of leading' means from the perspective of that which is in the meaning of leading, i.e., from the perspective of the thing whose meaning is leading, like definitionality (haddiyya), genericity (jinsiyya), specific difference (fasliyya)... etc. And it may be said: There is no need for that question and answer about it what is mentioned, unless the annexation (idafa) in his statement 'from the perspective of leading' is made explicative (bayaniyya), but it is not determined because it is permissible to make it real (haqiqiyya), i.e., except from the aspect of leading, i.e., except from the aspect by which leading to the desired occurs, like genericity and definitionality... etc. And the upshot is that his statement 'from the perspective of leading' means from the aspect that leads to the desired: the conceptual known thing becomes genus, species, general accident ('arad 'amm), definition, or description; and the assertoric known thing becomes a proposition, converse of a proposition, or contradictory of a proposition. So the accidents are that aspect, not leading itself.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological expansion on the subject matter of logic. It explains what it means for the logician to study known things (al-ma'lumat) from the perspective of 'leading' (al-isal) to the unknown. The commentator (Dasuqi) begins by distinguishing two types of leading: near and distant, and then gives a detailed answer to a potential objection about whether the predicates used in logic (like 'genus', 'definition', etc.) are actually the same as 'leading'. The passage proceeds in the author's own order: first, it states that mere concepts like 'universal' or 'genus' do not lead to conception without being combined; then it discusses assents (tasdiqat) and how they lead to unknown assents either directly (through qiyas, istiqra', tamthil) or indirectly (through being a proposition, etc.). It then addresses conceptions (tasawwurat) and their even more distant leading to assent. The core argument is that when the logician predicates something like 'genus' or 'definition' of a concept, what is really being predicated is that the concept leads to the desired knowledge at a certain degree of proximity. The answer breaks down three levels for each: for conceptions, a term like 'hadd' or 'rasm' leads directly ('bila wasita'), 'kulli' or 'jins' leads with one intermediary, and 'mawdu'' or 'mahmul' leads with two intermediaries. For assents, 'qiyas' etc. lead directly, 'qadiyya' etc. lead with one intermediary, and 'muqaddam' or 'tali' lead with two intermediaries. These three states are the 'essential accidents' ('awarid dhatiyya) of the known things. Finally, the commentator explains that the phrase 'min haythu al-isal' (from the perspective of leading) refers to the aspect that leads, not the leading itself, and that this interpretation resolves any confusion. All examples follow Islamic sciences: e.g., rational animal as a definition, the world changing and every changing thing being temporally originated as a syllogism.

المطلوب التصوري
المطلوب التصديقي
العارض الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, when a conceptual known thing is judged to be a 'genus' (jins), how does it lead to the desired conception?
A) Without any intermediary (bila wasita)
B) With one intermediary (bi wasita wahida)
C) With two intermediaries (bi wasitatayn)
D) It does not lead at all

Key Takeaway
The three degrees of leading (bila wasita, bi wasita, bi wasitatayn) apply symmetrically to conceptions and assents, and the logical terms used in textbooks are essential accidents that indicate these degrees.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the degree of leading for the following: 'الحيوان الناطق' (rational animal) as a definition, and 'العالَمُ مُتغيّر' (the world is changing) as a proposition. Explain your reasoning using the three-tier framework from the passage.

Given the syllogism: 'العالَمُ مُتغيّر، وكلُّ مُتغيّر حادث' (The world is changing, and every changing thing is temporally originated), state whether it leads to assent directly or with intermediaries, and justify your answer by locating the exact phrase in the passage.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner

Essential Distinction of Sciences by Subject
Author states principle and gives essential example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two sciences are distinguished by essence or by consideration based on their subjects.

Core Idea
Sciences are distinguished primarily by their subject matters, either in essence or by consideration.

In this lesson, we learn the foundational principle that each science is defined by its subject matter. The author illustrates this with the example of fiqh and usul al-fiqh, whose subjects are inherently different. Understanding this distinction helps us classify Islamic sciences correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sciences being compared
Look at the example: fiqh and usul al-fiqh. Note that the author explicitly states that they are distinguished because their subjects are different.
The subjects are the actions of the mukallaf and the scriptural evidences.
2
Extract the subject of each science
Fiqh's subject is the actions of the mukallaf in terms of halal/haram and sahih/fasid. Usul's subject is the scriptural evidences in terms of deriving rulings.
These are different in essence.
3
Determine the type of distinction
Since the subjects are different in themselves, the distinction is essential (tamayyuz bi al-dhat).
If subjects were the same but considered differently, it would be by consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَوْضُوعُ عِلْمِ الْمَنْطِقِ
تميز إلا بتمايز الموضوعاتِ، فإِنَّ عِلْمَ الفقه مثلاً، إنَّما امتاز عن علم أصول الفقه ؛ لأنَّ موضوعيهما مُتمايزان.
فَموضوعُ الفقه أفعال المكلَّفين؛ لأنَّ الفقية يبحث عنها من حيث الحِلّ والحُرمة، والصّحَّةُ والفساد.
وموضوع الأصول الأدلَّةُ السَّمعيَّةُ ؛ لأنَّ الأصولي يبحث عنها من حيث استنباط الأحكام الشَّرعيَّةِ منها.
(قَوْلُهُ : زِيَادَةَ تَميُّز أي : وأمَّا أصلُ التَّميز ؛ فهو حاصل بتصورِ العِلْمِ بالتّعريف .)
(قَوْلُهُ : إِلَّا بِتَمَايُزِ الْمَوْضُوْعَاتِ) أي : بأن كانَتْ مُتغايرة ذاتاً واعتباراً؛ كَموضوع عِلْمِ الفقهِ وَمَوضوع عِلْم النَّحو، أَوْ كانَت مُتَحدةٌ ذاتاً مختلفةً اعتباراً ؛ كَموضوع النَّحو والصَّرفِ ؛ فإِنَّهُ الكلماتُ العربيَّة، لكنَّها من حيثُ الإعراب والبناء؛ موضوع النَّحو، وَمِن حيثُ الإعلالُ والصَّحَةُ ؛ موضوعُ عِلْم الصَّرْفِ، وذلك لأنَّ المقصود مِنَ العلوم بيان أحوال الأشياء، ومعرفة أحكامها، فإذا كانَتْ طائفةٌ من الأحوالِ والأحكام متعلّقةً بشيء واحد، أو بأشياء متناسبة، وطائفةٌ أُخرى منها مُتعلّقةً بشيء آخر، أو بأشياءَ مُتناسبة أُخرى ؛ كانَتْ كلُّ واحدةٍ مِنَ الطَّائفتين علماً برأسها ممتازة عن الأُخرى، ولو كانَتِ الطَّائفتانِ مُتعلّقتين بشيءٍ واحدٍ ؛ لَكَانَنَا عَلَماً واحداً، ولم يستحقَّ عدُّ كلّ واحدة منهما علَماً على حدة.
تمايز العُلُومِ بحسب تمايز الموضوعات، فإن تمايزا بالذَّاتِ؛ كانَ تمايز العِلْمَيْنِ كذلك كَعِلْمٍ أُصولِ الفقهِ وعِلْمِ الفقه، وإنْ تَمَايزا بالاعتبار كَمَا في العلوم الأدبيَّةِ؛ كان تمايز العِلْمَيْنِ كَذلك ، ومِنَ التَّمايز الاعتباري القَوْلُ في أجرامِ العَالَمِ، فَإِنَّهَا مِن حيثُ الشَّكلُ كَكَوْنِهَا كُرويَّةً مثلاً ؛ موضوعُ عِلْمِ الهيئةِ، ومِنْ حيثُ الطَّبيعةُ كَكَوْنِ بعضها أجساماً بسيطةً وبعضها أجساماً غير بسيطةٍ؛ موضوع قسم بحثِ السَّماء والعالم مِنَ العِلْمِ الطَّبيعي، قال عبد الحكيم: ولذلك قد يتَّفِقُ اتِّحَادُ بعض المسائل في العِلْمَينِ بالموضوع والمحمول، ويختلفان بالبرهان؛ كَالقَولِ بأَنَّ الأرضَ مُستديرة ا.هـ. ، يعني: أنَّ القَوْلَ باستدارة الأرض مبحوث عنه في عِلْم الهيئة ومَبْعُوثُ عنه في العِلْم الطَّبيعي لكنَّه في الهيئة يثبتُ بالبرهان الآني، وفي الطبيعي بالبرهان اللُّمي.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَلَوْ لَمْ يَعْلَمِ الشَّارِعُ أَي يصدّق؛ لأنَّ العِلْمَ المتعلّق بموضوعيَّةِ الموضوعِ عِلْمٌ تَصديقيٌّ كَمَا تقدَّمَ.)
The subject of the science of logic: it is not distinguished except by the distinction of subjects. For the science of fiqh, for example, is only distinguished from the science of usul al-fiqh because their two subjects are distinct. The subject of fiqh is the actions of the legally responsible individuals, because the jurist investigates them in terms of lawful and forbidden, and validity and invalidity. The subject of usul is the scriptural evidences, because the usuli investigates them in terms of deriving legal rulings from them. (His statement: 'increase of distinction' means: as for the basis of distinction, it is attained by conceptualizing the science through its definition. His statement: 'except by the distinction of the subjects' means: that they are different in essence and consideration, like the subject of the science of fiqh and the subject of the science of grammar, or they are unified in essence but different in consideration, like the subject of grammar and morphology; for it is Arabic words, but from the perspective of inflection and construction it is the subject of grammar, and from the perspective of defectiveness and soundness it is the subject of morphology. This is because the purpose of the sciences is to explain the states of things and know their rulings. So if a class of states and rulings is related to one thing, or to appropriately related things, and another class of them is related to another thing, or to other appropriately related things, then each of the two classes becomes a science by itself, distinguished from the other. If the two classes were related to one thing, they would be one science, and it would not deserve to count each of them as a separate science. The distinction of sciences is according to the distinction of the subjects. If they are distinct by essence, then the distinction of the two sciences is likewise, like the science of usul al-fiqh and the science of fiqh. If they are distinct by consideration, as in the literary sciences, then the distinction of the two sciences is likewise. From the distinction by consideration is the statement concerning the celestial bodies; for from the perspective of shape, such as their being spherical for example, it is the subject of astronomy; and from the perspective of nature, such as some being simple bodies and some not simple, it is the subject of the section on heaven and the world from physics. Abd al-Hakim said: Therefore sometimes some issues may be unified in the two sciences in subject and predicate, but they differ in the proof, like the statement that the earth is round. That is, the statement about the roundness of the earth is investigated in astronomy and investigated in physics, but in astronomy it is proven by a demonstration of existence (burhan anni), and in physics by a demonstration of cause (burhan limmi). (His statement: 'If the student does not know' means he assents; because the knowledge related to the subject-hood of the subject is tasdiqi knowledge, as was previously mentioned.)
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory discussion about how sciences are distinguished from each other. It begins by establishing the general principle: a science is distinguished from another only by the distinction of their subject matters. The author immediately provides an example from the Islamic sciences: fiqh and usul al-fiqh are distinct because their subjects are different—fiqh deals with the actions of the mukallaf (legally responsible person) in terms of halal/haram and sahih/fasid, while usul deals with the scriptural evidences from which legal rulings are derived. This is a case of essential distinction (tamayyuz bi al-dhat) because the subjects are inherently different. The commentary (from al-Dasuqi) clarifies that the basic distinction is already obtained by conceptualizing the science through its definition, but the increase in distinction (ziyadat tamayyuz) is through the subject. The commentator then elaborates on the distinction by consideration (tamayyuz bi al-i'tibar), giving the example of grammar (nahw) and morphology (sarf). Both sciences study Arabic words, but grammar considers them from the perspective of i'rab (inflection) and bina' (construction), while morphology considers them from the perspective of i'lal (defectiveness) and sihha (soundness). Thus, the subject is one (Arabic words) but the aspects (i'tibar) differ. The passage then generalizes: the purpose of sciences is to explain the states and rulings of things. If a set of states and rulings relates to one thing or appropriately related things, and another set relates to a different thing or other appropriately related things, then each set constitutes a separate science. If they relate to the same thing, they would be one science. The text then returns to the principle, now explicitly stating that sciences are distinguished according to the distinction of their subjects. If the subjects are distinct in essence (like fiqh and usul), the sciences are distinct in essence. If they are distinct by consideration (like nahw and sarf), the sciences are distinct by consideration. A further example from the natural sciences is given: astronomy and physics both study the celestial bodies, but from different considerations. Astronomy considers their shape (e.g., spherical), while physics considers their nature (simple or composite bodies). The passage also quotes Abd al-Hakim, noting that sometimes the same proposition (e.g., 'the earth is round') can be investigated in both sciences but proven by different types of proof: in astronomy by a demonstration of existence (burhan anni, proving that it is round) and in physics by a demonstration of cause (burhan limmi, proving why it is round). Finally, the passage concludes with a practical implication: if the student (shari') does not know the subject of a science, he will not achieve increased distinction or increased insight. The commentary explains that the knowledge of the subject's being the subject is tasdiqi (assent) knowledge. Overall, the passage establishes that the subject matter is the primary basis for distinguishing sciences, whether by essence or by consideration, and that knowing the subject is essential for proper inquiry.

موضوع العلم
تمايز بالذات

A) Their methods are different.
B) Their subjects are different.
C) Their proofs are different.
D) Their goals are different.

Key Takeaway
The subject matter is the primary basis for distinguishing sciences, and fiqh and usul al-fiqh are a clear example of essential distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What Is Mantiq?
beginner

Distinction by Consideration: Grammar and Morphology
Author explains distinction by consideration using nahw and sarf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how two sciences can share the same subject but be distinguished by different aspects.

Core Idea
The same subject can be the basis for multiple sciences when studied from different perspectives.

In this lesson, we explore how sciences can be distinguished not by having different subjects, but by considering different aspects of the same subject. The author uses the example of grammar (nahw) and morphology (sarf), both studying Arabic words but from different angles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common subject
Both nahw and sarf study Arabic words. This is the shared subject.
The subject is the same in essence.
2
Identify the different aspects
Nahw considers words from the perspective of i'rab (inflection) and bina' (construction). Sarf considers them from i'lal (defectiveness) and sihha (soundness).
These are two different considerations (i'tibaran).
3
Understand why they are separate sciences
Because the class of states and rulings related to inflection is different from those related to defectiveness, they constitute separate sciences.
If the two classes were related to the same aspect, they would be one science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَوْضُوعُ عِلْمِ الْمَنْطِقِ
تميز إلا بتمايز الموضوعاتِ، فإِنَّ عِلْمَ الفقه مثلاً، إنَّما امتاز عن علم أصول الفقه ؛ لأنَّ موضوعيهما مُتمايزان.
فَموضوعُ الفقه أفعال المكلَّفين؛ لأنَّ الفقية يبحث عنها من حيث الحِلّ والحُرمة، والصّحَّةُ والفساد.
وموضوع الأصول الأدلَّةُ السَّمعيَّةُ ؛ لأنَّ الأصولي يبحث عنها من حيث استنباط الأحكام الشَّرعيَّةِ منها.
(قَوْلُهُ : زِيَادَةَ تَميُّز أي : وأمَّا أصلُ التَّميز ؛ فهو حاصل بتصورِ العِلْمِ بالتّعريف .)
(قَوْلُهُ : إِلَّا بِتَمَايُزِ الْمَوْضُوْعَاتِ) أي : بأن كانَتْ مُتغايرة ذاتاً واعتباراً؛ كَموضوع عِلْمِ الفقهِ وَمَوضوع عِلْم النَّحو، أَوْ كانَت مُتَحدةٌ ذاتاً مختلفةً اعتباراً ؛ كَموضوع النَّحو والصَّرفِ ؛ فإِنَّهُ الكلماتُ العربيَّة، لكنَّها من حيثُ الإعراب والبناء؛ موضوع النَّحو، وَمِن حيثُ الإعلالُ والصَّحَةُ ؛ موضوعُ عِلْم الصَّرْفِ، وذلك لأنَّ المقصود مِنَ العلوم بيان أحوال الأشياء، ومعرفة أحكامها، فإذا كانَتْ طائفةٌ من الأحوالِ والأحكام متعلّقةً بشيء واحد، أو بأشياء متناسبة، وطائفةٌ أُخرى منها مُتعلّقةً بشيء آخر، أو بأشياءَ مُتناسبة أُخرى ؛ كانَتْ كلُّ واحدةٍ مِنَ الطَّائفتين علماً برأسها ممتازة عن الأُخرى، ولو كانَتِ الطَّائفتانِ مُتعلّقتين بشيءٍ واحدٍ ؛ لَكَانَنَا عَلَماً واحداً، ولم يستحقَّ عدُّ كلّ واحدة منهما علَماً على حدة.
تمايز العُلُومِ بحسب تمايز الموضوعات، فإن تمايزا بالذَّاتِ؛ كانَ تمايز العِلْمَيْنِ كذلك كَعِلْمٍ أُصولِ الفقهِ وعِلْمِ الفقه، وإنْ تَمَايزا بالاعتبار كَمَا في العلوم الأدبيَّةِ؛ كان تمايز العِلْمَيْنِ كَذلك ، ومِنَ التَّمايز الاعتباري القَوْلُ في أجرامِ العَالَمِ، فَإِنَّهَا مِن حيثُ الشَّكلُ كَكَوْنِهَا كُرويَّةً مثلاً ؛ موضوعُ عِلْمِ الهيئةِ، ومِنْ حيثُ الطَّبيعةُ كَكَوْنِ بعضها أجساماً بسيطةً وبعضها أجساماً غير بسيطةٍ؛ موضوع قسم بحثِ السَّماء والعالم مِنَ العِلْمِ الطَّبيعي، قال عبد الحكيم: ولذلك قد يتَّفِقُ اتِّحَادُ بعض المسائل في العِلْمَينِ بالموضوع والمحمول، ويختلفان بالبرهان؛ كَالقَولِ بأَنَّ الأرضَ مُستديرة ا.هـ. ، يعني: أنَّ القَوْلَ باستدارة الأرض مبحوث عنه في عِلْم الهيئة ومَبْعُوثُ عنه في العِلْم الطَّبيعي لكنَّه في الهيئة يثبتُ بالبرهان الآني، وفي الطبيعي بالبرهان اللُّمي.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَلَوْ لَمْ يَعْلَمِ الشَّارِعُ أَي يصدّق؛ لأنَّ العِلْمَ المتعلّق بموضوعيَّةِ الموضوعِ عِلْمٌ تَصديقيٌّ كَمَا تقدَّمَ.)
The subject of the science of logic: it is not distinguished except by the distinction of subjects. For the science of fiqh, for example, is only distinguished from the science of usul al-fiqh because their two subjects are distinct. The subject of fiqh is the actions of the legally responsible individuals, because the jurist investigates them in terms of lawful and forbidden, and validity and invalidity. The subject of usul is the scriptural evidences, because the usuli investigates them in terms of deriving legal rulings from them. (His statement: 'increase of distinction' means: as for the basis of distinction, it is attained by conceptualizing the science through its definition. This is because the purpose of the sciences is to explain the states of things and know their rulings. So if a class of states and rulings is related to one thing, or to appropriately related things, and another class of them is related to another thing, or to other appropriately related things, then each of the two classes becomes a science by itself, distinguished from the other. If the two classes were related to one thing, they would be one science, and it would not deserve to count each of them as a separate science. The distinction of sciences is according to the distinction of the subjects. If they are distinct by essence, then the distinction of the two sciences is likewise, like the science of usul al-fiqh and the science of fiqh. If they are distinct by consideration, as in the literary sciences, then the distinction of the two sciences is likewise. (His statement: 'If the student does not know' means he assents; because the knowledge related to the subject-hood of the subject is tasdiqi knowledge, as was previously mentioned.)

What Is Mantiq?

Same Proposition, Different Proofs: Astronomy and Physics
Author gives example of astronomy and physics with different proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between burhan anni and burhan limmi and explain how the same proposition can be investigated in different sciences.

Core Idea
The same statement can be proven by existence or by cause, depending on the science.

In this final lesson, we see an advanced application of distinction by consideration: the same proposition (e.g., 'the earth is round') can be studied in two different sciences, astronomy and physics, each using a different type of proof. The author quotes Abd al-Hakim to illustrate this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared proposition
The proposition is that the earth is round (al-ardu mustadira).
This is the same claim investigated in both sciences.
2
Identify the two sciences and their considerations
Astronomy considers the celestial bodies from the perspective of shape (e.g., spherical). Physics considers them from the perspective of nature (e.g., simple or composite bodies).
Thus, the same object (earth) is studied under different aspects.
3
Understand the different proofs
In astronomy, the roundness is proven by burhan anni (demonstration of existence), which proves that the earth is round without explaining why. In physics, it is proven by burhan limmi (demonstration of cause), which explains the cause (e.g., the nature of celestial bodies).
This is why the same proposition can appear in both sciences.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory discussion about how sciences are distinguished from each other. It begins by establishing the general principle: a science is distinguished from another only by the distinction of their subject matters. The author immediately provides an example from the Islamic sciences: fiqh and usul al-fiqh are distinct because their subjects are different—fiqh deals with the actions of the mukallaf (legally responsible person) in terms of halal/haram and sahih/fasid, while usul deals with the scriptural evidences from which legal rulings are derived. This is a case of essential distinction (tamayyuz bi al-dhat) because the subjects are inherently different. The commentary (from al-Dasuqi) clarifies that the basic distinction is already obtained by conceptualizing the science through its definition, but the increase in distinction (ziyadat tamayyuz) is through the subject. The commentator then elaborates on the distinction by consideration (tamayyuz bi al-i'tibar), giving the example of grammar (nahw) and morphology (sarf). Both sciences study Arabic words, but grammar considers them from the perspective of i'rab (inflection) and bina' (construction), while morphology considers them from the perspective of i'lal (defectiveness) and sihha (soundness). Thus, the subject is one (Arabic words) but the aspects (i'tibar) differ. The passage then generalizes: the purpose of sciences is to explain the states and rulings of things. If a set of states and rulings relates to one thing or appropriately related things, and another set relates to a different thing or other appropriately related things, then each set constitutes a separate science. If they relate to the same thing, they would be one science. The text then returns to the principle, now explicitly stating that sciences are distinguished according to the distinction of their subjects. If the subjects are distinct in essence (like fiqh and usul), the sciences are distinct in essence. If they are distinct by consideration (like nahw and sarf), the sciences are distinct by consideration. A further example from the natural sciences is given: astronomy and physics both study the celestial bodies, but from different considerations. Astronomy considers their shape (e.g., spherical), while physics considers their nature (simple or composite bodies). The passage also quotes Abd al-Hakim, noting that sometimes the same proposition (e.g., 'the earth is round') can be investigated in both sciences but proven by different types of proof: in astronomy by a demonstration of existence (burhan anni, proving that it is round) and in physics by a demonstration of cause (burhan limmi, proving why it is round). Finally, the passage concludes with a practical implication: if the student (shari') does not know the subject of a science, he will not achieve increased distinction or increased insight. The commentary explains that the knowledge of the subject's being the subject is tasdiqi (assent) knowledge. Overall, the passage establishes that the subject matter is the primary basis for distinguishing sciences, whether by essence or by consideration, and that knowing the subject is essential for proper inquiry.

برهان آني
برهان لمي

What Is Mantiq?

The Three Rulings of Signification
Al-Dasuqi defines three rulings for the three significations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three rulings of signification as defined by al-Dasuqi and explain the relationships of necessity and non-necessity among the three types.

Core Idea
Conformity signification is necessary for inclusion and implication, but not vice versa.

Al-Dasuqi opens the chapter by stating that the three significations have three distinct rulings. These rulings describe logical relationships of necessity between the types. Understanding them is essential before moving into topics on words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
The three types are conformity (mutabaqah), inclusion (tadammun), and implication (iltizam). Conformity means the signifier signifies its entire meaning; inclusion signifies a part; implication signifies something external that is necessarily implied.
Example: 'house' signifies the whole building (conformity), a wall (inclusion), and a roof (implication).
2
Extract the three rulings from al-Dasuqi's statement
Al-Dasuqi says: (1) Conformity is necessary for both inclusion and implication; (2) Inclusion does not necessitate implication; (3) Implication does not necessitate inclusion. These are three rulings.
The necessity is one-way: if A includes B or implies B, then A's conformity holds, but not the reverse.
3
Verify the logical relationships with an Islamic example
Take the word 'salah' (prayer). Its conformity signifies the full act of worship. Inclusion signifies ruku' (bowing). Implication signifies wudu (ablution). The rulings: if the word 'salah' is used for ruku' (inclusion), then it must also refer to the full prayer (conformity) logically, but the reverse is not needed. Similarly, if it implies wudu, conformity is also needed.
This shows how the rulings govern the logical connections between the signification types.

(قَوْلُهُ : فِي مَنْهُوْمِ المُوصل ) أي : أفراد مفهوم الموصل؛ لِمَا تقدَّمَ لكَ مِنَ البحث في ذلك، ومَا قِيْلَ: إنَّه لا يُنافي ؛ هذا ما أشار إليهِ سَابِقاً مِنْ أَنَّ المرادَ مِنَ المعلومين الماصدقُ ؛ لأنَّ المعلومين المذكورينَ هِيَ مَفاهيم أيضاً موصلةٌ ليسَ بشيءٍ؛ لأنَّ الموصل مفهوم الماصدقات؛ لا نَفْسُ المفاهيم التي تصدق عليها، فيلزمُ المحذورُ السَّابق .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَتَوَقُفِ إِفَادَةِ ... إلخ) مِن مُجملةِ التَّعليل ؛ فَالعَطْفُ مُلاحَظٌ قَبلَهُ، فالعِلَّهُ مُركَّبَةٌ مِنَ الأمرين، وإلا ؛ فانحصار نظرِ المنطقي المذكور لا ينتج استحقاق تقدم هذا الفصل، بلْ رُبَّما يوهم عدم الاحتياج لِذِكْرِهِ، وأَمَّا قَوْلُهُ : بعد الفراغِ مِنَ المقدّمة ؛ فَلَا مدخلَ لَهُ في التَّعليل، وإنَّما هو رجوع للواقع.
Chapter: On the definition of the three significations and their rulings
It is worthy of being presented after completing the introduction.
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: and their rulings) They are the necessity of conformity to inclusion and implication without reverse, and the non-necessity of inclusion for implication and its reverse, so the rulings are three.
(His statement: and it) i.e., this chapter (worthy).
Al-Attar: Chapter: On signification
(His statement: and their rulings) It is the necessity of inclusion and implication for conformity, as he will say later: conformity is necessary for them even if hypothetically. The author mentioned in the chapter the topics of words; so the commentator should have addressed that. Thus in the gloss, perhaps his copy from which he wrote had missing the word 'chapter' after his statement: conformity is necessary for them even if hypothetically. As for the copy that mentions the chapter after that, there is no basis for what he said. And what he objected to, that the topics mentioned in this chapter up to the topics of universals are rulings of signification, is from lack of deliberation. Because the meaning of rulings of signification is that predicates are affirmed of them and judgments are made about them, so they become subjects for those predicates, as is said: conformity signification is such, inclusion signification is such, etc. And his statement: conformity is necessary for them... etc.; by saying: inclusion and implication are necessary for conformity, for example, up to the end of the rulings. As for the topics of words, they are issues whose subject is the word, so it is said, for example: the word is such, the simple and composite are such, up to the end of the subsequent topics. And the effort to refer those topics to rulings of signification by interpreting that the signification of conformity, its signifier is sometimes composite with its divisions, and sometimes simple with its divisions, up to the end of what is mentioned in the chapter, is unnecessary, since ultimately it returns to the attribution of those rulings to the signifier itself, where he said: its signifier is such... etc. It is as if it is a negligence of their statement: informing after knowledge of them are attributes. For if the rulings mentioned in those topics relate to signification, then describing signification with those rulings is valid, and no rational person, let alone a virtuous one, may say: signification is composite, signification is simple, signification is literal, signification is metaphorical... etc., in the subsequent topics. But if we undertake this interpretation and correct it, then all the rulings of words return to signification, so reflect. It cannot be said that what is mentioned in this chapter and after it are definitions, because we say: from them are taken those rulings which we mentioned.
(His statement: in the concept of the conveyor) i.e., individuals of the concept of the conveyor, due to what preceded for you of the research in that. And what was said: it does not contradict this, which he indicated previously, that the intended meaning of the two known things is the referent, because the two mentioned known things are also concepts that convey, is nothing; because the conveyor is the concept of referents, not the concepts that are true of them, so the previous objection follows.
(His statement: and the dependence of conveying... etc.) is part of the total reasoning; so the conjunction is taken into account before it, so the cause is composed of the two matters. Otherwise, the mentioned confinement of the logician's consideration does not yield the merit of precedence of this chapter, but perhaps it might suggest no need for its mention. As for his statement: after completing the introduction, it has no entry in the reasoning, but rather it is a return to reality.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary (hashiya) on the chapter heading 'On the definition of the three significations and their rulings' from a work on logic. The commentators, al-Dasuqi and al-Attar, refine the understanding of what 'rulings' means and challenge the scope of the chapter. Al-Dasuqi first explains that the rulings consist of the necessity of the conformity signification for both inclusion and implication, but not the reverse, and that inclusion does not necessarily imply implication or its reverse. This yields three rulings. Then al-Attar expands: he states that inclusion and implication are necessary for conformity (the opposite of al-Dasuqi's first ruling, which indicates a discrepancy in interpretation). Al-Attar then criticizes the original commentator for not addressing the fact that the author's chapter includes topics on words (alfaz) and universals, which al-Attar argues are not part of the rulings of signification but rather are issues whose subject is the word itself. He insists that forcing these into the category of 'rulings of signification' is a strained interpretation. He supports his view by pointing out that if the rulings in those topics relate to signification, then describing signification as 'composite' or 'simple' would be valid, but that is tantamount to attributing properties of the signifier to the signification itself. He concludes that even if one accepts this interpretation, the entire subsequent discussion reverts to the rulings. Then the discussion shifts to the phrase 'in the concept of the conveyor' (fi mafhum al-muwassil). Here the commentator clarifies that it refers to individuals of that concept, and argues against a previous objection. Finally, he explains that the cause for the precedence of this chapter is composite: both the confinement of the logician's consideration to the concept of the conveyor and the dependence of conveying and receiving meanings on words. The mere fact of confinement does not automatically justify precedence; the dependence on words is also needed. The phrase 'after the introduction' is not part of the reasoning but a statement of fact. Throughout, the commentary serves to correct misunderstandings about the chapter's content and its justification within the science of logic.

What Is Mantiq?

Distinguishing Rulings of Signification from Topics of Words
Al-Attar critiques a conflation between rulings of signification and topics on words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings of signification (ahkam al-dalalah) and the topics of words (mabahith al-alfaz) as argued by al-Attar, and critique the forced interpretation that conflates them.

Core Idea
The rulings of signification are about the logical relations between meanings, not about the properties of words.

Al-Attar criticizes the view that the topics on words discussed later in the chapter belong to the rulings of signification. He argues that those topics have the word as their subject, not signification, and forcing them under signification is a strained interpretation. This lesson clarifies the distinction and its importance.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Attar's objection
Al-Attar notes that the commentator should have addressed the fact that the author's chapter includes topics of words (mabahith al-alfaz) and universals. He objects that these are not rulings of signification (ahkam al-dalalah) but are separate issues whose subject is the word itself.
The objection is based on the definition: rulings of signification are predicates on signification, not on words.
2
Understand the distinction between subject and predicate
Al-Attar explains: rulings of signification treat signification as the subject and assign predicates (e.g., 'conformity is necessary for inclusion'). Topics of words treat the word as the subject (e.g., 'the word is simple or composite'). Conflating them confuses the subject matter.
Example: 'The signification of conformity is such' vs. 'The word 'salah' is a single term'.
3
Evaluate the forced interpretation
Al-Attar shows that even if one tries to reinterpret topics of words as rulings of signification, it ultimately leads to attributing word-properties to signification itself, which is incoherent – e.g., saying 'signification is composite' is nonsense.
This critique demonstrates the need for careful categorization in logical analysis.
