فَصْلٌ: في الدَّلالةِ
(قَوْلُهُ : وَأَحْكَامِهَا) هو لزومُ التَّضمن والالتزام للمطابقة كما قال فيما سيأتي : وتلزمُهُمَا المطابقةُ وَلَوْ تَقديراً. وقد ذكر المصنِّفُ في الفصل مباحث الألفاظ ؛ فكان ينبغي للشارح أن يتعرّض لذلك ؛ كذا في الحاشية، ولعلَّ نسخته التي كتب عليها ؛ سقط منها لفظ : فصل بعد قوله : وتلزمها المطابقة ولو تقديراً. أمَّا نُسْخَةُ ذِكْرِ الفَصْل بعد ذلكَ ؛ فَلَا اتِّجَاهَ لِمَا ذَكَرَهُ، وما اعترض بهِ عليهِ مِنْ أنَّ المباحث المذكورة في هذا الفصل إلى مباحِثِ الكُلّيّ أحكام لِلدَّلالةِ ؛ فَمِنْ قِلَّةِ التَّدَبُّرِ، وذلكَ لأَنَّ معنى أحكام الدَّلالةِ هُوَ أنْ يثبتَ لَهَا مَحمولات يحكم بها عليها، فتكونُ هِيَ مَوضُوعاتٍ لِتلكَ المحمولات، كما يُقَالُ : دَلَالَةُ المطابقة كَذَا ، دَلَالَةُ التَّضمُّنِ كَذَا ... إلخ، وقَوْلُهُ : وتلزمهما المطابقة . . . إلخ ؛ بأنْ يُقَالَ : التَّضمُّنُ والالتزام لازمَانِ للمطابقة مثلاً إلى آخِرِ الأحكام، وأمَّا مَبَاحِثُ الألفاظ فَهِيَ مَسائلُ مَوضوعُهَا اللَّفْظُ، فَيُقَالُ مثلاً : اللَّفظُ كَذَا ، المفرد والمركَّبُ كَذَا إلى آخِرِ المباحِثِ الآتية، وتكلُّفُ إرجاع تلك المباحث لأحكام الدَّلالةِ بتأويل أنَّ الدَّلالة المطابقيَّة؛ دالها يكونُ مُركَّباً تارةً بِأَقسامِهِ، ومُفرداً أُخرَى بأقسامِهِ إلى آخِرِ مَا ذكر في الفصل ؛ لا داعي لهُ معَ رجوعِهِ آخِرَ الأمر إلى عروض تلك الأحكامِ لِنَفْسِ الدَّال ؛ حيث قالَ : دَالُّها يكونُ كَذَا ... إلخ، وكأَنَّه ذهول عن قولهم : الإخبارُ بعدَ العِلْمِ بِهَا أوصافٌ ، فإِنَّهُ إذا كانَتِ الأحكام المذكورة في تِلْكَ المباحِثِ رَاجِعَةٌ لِلدَّلالةِ؛ صحَ وَصْفُ الدَّلالةِ بتلك الأحكام، ولا يسوغ لعاقل فضلا عَن فاضل أنْ يقول : الدَّلالةُ ،مُركَّبَةٌ، الدَّلالةُ مُفردةٌ، الدَّلالة حقيقةٌ، الدَّلالة مجاز . . . إلخ، المباحث الآتية على أنَّنا لو ارتكبنا هذا التَّأويلَ وصَحَّحنَاهُ؛ رَجَعَتْ أحكام الألفاظ كلّها للدَّلالةِ فَتَأَمَّلْ، لا يُقَالُ: إِنَّ المذكور في هذا الفصل وما بعده تعاريف؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : يؤخذُ مِنْها تلكَ الأحكامُ الَّتي ذكرناها .
(قَوْلُهُ : فِي مَنْهُوْمِ المُوصل ) أي : أفراد مفهوم الموصل؛ لِمَا تقدَّمَ لكَ مِنَ البحث في ذلك، ومَا قِيْلَ: إنَّه لا يُنافي ؛ هذا ما أشار إليهِ سَابِقاً مِنْ أَنَّ المرادَ مِنَ المعلومين الماصدقُ ؛ لأنَّ المعلومين المذكورينَ هِيَ مَفاهيم أيضاً موصلةٌ ليسَ بشيءٍ؛ لأنَّ الموصل مفهوم الماصدقات؛ لا نَفْسُ المفاهيم التي تصدق عليها، فيلزمُ المحذورُ السَّابق .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَتَوَقُفِ إِفَادَةِ ... إلخ) مِن مُجملةِ التَّعليل ؛ فَالعَطْفُ مُلاحَظٌ قَبلَهُ، فالعِلَّهُ مُركَّبَةٌ مِنَ الأمرين، وإلا ؛ فانحصار نظرِ المنطقي المذكور لا ينتج استحقاق تقدم هذا الفصل، بلْ رُبَّما يوهم عدم الاحتياج لِذِكْرِهِ، وأَمَّا قَوْلُهُ : بعد الفراغِ مِنَ المقدّمة ؛ فَلَا مدخلَ لَهُ في التَّعليل، وإنَّما هو رجوع للواقع.
Chapter: On the definition of the three significations and their rulings
It is worthy of being presented after completing the introduction.
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: and their rulings) They are the necessity of conformity to inclusion and implication without reverse, and the non-necessity of inclusion for implication and its reverse, so the rulings are three.
(His statement: and it) i.e., this chapter (worthy).
Al-Attar: Chapter: On signification
(His statement: and their rulings) It is the necessity of inclusion and implication for conformity, as he will say later: conformity is necessary for them even if hypothetically. The author mentioned in the chapter the topics of words; so the commentator should have addressed that. Thus in the gloss, perhaps his copy from which he wrote had missing the word 'chapter' after his statement: conformity is necessary for them even if hypothetically. As for the copy that mentions the chapter after that, there is no basis for what he said. And what he objected to, that the topics mentioned in this chapter up to the topics of universals are rulings of signification, is from lack of deliberation. Because the meaning of rulings of signification is that predicates are affirmed of them and judgments are made about them, so they become subjects for those predicates, as is said: conformity signification is such, inclusion signification is such, etc. And his statement: conformity is necessary for them... etc.; by saying: inclusion and implication are necessary for conformity, for example, up to the end of the rulings. As for the topics of words, they are issues whose subject is the word, so it is said, for example: the word is such, the simple and composite are such, up to the end of the subsequent topics. And the effort to refer those topics to rulings of signification by interpreting that the signification of conformity, its signifier is sometimes composite with its divisions, and sometimes simple with its divisions, up to the end of what is mentioned in the chapter, is unnecessary, since ultimately it returns to the attribution of those rulings to the signifier itself, where he said: its signifier is such... etc. It is as if it is a negligence of their statement: informing after knowledge of them are attributes. For if the rulings mentioned in those topics relate to signification, then describing signification with those rulings is valid, and no rational person, let alone a virtuous one, may say: signification is composite, signification is simple, signification is literal, signification is metaphorical... etc., in the subsequent topics. But if we undertake this interpretation and correct it, then all the rulings of words return to signification, so reflect. It cannot be said that what is mentioned in this chapter and after it are definitions, because we say: from them are taken those rulings which we mentioned.
(His statement: in the concept of the conveyor) i.e., individuals of the concept of the conveyor, due to what preceded for you of the research in that. And what was said: it does not contradict this, which he indicated previously, that the intended meaning of the two known things is the referent, because the two mentioned known things are also concepts that convey, is nothing; because the conveyor is the concept of referents, not the concepts that are true of them, so the previous objection follows.
(His statement: and the dependence of conveying... etc.) is part of the total reasoning; so the conjunction is taken into account before it, so the cause is composed of the two matters. Otherwise, the mentioned confinement of the logician's consideration does not yield the merit of precedence of this chapter, but perhaps it might suggest no need for its mention. As for his statement: after completing the introduction, it has no entry in the reasoning, but rather it is a return to reality.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary (hashiya) on the chapter heading 'On the definition of the three significations and their rulings' from a work on logic. The commentators, al-Dasuqi and al-Attar, refine the understanding of what 'rulings' means and challenge the scope of the chapter. Al-Dasuqi first explains that the rulings consist of the necessity of the conformity signification for both inclusion and implication, but not the reverse, and that inclusion does not necessarily imply implication or its reverse. This yields three rulings. Then al-Attar expands: he states that inclusion and implication are necessary for conformity (the opposite of al-Dasuqi's first ruling, which indicates a discrepancy in interpretation). Al-Attar then criticizes the original commentator for not addressing the fact that the author's chapter includes topics on words (alfaz) and universals, which al-Attar argues are not part of the rulings of signification but rather are issues whose subject is the word itself. He insists that forcing these into the category of 'rulings of signification' is a strained interpretation. He supports his view by pointing out that if the rulings in those topics relate to signification, then describing signification as 'composite' or 'simple' would be valid, but that is tantamount to attributing properties of the signifier to the signification itself. He concludes that even if one accepts this interpretation, the entire subsequent discussion reverts to the rulings. Then the discussion shifts to the phrase 'in the concept of the conveyor' (fi mafhum al-muwassil). Here the commentator clarifies that it refers to individuals of that concept, and argues against a previous objection. Finally, he explains that the cause for the precedence of this chapter is composite: both the confinement of the logician's consideration to the concept of the conveyor and the dependence of conveying and receiving meanings on words. The mere fact of confinement does not automatically justify precedence; the dependence on words is also needed. The phrase 'after the introduction' is not part of the reasoning but a statement of fact. Throughout, the commentary serves to correct misunderstandings about the chapter's content and its justification within the science of logic.

مباحث الألفاظ (mabahith al-alfaz)

Checkpoint
According to al-Attar, what is the subject (mawdu‘) of the topics of words (mabahith al-alfaz)?
A) The signification (dalalah)
B) The word (lafz)
C) The concept (mafhum)
D) The meaning (ma‘na)

Key Takeaway
Al-Attar's critique establishes a clear divide between rulings of signification (which are about relations between meanings) and topics of words (which are about the word itself), preventing category errors in logical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each statement below belongs to the rulings of signification or topics of words: (a) 'The word 'riba' is a verbal noun.' (b) 'The signification of 'riba' includes any excess in exchange.' Explain your reasoning based on al-Attar's criteria.
?
Given the forced interpretation al-Attar criticizes, imagine a student says 'The signification is composite.' Explain why this is nonsensical according to al-Attar's argument.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
5 min
The Three Indications: Conventional, Rational, and Natural
Classification of the three types of indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any sign (word or non-word) into conventional, rational, or natural indication and provide an original example from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Every sign conveys meaning through convention, reason, or nature.

This lesson unpacks the foundational classification of indication. Al-Dasuqi and al-Attar explain that every indicator—whether a word or a gesture—can be classified into three types. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing how texts and signs convey rulings in fiqh, kalam, and other disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the indicator
Determine whether the sign is a word (e.g., 'salat') or a non-word (e.g., a nod). Write it down.
2
Assess the basis of indication
Ask: Is the connection between the sign and its meaning (a) created by human convention, (b) known through rational necessity, or (c) compelled by nature?
Conventional: e.g., 'man' for male human. Rational: e.g., a word implies a speaker. Natural: e.g., 'ah' for pain.
3
Apply the classification
Assign the indicator to one of the three types. For each type, give a concrete example from Islamic sciences (e.g., conventional: the word 'hajj' for pilgrimage; rational: the sound of a call to prayer indicates a mu'adhdhin; natural: a cry of distress indicates urgency).
4
Verify with the text
Check your examples against those given by al-Dasuqi: conventional word = 'rajul' for male; rational word = word indicates utterer; natural word = 'akh' for pain; non-word conventional = nodding for 'yes'.

Source Text

فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها. وكَوْنِ الألفاظ منظوراً فيها.
الدسوقي: المعطوفين والمعطوف عليه لا ينتجُ المدَّعى كما يظهرُ بالتَّأمل، وحينئذ؛ فالمعنى على المعيَّة؛ أي: للانحصار المذكور معَ التَّوقُفِ والكونية المذكورتين. والمراد بالمعاني: الصُّورُ الذهنيَّةُ؛ سواء كانَتْ مَفاهيمَ مُوصلةً أَمْ لَا، فَالمنطقي مثلاً إذا أرادَ أَنْ يُعلِّمَ غيرَهُ مَجهولاً تصوريًا أو تصديقيا بالقول الشارح أو بالقياس؛ فَلَا بُدَّ لَهُ في التَّعليم مِنَ الألفاظ؛ لأجل أنْ يمكنه التَّعليم، وإنَّما قالَ: وتوقُفُ إفادة المعاني ... إلخ، ولم يقل: وتوقُفُ فَهُم المعاني وتحصيلها على الألفاظ؛ لأنَّ الشَّخصَ إذا أرادَ تحصيلها في نَفْسِهِ؛ لا يتوقَّفُ تحصيلها على الألفاظ، فالمنطقي إذا أراد أن يحصل لِنَفْسِهِ أحد المجهولين بأحدِ الطَّريقين؛ لم تكن الألفاظ في في هذا التحصيل أمراً ضروريًا؛ إذ يمكنُهُ تعقَّلُ المعاني مُجرَّدةً عن الألفاظ، لكنَّهُ عسير جدًّا، وذلكَ لأنَّ النَّفْسَ تَعوَّدَتْ مُلاحظة المعاني مِنَ الألفاظ، بحيث إذا أرادت أن تَتعقَّلَ المعاني وتُلاحظها؛ تتخيَّلُ الألفاظ وتنتقل منها للمعاني، وَلَو أرادَتْ أنْ تتعقَّلَ المعاني خالصةً مِنَ الألفاظ المخيَّلة والمحققة؛ صعب عليها صعوبة تامة، كما يشهدُ بِهِ الرَّجوعُ لِلوجدان.
(قَوْلُهُ: عَلَى الْأَلْفَاظِ) أي: فَاحتيج لمبحث الألفاظ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَكَوْنِ الْأَلْفَاظِ) أي: المتوفّقُ عليها إفادة المعاني واستفادتُها؛ منظوراً فيها من حيثُ إِنَّها دلائل المعاني؛ أي: لَا مِن حيثُ إِنَّهَا مُفردةٌ أَو مُركَّبة، وَلَا مِن حيثُ إِنَّها عَرَضُ، وَلَا مِن حيثُ إنَّها موجودةٌ خارجاً أو ذهناً، وبهذه الحيثية اندفع مَا يُقالُ: إِنَّ الدَّلالةَ وصفٌ لِلألفاظ، ومرتبةً الموصوفِ مُقدَّمةٌ على مرتبة الوصف فكانَ اللائقُ ذِكْرَ مباحث اللفظ قبل مباحثِ الدَّلالةِ.
العطار: هذه المباحث مناسبة للمباحِثِ المنطقيَّة، فإنّها أُمورٌ قَانونيَّةٌ مُتناولةٌ لِجَميعِ المفهومات، وربَّما يورد على الندرة أقوال مخصوصةٌ باللُّغةِ الَّتِي دُوِّنَ بِهَا هذا الفن لزيادة الاعتناء بها ا.هـ. فَعُلِمَ مِنْهُ اختلافُ بَحْثَيْ أهل العربية والمناطقة عن أحوالِ الألفاظ، فإنَّ أهلَ العربيَّةِ يبحثون عنها على قاعدة لُغَةِ العرب، والمناطقة يبحثونَ عن أحوالِهَا الشَّاملة لجميع اللُّغَاتِ.
دلالة اللفظ: وحاصلُ الدَّفع: أنَّ اللَّفظَ منظورٌ له من حيثُ إِنَّهُ يدلُّ على المعنى، فالملتفت إليه في الحقيقةِ إنَّما هو دلالته على المعنى لا غيرها، فكان تقديمُ الدَّلالةِ هو اللائق. (قَوْلُهُ: دَلَائِلُ الْمَعَانِي) أي: أمور دالة على المعاني.
(قَوْلُهُ: فَلِذَا) أي: فَلِأجل أنَّ هذا الفصل حقيق بالتَّقدُّم لأجلِ مَا ذكرَ؛ قدَّمَ الكلام .. إلخ؛ أي: قدَّمَهُ بالفعل، فَلَا يُقالُ: إِنَّهُ كَالتَّكرار مع قوله: (وهو حقيق ... إلخ). (قَوْلُهُ: دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ) أي: الوضعية، فخرج بإضافة دلالةِ اللَّفْظِ دلالة غيرِ اللَّفْظِ بأقسامها الثلاثة، وبتقدير الوضعية؛ دلالة اللفظ العقلية والطبيعية، واعلم أنَّ الدَّالَّ؛ إمَّا لفظ أو غيره، ودلالةُ كُلِّ منهما إما وضعيَّةٌ، أو عقلية، أو طبيعية، ويُقال لها أيضاً: عاديَّة، فالمجموع ستّة فَدلالةُ اللّفظ الوضعيّةِ: كَدلالةِ لفظ رَجل على الذَّكْرِ الإنساني، ودلالته العقلية: كَدلالةِ اللَّفظِ على لافظِهِ؛ لأنَّ اللَّفظَ عَرَضٌ لا بُدَّ لَهُ عقلاً من جرم يقوم به، وهو المتلفّظ به، ودلالته الطبيعية: كدلالة «أخ» على الوجع، فإنَّ الطَّبعَ عند عروض الوجع يلجأ إلى النُّطق بذلك. وأمَّا دلالة غير اللفظ الوضعية: كدلالة الإشارة المخصوصة؛ كالإشارة بالرأس مثلاً على معنى «نعم»؛ وهو الإجابة، أو على معنى «لا»؛ وهو عدم الإجابة.
العطار: أضافها لِلَّفظ لَمَّا أَنَّ التَّقسيم الآتي إنَّما يجري فيها دونَ غيرها من بقيَّةِ الدَّلالات، ولم يقيَّد بالوضعيّة لأنَّ الوضعَ أخذَ فَصْلاً فيها، ومَا قِيلَ لو أرادَ اشتمالَ التَّعريفات على الجنس القريب اعتبر قيد الوضعية ليس بشيء على تقديرِ أخذه في التَّعريف لا يكونُ جنساً، بل هو فصل؛ لأنَّهُ لا شمول فيه، كَما هو قاعدة الجنس.
A chapter: On the definition of the three indications and their rulings. And that words are considered in them. al-Dasuqi: The conjoined and the conjoined-to do not produce the claim as appears by reflection; then the meaning is on concomitance: i.e., for the aforementioned confinement with the aforementioned dependence and state. What is meant by 'meanings' are mental forms, whether they are connected concepts or not. For the logician, for example, when he wants to teach another an unknown conceptual or assenting [matter] by an explanatory statement or by syllogism, he necessarily needs words in teaching in order that teaching be possible. He only said: 'And the dependence of conveying meanings...' and did not say: 'And the dependence of understanding meanings and acquiring them on words' because a person when he wants to acquire them for himself, their acquisition does not depend on words. The logician when he wants to acquire for himself one of the two unknowns by one of the two methods, words are not necessary in this acquisition; for he can conceive meanings abstracted from words, but it is very difficult, because the soul has become accustomed to observing meanings from words such that when it wants to conceive meanings and observe them, it imagines words and transfers from them to the meanings. And if it wanted to conceive meanings purely, free from imagined and realized words, it would be extremely difficult, as attested by recourse to introspection. His statement: 'on the words' means: so there is a need for the topic of words. His statement: 'And that words' means: that on which the conveying and acquisition of meanings depend are considered insofar as they are indicators of meanings; i.e., not insofar as they are single or compound, nor insofar as they are accidents, nor insofar as they exist externally or mentally. By this respect, what is said is rebutted: 'Indication is a description of words, and the rank of the described precedes the rank of the description, so it would be fitting to mention the topics of words before the topics of indication.' al-Attar: These topics are appropriate for logical topics, for they are universal rules covering all concepts; and occasionally there occur exceptional statements specific to the language in which this art was recorded for added care. So it is known from this the difference between the inquiries of Arabic scholars and logicians concerning the states of words: Arabic scholars investigate them on the basis of the Arabic language, while logicians investigate their states that are common to all languages. Indication of the word: The gist of the rebuttal is that the word is considered insofar as it indicates the meaning, so what is actually attended to is its indication of the meaning, nothing else. Thus, presenting indication first is fitting. His statement: 'indicators of meanings' means: things that indicate meanings. His statement: 'therefore' means: because this chapter deserves precedence for what was mentioned, he presented the discussion... i.e., he presented it actually, so it cannot be said that it is repetitive with his statement: 'and it deserves... etc.' His statement: 'indication of the word' means: the conventional; so by adding 'indication' to 'the word,' the indication of other than the word with its three divisions is excluded; and by assuming conventionality, the rational and natural indication of the word are excluded. Know that the indicator is either a word or other, and the indication of each is either conventional, rational, or natural; also called customary. So the total is six: the conventional indication of the word: like the indication of the word 'man' upon the human male; its rational indication: like the indication of the word upon its utterer, because the word is an accident that necessarily requires a body to subsist in, which is the utterer; its natural indication: like the indication of 'ah' upon pain, for nature when pain occurs resorts to uttering that. As for the conventional indication of other than a word: like the indication of a specific sign, such as nodding the head for the meaning of 'yes'—which is affirmation—or for the meaning of 'no'—which is negation. al-Attar: He added it to the word because the upcoming division only applies to it and not to the rest of the indications, and he did not restrict to the conventional because the conventional is taken as a differentia in it. And what is said: 'if he intended the definitions to include the proximate genus, then the restriction of conventionality would be considered' is nothing; upon the assumption of taking it in the definition it would not be a genus, but a differentia, because it has no inclusiveness, as is the rule of the genus.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of indication and addresses why indication is discussed before words in logic. The author begins by stating that the chapter is on the definition of the three indications and their rulings, noting that words are considered in them. Al-Dasuqi explains that what is meant by 'meanings' are mental forms, whether they are connected concepts or not. He clarifies that a logician needs words when teaching another person, but not when acquiring knowledge for oneself, though it is difficult to conceive meanings without words due to habit. The passage then addresses an objection: since indication is a property of words, words should be discussed first. The rebuttal is that words are considered only insofar as they indicate meanings, so indication is primary. Al-Attar distinguishes the approach of Arabic scholars (focused on Arabic language) from logicians (focused on universal aspects). Finally, a detailed classification of indications is given: the indicator is either a word or other, and each has three types: conventional, rational, and natural, totaling six. Examples are provided: conventional indication of a word (e.g., 'man' for human male), rational indication (e.g., that the word implies an utterer), natural indication (e.g., 'ah' for pain). Non-word conventional indication includes nodding for 'yes' or 'no'. Al-Attar notes that the term 'indication of the word' is used without restricting to conventional because the conventional is the differentia in the definition, not the genus.

Indication is divided into conventional, rational, and natural, each applying to both words and non-words, forming a total of six types.

What Is Mantiq?

Why Indication Precedes Words in Logic
Defense of the order of topics in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the logical reason why the topic of indication precedes the topic of words in logic textbooks and defend this order against the objection that description should follow the described.

Core Idea
In logic, words are studied only as indicators of meanings, so indication is the true focus.

A careful reader might object: if indication is a property of words, shouldn't words be discussed first? Al-Dasuqi and al-Attar anticipate this and provide a robust rebuttal. This lesson unpacks the dialectic, showing that the logician's interest lies solely in words as conveyors of meaning, thus establishing the proper order of topics.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Identify the objection as it appears in the text: 'Indication is a description of words, and the rank of the described precedes the rank of the description, so it would be fitting to mention the topics of words before the topics of indication.'
2
Extract the rebuttal
Find the gist: 'the word is considered insofar as it indicates the meaning, so what is actually attended to is its indication of the meaning, nothing else. Thus, presenting indication first is fitting.'
3
Identify the presupposition
Notice that the logician's perspective is universal: words are studied not in their linguistic particularity but as indicators of meaning across all languages.
Al-Attar contrasts this with Arabic grammarians, who study words for their specific language rules.
4
Summarize the resolution
The objection fails because it assumes words have independent significance, whereas in logic they are only relevant as bearers of indication. Therefore indication logically precedes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها. وكَوْنِ الألفاظ منظوراً فيها.
الدسوقي: المعطوفين والمعطوف عليه لا ينتجُ المدَّعى كما يظهرُ بالتَّأمل، وحينئذ؛ فالمعنى على المعيَّة؛ أي: للانحصار المذكور معَ التَّوقُفِ والكونية المذكورتين. والمراد بالمعاني: الصُّورُ الذهنيَّةُ؛ سواء كانَتْ مَفاهيمَ مُوصلةً أَمْ لَا، فَالمنطقي مثلاً إذا أرادَ أَنْ يُعلِّمَ غيرَهُ مَجهولاً تصوريًا أو تصديقيا بالقول الشارح أو بالقياس؛ فَلَا بُدَّ لَهُ في التَّعليم مِنَ الألفاظ؛ لأجل أنْ يمكنه التَّعليم، وإنَّما قالَ: وتوقُفُ إفادة المعاني ... إلخ، ولم يقل: وتوقُفُ فَهُم المعاني وتحصيلها على الألفاظ؛ لأنَّ الشَّخصَ إذا أرادَ تحصيلها في نَفْسِهِ؛ لا يتوقَّفُ تحصيلها على الألفاظ، فالمنطقي إذا أراد أن يحصل لِنَفْسِهِ أحد المجهولين بأحدِ الطَّريقين؛ لم تكن الألفاظ في في هذا التحصيل أمراً ضروريًا؛ إذ يمكنُهُ تعقَّلُ المعاني مُجرَّدةً عن الألفاظ، لكنَّهُ عسير جدًّا، وذلكَ لأنَّ النَّفْسَ تَعوَّدَتْ مُلاحظة المعاني مِنَ الألفاظ، بحيث إذا أرادت أن تَتعقَّلَ المعاني وتُلاحظها؛ تتخيَّلُ الألفاظ وتنتقل منها للمعاني، وَلَو أرادَتْ أنْ تتعقَّلَ المعاني خالصةً مِنَ الألفاظ المخيَّلة والمحققة؛ صعب عليها صعوبة تامة، كما يشهدُ بِهِ الرَّجوعُ لِلوجدان.
(قَوْلُهُ: عَلَى الْأَلْفَاظِ) أي: فَاحتيج لمبحث الألفاظ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَكَوْنِ الْأَلْفَاظِ) أي: المتوفّقُ عليها إفادة المعاني واستفادتُها؛ منظوراً فيها من حيثُ إِنَّها دلائل المعاني؛ أي: لَا مِن حيثُ إِنَّهَا مُفردةٌ أَو مُركَّبة، وَلَا مِن حيثُ إِنَّها عَرَضُ، وَلَا مِن حيثُ إنَّها موجودةٌ خارجاً أو ذهناً، وبهذه الحيثية اندفع مَا يُقالُ: إِنَّ الدَّلالةَ وصفٌ لِلألفاظ، ومرتبةً الموصوفِ مُقدَّمةٌ على مرتبة الوصف فكانَ اللائقُ ذِكْرَ مباحث اللفظ قبل مباحثِ الدَّلالةِ.
العطار: هذه المباحث مناسبة للمباحِثِ المنطقيَّة، فإنّها أُمورٌ قَانونيَّةٌ مُتناولةٌ لِجَميعِ المفهومات، وربَّما يورد على الندرة أقوال مخصوصةٌ باللُّغةِ الَّتِي دُوِّنَ بِهَا هذا الفن لزيادة الاعتناء بها ا.هـ. فَعُلِمَ مِنْهُ اختلافُ بَحْثَيْ أهل العربية والمناطقة عن أحوالِ الألفاظ، فإنَّ أهلَ العربيَّةِ يبحثون عنها على قاعدة لُغَةِ العرب، والمناطقة يبحثونَ عن أحوالِهَا الشَّاملة لجميع اللُّغَاتِ.
دلالة اللفظ: وحاصلُ الدَّفع: أنَّ اللَّفظَ منظورٌ له من حيثُ إِنَّهُ يدلُّ على المعنى، فالملتفت إليه في الحقيقةِ إنَّما هو دلالته على المعنى لا غيرها، فكان تقديمُ الدَّلالةِ هو اللائق. (قَوْلُهُ: دَلَائِلُ الْمَعَانِي) أي: أمور دالة على المعاني.
(قَوْلُهُ: فَلِذَا) أي: فَلِأجل أنَّ هذا الفصل حقيق بالتَّقدُّم لأجلِ مَا ذكرَ؛ قدَّمَ الكلام .. إلخ؛ أي: قدَّمَهُ بالفعل، فَلَا يُقالُ: إِنَّهُ كَالتَّكرار مع قوله: (وهو حقيق ... إلخ). (قَوْلُهُ: دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ) أي: الوضعية، فخرج بإضافة دلالةِ اللَّفْظِ دلالة غيرِ اللَّفْظِ بأقسامها الثلاثة، وبتقدير الوضعية؛ دلالة اللفظ العقلية والطبيعية، واعلم أنَّ الدَّالَّ؛ إمَّا لفظ أو غيره، ودلالةُ كُلِّ منهما إما وضعيَّةٌ، أو عقلية، أو طبيعية، ويُقال لها أيضاً: عاديَّة، فالمجموع ستّة فَدلالةُ اللّفظ الوضعيّةِ: كَدلالةِ لفظ رَجل على الذَّكْرِ الإنساني، ودلالته العقلية: كَدلالةِ اللَّفظِ على لافظِهِ؛ لأنَّ اللَّفظَ عَرَضٌ لا بُدَّ لَهُ عقلاً من جرم يقوم به، وهو المتلفّظ به، ودلالته الطبيعية: كدلالة «أخ» على الوجع، فإنَّ الطَّبعَ عند عروض الوجع يلجأ إلى النُّطق بذلك. وأمَّا دلالة غير اللفظ الوضعية: كدلالة الإشارة المخصوصة؛ كالإشارة بالرأس مثلاً على معنى «نعم»؛ وهو الإجابة، أو على معنى «لا»؛ وهو عدم الإجابة.
العطار: أضافها لِلَّفظ لَمَّا أَنَّ التَّقسيم الآتي إنَّما يجري فيها دونَ غيرها من بقيَّةِ الدَّلالات، ولم يقيَّد بالوضعيّة لأنَّ الوضعَ أخذَ فَصْلاً فيها، ومَا قِيلَ لو أرادَ اشتمالَ التَّعريفات على الجنس القريب اعتبر قيد الوضعية ليس بشيء على تقديرِ أخذه في التَّعريف لا يكونُ جنساً، بل هو فصل؛ لأنَّهُ لا شمول فيه، كَما هو قاعدة الجنس.
A chapter: On the definition of the three indications and their rulings. And that words are considered in them. al-Dasuqi: The conjoined and the conjoined-to do not produce the claim as appears by reflection; then the meaning is on concomitance: i.e., for the aforementioned confinement with the aforementioned dependence and state. What is meant by 'meanings' are mental forms, whether they are connected concepts or not. For the logician, for example, when he wants to teach another an unknown conceptual or assenting [matter] by an explanatory statement or by syllogism, he necessarily needs words in teaching in order that teaching be possible. He only said: 'And the dependence of conveying meanings...' and did not say: 'And the dependence of understanding meanings and acquiring them on words' because a person when he wants to acquire them for himself, their acquisition does not depend on words. The logician when he wants to acquire for himself one of the two unknowns by one of the two methods, words are not necessary in this acquisition; for he can conceive meanings abstracted from words, but it is very difficult, because the soul has become accustomed to observing meanings from words such that when it wants to conceive meanings and observe them, it imagines words and transfers from them to the meanings. And if it wanted to conceive meanings purely, free from imagined and realized words, it would be extremely difficult, as attested by recourse to introspection. His statement: 'on the words' means: so there is a need for the topic of words. His statement: 'And that words' means: that on which the conveying and acquisition of meanings depend are considered insofar as they are indicators of meanings; i.e., not insofar as they are single or compound, nor insofar as they are accidents, nor insofar as they exist externally or mentally. By this respect, what is said is rebutted: 'Indication is a description of words, and the rank of the described precedes the rank of the description, so it would be fitting to mention the topics of words before the topics of indication.' al-Attar: These topics are appropriate for logical topics, for they are universal rules covering all concepts; and occasionally there occur exceptional statements specific to the language in which this art was recorded for added care. So it is known from this the difference between the inquiries of Arabic scholars and logicians concerning the states of words: Arabic scholars investigate them on the basis of the Arabic language, while logicians investigate their states that are common to all languages. Indication of the word: The gist of the rebuttal is that the word is considered insofar as it indicates the meaning, so what is actually attended to is its indication of the meaning, nothing else. Thus, presenting indication first is fitting. His statement: 'indicators of meanings' means: things that indicate meanings. His statement: 'therefore' means: because this chapter deserves precedence for what was mentioned, he presented the discussion... i.e., he presented it actually, so it cannot be said that it is repetitive with his statement: 'and it deserves... etc.' His statement: 'indication of the word' means: the conventional; so by adding 'indication' to 'the word,' the indication of other than the word with its three divisions is excluded; and by assuming conventionality, the rational and natural indication of the word are excluded. Know that the indicator is either a word or other, and the indication of each is either conventional, rational, or natural; also called customary. So the total is six: the conventional indication of the word: like the indication of the word 'man' upon the human male; its rational indication: like the indication of the word upon its utterer, because the word is an accident that necessarily requires a body to subsist in, which is the utterer; its natural indication: like the indication of 'ah' upon pain, for nature when pain occurs resorts to uttering that. As for the conventional indication of other than a word: like the indication of a specific sign, such as nodding the head for the meaning of 'yes'—which is affirmation—or for the meaning of 'no'—which is negation. al-Attar: He added it to the word because the upcoming division only applies to it and not to the rest of the indications, and he did not restrict to the conventional because the conventional is taken as a differentia in it. And what is said: 'if he intended the definitions to include the proximate genus, then the restriction of conventionality would be considered' is nothing; upon the assumption of taking it in the definition it would not be a genus, but a differentia, because it has no inclusiveness, as is the rule of the genus.

Al-Dasuqi explains that what is meant by 'meanings' are mental forms, whether they are connected concepts or not. The Three Types of Signification: Rational, Natural, and Conventional
Author defines the three types of verbal conventional signification and explains why logicians limit consideration to them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rational, natural, and conventional signification and give an example of each from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Signification is either by reason, nature, or convention—only the last is reliable for logical analysis.

In this passage, the author introduces the three fundamental types of signification that form the basis of logical inquiry. While all three exist in human communication, logicians prioritize conventional signification for its regularity and ease of use. Understanding the distinction helps you see why definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences rely on linguistic convention rather than natural reactions or intellectual inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Look for the three categories: rational ('aqliyya), natural (tabi'iyya), and conventional (wad'iyya). Note the examples given.
2
Examine the rational example
The author uses the concomitance of accidental attributes with a body to indicate the body's temporality. Understand the logical necessity here: the mind cannot conceive of an eternal concomitant of an originated thing.
This is a logical, not a linguistic, connection.
3
Examine the natural example
Yellowing of the face indicates fear, redness indicates shyness. This is a natural, not a logical, connection.
Natural reactions can vary between individuals.
4
Understand the conventional
The passage then focuses on conventional signification, which is based on assignment (wad'). This is what logicians use.
5
See the classification logic
The author presents a division: if convention is involved, it's conventional; if not, then if changeable it's natural, if unchangeable it's rational. This yields six total categories but only verbal conventional is considered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريفِ الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها
وَالْعَقْلِيَّةُ : كَدَلالَةِ مُلازَمَةِ الأَعْرَاضِ الحَادِثَةِ لِلْجِرْمِ عَلَى حُدُوثِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ العَقْلَ يُحِيلُ قِدَمَ مُلازَمِ الحَادِثِ.
وَالطَّبِيعِيَّةُ : كَدَلالَةِ صُفْرَةِ الوَجْهِ عَلَى الوَجَلِ؛ أَيِ: الخَوْفِ، وَدَلالَةِ حُمْرَتِهِ عَلَى الخَجَلِ؛ أَيِ: الحَيَاءِ، فَإِنَّ مِنْ طَبْعِ الشَّخْصِ أَنْ تَحْدُثَ لَهُ صُفْرَةٌ فِي وَجْهِهِ عِنْدَ الوَجَلِ، وَحُمْرَةٌ فِي وَجْهِهِ عِنْدَ الخَجَلِ.
وَوَجْهُ انْقِسَامِ الدَّلَالَةِ لِمَا ذُكِرَ : أَنَّ الدَّلَالَةَ؛ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ لِلْوَضْعِ مَدْخَلٌ فِيهَا أَوْ لَا، فَإِنْ كَانَ لَهُ مَدْخَلٌ فِيهَا؛ فَهِيَ الوَضْعِيَّةُ فِي اللَّفْظِ وَغَيْرِهِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لِلْوَضْعِ مَدْخَلٌ فِيهَا؛ فَإِنْ أَمْكَنَ تَغَيُّرُهَا فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ؛ فَهِيَ الطَّبِيعِيَّةُ فِي اللَّفْظِ وَغَيْرِهِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُمْكِنْ تَغَيُّرُهَا؛ فَهِيَ العَقْلِيَّةُ فِي اللَّفْظِ وَغَيْرِهِ، فَهَذِهِ سِتَّةُ أَقْسَامٍ، وَالمُعْتَبَرُ مِنْهَا عِنْدَ المُنَاطِقَةِ قِسْمٌ وَاحِدٌ؛ وَهُوَ الدَّلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِيَّةُ الوَضْعِيَّةُ، فَقَسَّمُوهَا ثَلَاثَةَ أَقْسَامٍ: مُطَابِقِيَّةٌ، وَتَضَمُّنِيَّةٌ، وَالْتِزَامِيَّةٌ.
وَإِنَّمَا اعْتَبَرُوهَا دُونَ غَيْرِهَا؛ لِعُمُومِهَا وَانْضِبَاطِهَا وَسُهُولَةِ تَنَاوُلِهَا، بِخِلَافِ الطَّبِيعِيَّةِ؛ فَإِنَّهَا مَخْصُوصَةٌ بِبَعْضِ الأُمُورِ مَعَ عَدَمِ الوُثُوقِ بِانْضِبَاطِهَا؛ لِإِمْكَانِ اخْتِلَافِ الطَّبَائِعِ، وَتَنَاوُلُهَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى البَحْثِ عَنْ مُقْتَضَى الطَّبْعِ، وَقَدْ يَصْعُبُ، وَكَذَا العَقْلِيَّةُ؛ فَإِنَّهَا تَخْتَصُّ بِمَا بَيْنَهُمَا لُزُومٌ عَقْلِيٌّ، وَالعُقُولُ تَتَنَاقَضُ وَلَا تَنْضَبِطُ أَفْهَامُهَا بِاعْتِبَارِ الفَاهِمِينَ، وَهِيَ مُتَوَقِّفَةٌ عَلَى إِدْرَاكِ اللُّزُومِ، وَقَدْ يَكُونُ صَعْبَ التَّنَاوُلِ، بِخِلَافِ اللَّفْظِيَّةِ الوَضْعِيَّةِ؛ فَإِنَّهَا إِنَّمَا تَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الِاطِّلَاعِ عَلَى الوَضْعِ، وَهُوَ سَهْلٌ، فَكُلَّمَا عُرِفَ الوَضْعُ؛ انْضَبَطَ فِي أَفْرَادِ المَوْضُوعِ لَهُ.
Chapter: On defining the three types of signification and their rulings. The rational: like the signification of the concomitance of accidental attributes originating from the body for its origination; because the rational mind deems impossible the pre-eternity of the concomitant of the originated. The natural: like the signification of the yellowness of the face for fear, and the signification of its redness for shyness; for it is from the nature of a person that yellowness occurs on his face at the time of fear, and redness on his face at the time of shyness. The reason for the division of signification into what was mentioned: that signification either has a role for convention (wad') or not. If convention has a role in it, then it is conventional in speech and in other things. If convention has no role in it, then if it can change in itself, it is natural in speech and other things; if it cannot change, then it is rational in speech and other things. So these are six divisions. What is considered among them by the logicians is one category: verbal conventional signification. So they divided it into three categories: corresponding (mutābiqīyya), implicative (taḍammunīyya), and commitmental (iltizāmīyya). They considered it to the exclusion of others because of its generality, regularity, and ease of handling, unlike the natural, which is specific to some matters with a lack of confidence in its regularity because of the possibility of differences in natures, and its handling depends on investigation into what nature requires, which may be difficult. Likewise the rational, for it is specific to what has rational necessity between them, and intellects disagree and their understandings are not regulated based on the understanding of the ones understanding, and it depends on grasping the necessity, which may be difficult to handle, unlike the verbal conventional; for it depends only on looking into the convention, which is easy, so whenever the convention is known, it becomes regular in the individuals of what it is assigned to.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section (fasl) from a commentary on the Tadhheeb. It defines the three types of signification—rational, natural, and conventional—and then explains why logicians restrict their consideration to verbal conventional signification. The author first gives examples of rational signification: the concomitance of accidental attributes with a body indicates the body's temporal origination because the mind cannot accept that the concomitant of an originated thing is eternal. For natural signification, examples are the yellowing of the face from fear and reddening from shyness, which are natural responses. Then the author provides a logical classification: signification either involves convention or not. If convention is involved, it is conventional (whether in speech or otherwise). If not, and the signification can change, it is natural; if it cannot change, it is rational. This yields six types. Logicians consider only verbal conventional signification because it is universal, well-defined, and easy to handle. Natural signification is specific and unreliable due to variation in natures; rational signification is subject to differing intellects and difficult to grasp. Verbal conventional signification only requires knowing the convention, which is easy. This passage emphasizes the epistemological priority of conventional linguistic signification in logic.

عقلية
طبيعية
وضعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of signification is based on a changeable natural reaction?
A) Rational
B) Natural
C) Conventional
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Signification is divided into rational, natural, and conventional; only conventional is fully reliable for logical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take an example from the Qur'an: the word 'al-sirat' (the path). Explain which type of signification gives you its meaning: rational, natural, or conventional? Why?
?
In a hadith, the Prophet (saws) turned red with anger. Should that redness be considered natural or conventional signification? Explain.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
10 min
The Three Subtypes of Conventional Signification: Mutabiqa, Tadammun, Iltizam
Author defines the three types of verbal conventional signification and explains why logicians limit consideration to them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given conventional signification is corresponding, implicative, or commitmental, and give an Islamic example.

Core Idea
A conventional word can signify its whole meaning, a part of it, or something necessary along with it.

After establishing that logicians focus only on verbal conventional signification, the author divides it into three categories based on how the expression relates to its meaning. This distinction is crucial for understanding definitions, arguments, and how we derive implications from texts.

Guided Path
1
Learn the three terms
muṭābiqiyya (whole meaning), taḍammuniyya (part), iltizāmiyya (necessary concomitant).
2
Examine the given example: 'human' meaning animal rational
This is mutabiqa. 'Human' signifying 'animal' alone is tadammun. 'Human' signifying 'capable of knowledge' might be iltizam.
3
Understand the author's classification
The division is based on whether the expression signifies the entire meaning, a part of it, or something external that necessarily accompanies it.
4
Apply to an Islamic example: the word 'wine' (khamr)
The mutabiqa meaning is the intoxicating drink. Tadammun: the liquid. Iltizam: the prohibition (since prohibition necessarily accompanies wine in Islamic law).
This illustrates how iltizam is used in qiyas.
5
Note the logical importance
Iltizami signification allows us to derive rulings from the text without explicit mention, as in analogical reasoning.

فَصْلٌ: في تعريفِ الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها
وَالْعَقْلِيَّةُ : كَدَلالَةِ مُلازَمَةِ الأَعْرَاضِ الحَادِثَةِ لِلْجِرْمِ عَلَى حُدُوثِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ العَقْلَ يُحِيلُ قِدَمَ مُلازَمِ الحَادِثِ.

وَإِنَّمَا اعْتَبَرُوهَا دُونَ غَيْرِهَا؛ لِعُمُومِهَا وَانْضِبَاطِهَا وَسُهُولَةِ تَنَاوُلِهَا، بِخِلَافِ الطَّبِيعِيَّةِ؛ فَإِنَّهَا مَخْصُوصَةٌ بِبَعْضِ الأُمُورِ مَعَ عَدَمِ الوُثُوقِ بِانْضِبَاطِهَا؛ لِإِمْكَانِ اخْتِلَافِ الطَّبَائِعِ، وَتَنَاوُلُهَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى البَحْثِ عَنْ مُقْتَضَى الطَّبْعِ، وَقَدْ يَصْعُبُ، وَكَذَا العَقْلِيَّةُ؛ فَإِنَّهَا تَخْتَصُّ بِمَا بَيْنَهُمَا لُزُومٌ عَقْلِيٌّ، وَالعُقُولُ تَتَنَاقَضُ وَلَا تَنْضَبِطُ أَفْهَامُهَا بِاعْتِبَارِ الفَاهِمِينَ، وَهِيَ مُتَوَقِّفَةٌ عَلَى إِدْرَاكِ اللُّزُومِ، وَقَدْ يَكُونُ صَعْبَ التَّنَاوُلِ، بِخِلَافِ اللَّفْظِيَّةِ الوَضْعِيَّةِ؛ فَإِنَّهَا إِنَّمَا تَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الِاطِّلَاعِ عَلَى الوَضْعِ، وَهُوَ سَهْلٌ، فَكُلَّمَا عُرِفَ الوَضْعُ؛ انْضَبَطَ فِي أَفْرَادِ المَوْضُوعِ لَهُ.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
7 min
Why Logicians Limit Themselves to Verbal Conventional Signification
Author defines the three types of verbal conventional signification and explains why logicians limit consideration to them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument for excluding natural and rational signification from logical consideration.

Core Idea
Natural and rational signification are unreliable and difficult, while verbal conventional signification is universal, regular, and easy.

The passage not only defines types of signification but also provides a justification for focusing on one. This epistemological argument is crucial for understanding the scope of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Logicians consider only verbal conventional signification.
2
Find the reasons
The author gives three advantages: generality ('umum), regularity (indibat), ease of handling (suhulat al-tanawul).
3
See the contrast with natural
Natural signification is specific to certain matters, unreliable because natures differ, and requires investigation.
4
See the contrast with rational
Rational signification depends on rational necessity, but intellects differ, and grasping the necessity is difficult.
5
Conclude
Conventional signification only requires knowledge of the convention, which is easy to acquire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريفِ الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها
وَالْعَقْلِيَّةُ : كَدَلالَةِ مُلازَمَةِ الأَعْرَاضِ الحَادِثَةِ لِلْجِرْمِ عَلَى حُدُوثِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ العَقْلَ يُحِيلُ قِدَمَ مُلازَمِ الحَادِثِ.
وَالطَّبِيعِيَّةُ : كَدَلالَةِ صُفْرَةِ الوَجْهِ عَلَى الوَجَلِ؛ أَيِ: الخَوْفِ، وَدَلالَةِ حُمْرَتِهِ عَلَى الخَجَلِ؛ أَيِ: الحَيَاءِ، فَإِنَّ مِنْ طَبْعِ الشَّخْصِ أَنْ تَحْدُثَ لَهُ صُفْرَةٌ فِي وَجْهِهِ عِنْدَ الوَجَلِ، وَحُمْرَةٌ فِي وَجْهِهِ عِنْدَ الخَجَلِ.
وَوَجْهُ انْقِسَامِ الدَّلَالَةِ لِمَا ذُكِرَ : أَنَّ الدَّلَالَةَ؛ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ لِلْوَضْعِ مَدْخَلٌ فِيهَا أَوْ لَا، فَإِنْ كَانَ لَهُ مَدْخَلٌ فِيهَا؛ فَهِيَ الوَضْعِيَّةُ فِي اللَّفْظِ وَغَيْرِهِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لِلْوَضْعِ مَدْخَلٌ فِيهَا؛ فَإِنْ أَمْكَنَ تَغَيُّرُهَا فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ؛ فَهِيَ الطَّبِيعِيَّةُ فِي اللَّفْظِ وَغَيْرِهِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُمْكِنْ تَغَيُّرُهَا؛ فَهِيَ العَقْلِيَّةُ فِي اللَّفْظِ وَغَيْرِهِ، فَهَذِهِ سِتَّةُ أَقْسَامٍ، وَالمُعْتَبَرُ مِنْهَا عِنْدَ المُنَاطِقَةِ قِسْمٌ وَاحِدٌ؛ وَهُوَ الدَّلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِيَّةُ الوَضْعِيَّةُ، فَقَسَّمُوهَا ثَلَاثَةَ أَقْسَامٍ: مُطَابِقِيَّةٌ، وَتَضَمُّنِيَّةٌ، وَالْتِزَامِيَّةٌ.
وَإِنَّمَا اعْتَبَرُوهَا دُونَ غَيْرِهَا؛ لِعُمُومِهَا وَانْضِبَاطِهَا وَسُهُولَةِ تَنَاوُلِهَا، بِخِلَافِ الطَّبِيعِيَّةِ؛ فَإِنَّهَا مَخْصُوصَةٌ بِبَعْضِ الأُمُورِ مَعَ عَدَمِ الوُثُوقِ بِانْضِبَاطِهَا؛ لِإِمْكَانِ اخْتِلَافِ الطَّبَائِعِ، وَتَنَاوُلُهَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى البَحْثِ عَنْ مُقْتَضَى الطَّبْعِ، وَقَدْ يَصْعُبُ، وَكَذَا العَقْلِيَّةُ؛ فَإِنَّهَا تَخْتَصُّ بِمَا بَيْنَهُمَا لُزُومٌ عَقْلِيٌّ، وَالعُقُولُ تَتَنَاقَضُ وَلَا تَنْضَبِطُ أَفْهَامُهَا بِاعْتِبَارِ الفَاهِمِينَ، وَهِيَ مُتَوَقِّفَةٌ عَلَى إِدْرَاكِ اللُّزُومِ، وَقَدْ يَكُونُ صَعْبَ التَّنَاوُلِ، بِخِلَافِ اللَّفْظِيَّةِ الوَضْعِيَّةِ؛ فَإِنَّهَا إِنَّمَا تَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الِاطِّلَاعِ عَلَى الوَضْعِ، وَهُوَ سَهْلٌ، فَكُلَّمَا عُرِفَ الوَضْعُ؛ انْضَبَطَ فِي أَفْرَادِ المَوْضُوعِ لَهُ.
Chapter: On defining the three types of signification and their rulings. The rational: like the signification of the concomitance of accidental attributes originating from the body for its origination; because the rational mind deems impossible the pre-eternity of the concomitant of the originated. The natural: like the signification of the yellowness of the face for fear, and the signification of its redness for shyness; for it is from the nature of a person that yellowness occurs on his face at the time of fear, and redness on his face at the time of shyness. The reason for the division of signification into what was mentioned: that signification either has a role for convention (wad') or not. If convention has a role in it, then it is conventional in speech and in other things. If convention has no role in it, then if it can change in itself, it is natural in speech and other things; if it cannot change, then it is rational in speech and other things. So these are six divisions. What is considered among them by the logicians is one category: verbal conventional signification. So they divided it into three categories: corresponding (mutābiqīyya), implicative (taḍammunīyya), and commitmental (iltizāmīyya). They considered it to the exclusion of others because of its generality, regularity, and ease of handling, unlike the natural, which is specific to some matters with a lack of confidence in its regularity because of the possibility of differences in natures, and its handling depends on investigation into what nature requires, which may be difficult. Likewise the rational, for it is specific to what has rational necessity between them, and intellects disagree and their understandings are not regulated based on the understanding of the ones understanding, and it depends on grasping the necessity, which may be difficult to handle, unlike the verbal conventional; for it depends only on looking into the convention, which is easy, so whenever the convention is known, it becomes regular in the individuals of what it is assigned to.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section (fasl) from a commentary on the Tadhheeb. It defines the three types of signification—rational, natural, and conventional—and then explains why logicians restrict their consideration to verbal conventional signification. The author first gives examples of rational signification: the concomitance of accidental attributes with a body indicates the body's temporal origination because the mind cannot accept that the concomitant of an originated thing is eternal. For natural signification, examples are the yellowing of the face from fear and reddening from shyness, which are natural responses. Then the author provides a logical classification: signification either involves convention or not. If convention is involved, it is conventional (whether in speech or otherwise). If not, and the signification can change, it is natural; if it cannot change, it is rational. This yields six types. Logicians consider only verbal conventional signification because it is universal, well-defined, and easy to handle. Natural signification is specific and unreliable due to variation in natures; rational signification is subject to differing intellects and difficult to grasp. Verbal conventional signification only requires knowing the convention, which is easy. This passage emphasizes the epistemological priority of conventional linguistic signification in logic.

Key Terms
عموم
انضباط
سهولة تنال
طبيعية
عقلية
وضعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is natural signification less reliable than conventional?
A) It is not based on convention
B) It is specific to some matters and natures can differ
C) It requires rational inference
D) It is not used in the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
Logic restricts itself to verbal conventional signification because it alone is universal, regular, and easy to know.

Definition of Dalala and Its Three Types
Author defines dalala and distinguishes its three types: mutabiqa, tadammuniyya, iltizamiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of verbal indication (dalala lafziyya): mutabiqa, tadammun, and iltizam, and identify each in a given example from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Dalala is the relation that moves the mind from the known to the unknown; its three types correspond to whole, part, or necessary concomitant.

Indication is the foundation of all reasoning from signs. In this lesson, we unpack the technical definition of dalala and its three types as presented in classical Islamic logic. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for precise interpretation of texts in fiqh, kalam, and tafsir.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the general definition of dalala
Dalala is defined as 'the state of a thing such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of something else.' This definition applies to all types of indication, not just one.
The commentator warns against reading the initial 'fa' as restricting the definition to mutabiqa only.
2
Understand mutabiqa (correspondence)
Mutabiqa is when the word indicates its entire intended meaning. Example: the word 'insan' indicates the whole definition 'rational animal'.
This is the most literal form of indication.
3
Understand tadammun (implication)
Tadammun is when the word indicates only a part of its meaning. Example: 'ayn' (eye) indicates the faculty of sight, which is a part of the meaning of 'eye'.
The part must be a necessary constituent of the whole meaning.
4
Understand iltizam (accompaniment)
Iltizam is when the word indicates something necessarily associated with its meaning but not part of it. Example: 'point' indicates the end of a line.
The associated meaning must be inseparable from the primary meaning in the mind.
5
Apply the definition to the three types
Check that each type satisfies the general definition: knowledge of the word leads to knowledge of something else. In mutabiqa, knowledge of 'insan' leads to knowledge of 'rational animal'. In tadammun, knowledge of 'ayn' leads to knowledge of 'seeing faculty'. In iltizam, knowledge of 'point' leads to knowledge of 'end of line'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في تعريفِ الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها. فالدَّلالَةُ : كَوْنُ الشَّيْءِ بِحَالَةٍ يَلْزَمُ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ بِهِ الْعِلْمُ بِشَيْءٍ آخَرَ. فَالتَّعريف لِمُطلقِ الدَّلالةِ؛ لَا لِخصوص المطابقيَّةِ الَّتي هي قسم مِنَ اللفظية الوضعيَّةِ كما يوهمُهُ التَّفريع بالفاء. ومثال المطابقة: دلالة الإنسان على الحيوان الناطق. ومثال التضمن: دلالة العين على الباصرة. ومثال الالتزام: دلالة النقطة على نهاية الخط.
Section: On the definition of the three indications and their rulings. Indication is: the state of a thing such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of something else. The definition is for absolute indication, not specifically for mutabiqa which is a division of verbal conventional indication, as the branching with 'fa' might suggest. Example of mutabiqa: the indication of 'insan' for 'rational animal'. Example of tadammun: the indication of 'ayn' for the eye's seeing faculty. Example of iltizam: the indication of the point for the end of the line.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage opens the section on the three types of indication (dalala) in Islamic logic. It first gives the technical definition of dalala as 'the state of a thing such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of something else.' The commentator immediately clarifies that this definition applies to indication in general, not just to one of its subdivisions. The three types are: (1) mutabiqa (correspondence), where the word indicates its entire meaning, like 'insan' indicating the whole definition 'rational animal'; (2) tadammun (implication), where the word indicates only part of its meaning, like 'ayn' (eye) indicating the faculty of sight; (3) iltizam (accompaniment), where the word indicates something necessarily associated with the meaning, like 'point' indicating the end of a line. The commentator warns that the initial use of the particle 'fa' might mistakenly suggest the definition is restricted to mutabiqa, but it is not.

Key Terms
الدَّلالةُ (al-dalala): The state of a thing such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of something else; the relationship that causes the mind to move from the known to the unknown. [Note: In earlier texts, dalala was defined as 'understanding one thing from another' (fahm amr min amr), stressing the cognitive act; later muta'akhirun shifted to a more ontological definition focusing on the state of the indicator.]
مطابقيَّة (mutabiqa): Indication of a word for its entire intended meaning, e.g., 'insan' for 'rational animal'. [Note: Stable across the tradition; no significant shift.]
تضمُّنيَّة (tadammuniyya): Indication of a word for a part of its meaning, e.g., 'ayn' for the seeing faculty (part of the meaning of eye). [Note: Stable across the tradition; no significant shift.]
التزاميَّة (iltizamiyya): Indication of a word for something necessarily concomitant with its meaning but not part of it, e.g., 'point' for the end of a line. [Note: Stable across the tradition; no significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of indication is exemplified by the word 'eye' (عين) indicating the faculty of sight?
A) Mutabiqa (correspondence)
B) Tadammun (implication)
C) Iltizam (accompaniment)
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Dalala is the relation of a sign to its meaning, and it has three types: whole-part implication (mutabiqa), part-whole implication (tadammun), and necessary accompaniment (iltizam).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

(b) The word 'hajj' indicating the pilgrimage itself. (c) The word 'imam' indicating the leader of prayer, and by extension, moral guidance. Give reasons for each.

Objection and Answer on Defining Indication
Dialectical objection and answer on defining indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical mistake in the objection to the early definition of dalala and explain how the full definition resolves the confusion.

Core Idea
Indication is a property of the indicator, not the understander, when defined as 'understanding from something'.

In this passage, the author presents an objection to the early scholars' definition of indication (dalala) and then answers it. The objection claims that the definition describes the indicator using a property of the understander. The answer reveals that the objection relies on an incomplete version of the definition. Understanding this dialectical exchange sharpens your ability to spot when definitions are misquoted or taken out of context, a skill essential in juridical debates (jadal) and theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's core claim
The objector argues that the early definition of dalala as 'understanding from a thing' (fahm min amr) is flawed because 'understanding' is an attribute of the understander, while indication is an attribute of the indicator. This would make the definition attribute something to the indicator that belongs to someone else.
2
Locate the incomplete quotation
The answer points out that the objector only used part of the definition: just 'understanding' (fahm) without the qualifying phrase 'from something' (min amr). The full definition is 'a thing from which another thing is understood' (amr yufhamu minhu amr akhar). The preposition 'min' is crucial.
3
Understand the relational nature of the definition
In the complete definition, 'understanding from' (fahm min) is a relational concept that inheres in the indicator. The indicator is the source of the understanding, not the subject. The person only has 'understanding of' (fahm li) the thing, not 'understanding from' it.
4
Distinguish between 'fahm min' and 'fahm li'
The answer emphasises that the person is 'fahim' (one who understands) but not 'mafhum minhu' (that from which something is understood). The attribute of being understood from belongs solely to the indicator. Confusing these two leads to the objection's error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترضَ مَذهب المتقدمين بأنَّ الدّلالة وصفٌ للدَّالّ، والفهم وصف للفاهم، وحينئذ ؛ فيلزم على تفسيرهم تفسير ما هو وصف لأمر بما هو وصف لِغَيره ، وَلِذَا ؛ فسَّرها المتأخّرونَ بما عَلِمْتَ . وأُجيبُ : بأنَّ هذا الاعتراض غلط نشأ من الاقتصار على جزء المركب، حيث اقتصر على فَهم، وترك الجزء الآخر؛ وهو من أمر ، فإِنَّ الفهم الَّذِي فُسِّرَتْ به الدَّلالةُ ؛ فهم مُقيَّد بالمجرور بمن الذي هو الأمرُ الدَّالُّ، أنَّ الدَّلالة بمعنى كونُ أمرٍ يُفهَمُ منه بالفعل أمرُ آخَر وَلَا شكٍّ أنَّ الَّذي فهم منه أمرٌ؛ هو الأمرُ الدَّالُّ ؛ لَا غيره، والذي اتَّصفَ بِهِ غيرُه ؛ إنَّما هو الفهم لأمر ؛ أي: كونُهُ فَاهماً له؛ لا الفهم منه ؛ أي: كونُهُ مَفهوماً مفهوما منه ، فالشَّخصُ فاهم لا مفهوم منه .
And he objected to the doctrine of the early scholars that indication (al-dalala) is a description of the indicator (al-dall), and understanding (al-fahm) is a description of the one who understands (al-fahim). Then consequently, according to their interpretation, there would be an interpretation of that which is a description of one thing by that which is a description of another. Therefore, the later scholars interpreted it as you have learned. And it was answered: that this objection is a mistake arising from restricting oneself to part of the compound, where he restricted himself to 'understanding' and left the other part, which is 'from a thing' (min amr). For the understanding by which indication is interpreted is an understanding qualified by the preposition 'min' (from) which is the indicating thing. Indeed, indication means: a thing from which another thing is actually understood. There is no doubt that that from which a thing is understood is the indicator itself, not other than it. And that which is attributed to other than it is only the understanding of a thing, i.e., his being understanding of it; not the understanding from it, i.e., its being understood from it. So the person is understanding (fahim), not understood from (mafhum minhu).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objection and its answer concerning the definition of indication (dalala). The objector points out that if indication is defined as 'understanding from a thing', then in 'understanding from Zayd' (fahm min Zayd), the understanding is an attribute of the person, while indication should be an attribute of the indicator. This would be describing the indicator by something belonging to the understander. The answer clarifies that the definition was only partially quoted: the full definition is 'a thing from which another thing is understood' (amr yufhamu minhu amr akhar). Here, the understanding is not absolute but qualified by 'from', making it a relational concept. The indicator is the source, not the subject of understanding. The person is the one who understands (fahim), not the one understood from (mafhum minhu). Thus, the objection is based on an incomplete quotation.

Key Terms
الدلالة
الفهم
الدال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the objection, what is the correct interpretation of 'fahm' in the definition of dalala?
A) An absolute understanding by the understander.
B) An understanding qualified by 'from' (min), belonging to the indicator.
C) An understanding that is a description of the indicator's essence.
D) An understanding that is synonymous with 'ilm.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it truncates the definition; the full definition correctly places the property of indication with the indicator by using the relational phrase 'from which understanding proceeds'.

Defining Indication: The Role of the Connecting State (Ḥāla)
Author defines indication and commentator expands on the state's role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three elements of indication (indicator, indicated, state) and explain the commentator's refinement of the definition.

Core Idea
Indication is not simply a relation; it requires a specific state that enables the transition from indicator to indicated.

The author defines indication as 'a thing being in a state that necessitates transition from it to another thing'. This definition emphasizes that the connection is not arbitrary but is mediated by a state (ḥāla), which varies by type. The commentator al-ʿAṭṭār defends this definition against a rival interpretation, clarifying the role of the state. Understanding this definition is essential for analyzing how meaning is conveyed in Islamic texts and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
Find the indicator (al-dāll), the indicated (al-madlūl), and the state (al-ḥāla) in the definition. The definition states 'a thing being in a state that necessitates transition from it to another thing'.
2
Understand the bā' of association
The commentator explains that 'bi-ḥāla' means 'accompanying a state', not 'by a state'. This clarifies that the state is simultaneous with the indication.
In Arabic grammar, ba' can indicate causation or association; here it is association.
3
Recognize the state as the cause of transition
The state is that which makes the transition possible. For example, in conventional indication, the state is knowledge of the convention that links the word to its meaning.
4
Analyze the commentator's critique
Al-ʿAṭṭār rejects the interpretation that the state is the condition of necessary implication itself, because that would be redundant. Instead, the state is one of three specific modes (convention, nature, intellect).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُهُ : بِحَالَةٍ (الباء للملابسة ؛ أي: كونُ الشَّيء مُلتبساً بحالة، وهي الَّتي بينَ الدَّالّ ،والمدلول، بحيث ينتقل منه إليه بسببها، كالوضع في الوضعية، العطار العلاقةُ مُقَدَّرٍ مَنشؤُهُ تَعريفُ المصنفِ، حيثُ أخذ فيه الدَّالَّة والوضعَ؛ أي: إن أردتَ معرفةً الدَّلالةِ لوقوعِهَا جنساً في التّعريف، والوضع لوقوعِهِ فَضْلاً فِيهِ حَتَّى لا يكون تعريفاً بالمجهول ؛ فنقولُ الدَّلالة ا.هـ. و وفيهِ أَنَّهُ يشترط في المعرَّفِ أنْ يكونَ مَعلُوماً مِنْ قبلُ؛ لا أَنَّهُ يُذكَرُ ثُمَّ تُعرَّفُ أجزاؤُهُ، أَلا ترى قولَهُم : المعرَّفُ مَا يلزمُ مِن معرفتِهِ معرفة المعرّف، فهذا ينادي باشتراط سبق معرفته على المعرّف . (قَوْلُهُ : كَوْنُ الشَّيْءِ بِحَالَةٍ . . . إِلَخ ) لَمَّا كانَ هذا تعريفاً لِمُطلَقِ الدَّلالةِ؛ عبّرَ بلفظ شيءٍ حتّى ينطبق التّعريفُ على أقسام الدَّلالةِ كُلِّهَا، وَبَاءُ بِحَالَةٍ؛ لِلملابسة، فَهِيَ بمعنى مَعْ ؛ أي: مُصَاحِبَاً لِحالةٍ، وتلكَ الحالةُ هِيَ العِلْمُ بالوضع في الوضيَّعةِ أو اقتضاءُ الطَّبْعِ في الطَّبيعية أو مُجرَّدُ العَقْلِ في العقليَّةِ، ومَا قِيْلَ إِنَّ الحالةَ هِيَ قَوْلُهُ : يلزمُ مِنَ العِلْمِ بِهِ ... إلخ؛ فليسَ بِشيءٍ للزوم استدراكِ لَفْظِ حالةٍ في التّعريفِ، مع أَنَّهُ قصد بِهِ التَّنبية على وجهِ الدَّلالة، وهي إحدى هذه الأمور الثَّلاثةِ .
(His saying: 'in a state' (bi-ḥāla): the bā' is for association; i.e., a thing's being associated with a state which is between the indicator and the indicated, such that one moves from it to it because of it, like convention (al-waḍʿ) in conventional indication. al-ʿAṭṭār: The relation is implicit; its origin is the author's definition, where he included the indicator and the convention. That is, if you want to know the definition of indication because it occurs as a genus in the definition, and convention because it occurs as a differentia therein, so that it is not a definition by the unknown, we say: indication [is...]. End. And in it there is that it is required in the definiendum that it be known beforehand; not that it is mentioned and then its parts are defined. Do you not see their saying: The definiendum is that which, from its knowledge, knowledge of the definer follows? This calls for the condition of its prior knowledge to the definer. (His saying: 'a thing's being in a state... etc.') Since this is a definition of absolute indication, he used the word 'thing' so that the definition applies to all divisions of indication. And the bā' of 'bi-ḥāla' is for association, so it means 'with' (maʿ), i.e., accompanying a state. And that state is: knowledge of the convention in conventional [indication], or natural requirement in natural [indication], or mere intellect in rational [indication]. And what is said that the state is his saying: 'it necessitates from knowledge of it... etc.' is nothing, because it necessitates the repetition of the word 'state' in the definition, while he intended thereby to draw attention to the mode of indication, which is one of these three things.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is definitional: it explains the definition of indication (dalāla) as 'a thing being in a state (ḥāla) that causes a transition from it to another thing.' The commentator clarifies that 'bi-ḥāla' means 'accompanying a state', and the state varies according to the type of indication: conventional (waḍʿiyya) where the state is knowledge of the convention, natural (ṭabīʿiyya) where it is natural requirement, and rational (ʿaqliyya) where it is mere intellect. The commentator 'Attar criticizes a different interpretation that claims the state is the phrase 'it necessitates from knowledge of it' (the condition of necessary implication), arguing that this would be redundant. He also points out a methodological issue: the definiendum must be known before the definition; otherwise it would be circular. The definition uses 'thing' generically to cover all three types.

Key Takeaway
Indication is defined as a thing's being in a state that necessitates a transition to another thing; the state varies across the three types of indication.

Three Types of Indication and the Methodological Rule of Definition
Author lists the three types of indication and commentator inserts a methodological note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of indication based on their state and apply the principle that the definiendum must be known before its definition.

Core Idea
Indication is divided by its state: convention, nature, or intellect; and a definition is valid only if the term defined is already known.

The author distinguishes three types of indication based on the nature of the connecting state: conventional (waḍʿiyya) where the state is knowledge of the convention, natural (ṭabīʿiyya) where it is natural requirement (like smoke indicating fire), and rational (ʿaqliyya) where it is mere intellect (like an effect indicating a cause). The commentator also draws attention to a logical rule: the definiendum must be known before its definition, otherwise we fall into circularity. This rule is essential for sound definitions in all Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
List the three types of indication
From the passage: conventional (waḍʿiyya), natural (ṭabīʿiyya), and rational (ʿaqliyya).
2
Identify the state for each type
Conventional: knowledge of convention; Natural: natural requirement; Rational: mere intellect.
These states are the 'ḥāla' in the definition.
3
Apply the methodological rule
The commentator states that the definiendum must be known beforehand. Consider whether the definition of indication meets this condition: is 'indication' already known before its definition?
The commentator argues yes, otherwise the definition would be circular.
4
Test a real definition
Take a definition from fiqh, such as 'Ḥukm is Allah's address concerning the actions of the legally responsible'. Is the term 'Allah's address' known beforehand? If not, the definition fails the rule.

Necessity of a Relation Between Indicator and Indicated
Justification for requiring a relation in indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a relation (ʿalāqa) is required between the indicator and the indicated, and identify the logical principle that would be violated without it.

Core Idea
Without a specific relation, indication would be arbitrary — a logical impossibility called 'preference without a preponderator'.

In the definition of logic, a key condition is that the indicator and indicated must have a specific relation. This short passage explains why this condition is not arbitrary but necessary for rational coherence. We'll unpack the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being explained
The condition is that there must be a relation (ʿalāqa) between the indicator (dalīl) and the indicated (madlūl) that necessitates the transition from one to the other.
This is a core requirement for valid indication.
2
Understand the consequence of its absence
If no such relation existed, the indicator would indicate everything else equally, because there would be no reason to pick one indicated over another.
This leads to absurdity: the indicator would have no specific significance.
3
Recognize the logical principle at stake
Moving the mind from the indicator to one specific thing over everything else without a relation would be a 'preference without a preponderator' (tarjīḥ bi-lā murajjiḥ), which is rationally unacceptable in Islamic theology and logic.
This principle is often used in arguments for the existence of God and in legal reasoning.
4
Reconstruct the implicit argument
The argument: If there were no relation, then the indicator would indicate all things equally (absurd). Therefore, a relation must exist to avoid irrational preference.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the negation of the condition leads to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما اشترط في دلالة شيءٍ على آخَرَ أنْ يكونَ بينَهُما علاقةٌ تقتضي أن ينتقل منهُ إليه ؛ لأنَّهُ لولا ذلك؛ لدلَّ على جميع ما عداه؛ لأنَّ الانتقال إلى شيءٍ دونَ آخَر ترجيح من غيرِ مُرجّح .
And the condition was stipulated in the indication of one thing upon another that there be a relation between them that necessitates that one moves from it to it, because if not for that, it would indicate everything else; because moving to something over another is a preference without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage provides a brief but crucial justification for requiring a relation (ʿalāqa) in indication. The author argues that without a specific relation linking the indicator to the indicated, there would be no reason for the mind to move from the indicator to one particular thing rather than any other. Such a preference would be baseless, as it would be assigning a preponderance without a preponderating factor (tarjīḥ bi-lā murajjiḥ). This is a classical argument from the principle of sufficient reason. The implicit syllogism is: if no relation connects A to B specifically, then A could indicate any C; but indication is specific; therefore a relation is necessary.

Key Terms
علاقة
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the definition of indication requires it.

Key Takeaway
Indication requires a specific relation; otherwise, the mind's transition from indicator to indicated would be arbitrary and irrational.

Universal Necessity and Types of Indicators
Author expands on the condition 'it necessitates from knowledge of it knowledge of something else' and distinguishes types of indicators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dāll, dalīl, and amāra based on the type of knowledge they yield, and explain the condition of universal necessity (luzūm kullī).

Core Idea
The type of knowledge an indicator yields determines whether it is called a dāll, dalīl, or amāra.

In this passage, the commentator deepens the definition of indication by specifying that the necessity (luzūm) must be universal—holding at all times when the first knowledge exists. He then clarifies that 'knowledge' here means apprehension (idrāk), which can be conceptual or assertive, certain or uncertain. This leads to a threefold classification: things that yield conceptual knowledge are called indicators (dāll), those yielding certain knowledge are proofs (dalīl), and those yielding conjecture are signs (amāra). Understanding this classification is essential for evaluating the epistemic value of evidence in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being expanded
The commentator picks up the phrase 'it necessitates' (yalzamu) from the definition. He adds two conditions: after knowledge of the state and after knowledge of the context, to include metaphorical indications.
The expansion ensures metaphorical usage is covered.
2
Understand 'universal necessity' (al-luzūm al-kullī)
The necessity is universal, meaning that whenever the first knowledge is present, knowledge of something else necessarily follows; it never separates at any time.
This is stronger than occasional implication.
3
Recognize the broad sense of 'knowledge'
The first and second 'knowledge' refer to apprehension (idrāk), which includes both conceptual (taṣawwur) and assertive (taṣdīq) cognition, and can be certain or uncertain.
This broadens the scope of the definition.
4
Classify indicators by the type of knowledge they yield
If the first knowledge yields conceptual knowledge, the thing is called dāll (indicator). If it yields certain knowledge, it is called dalīl (proof). If it yields conjecture (ẓann), it is called amāra (sign) or persuasive proof.
The classification is based on epistemic output, not the thing itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُهُ : يَلْزَمُ ) أي : بعد العِلم بتلك الحالة، وبعد العِلْمِ بالقرينة؛ ليشمل دلالة الألفاظ على معانيها المجازيَّة، والمراد اللُّزومُ الكُلّيُّ؛ أي: يلزم من العِلْمِ بِهِ في جميع أوقاتِ ذلكَ العِلْم ؛ العِلْمُ بشيءٍ آخر ، فَلَا ينفك عنه في وقتٍ مِن أَوقاتِهِ، والمراد بالعِلْم الأَوَّلِ ، والثَّاني الإدراك أعمُّ مِن أنْ يكونَ تصوريًا أو تصديقيًا يقينيا أو غيره، لكنَّ إنْ كانَ العِلْمُ بالشَّيء يفيدُ العِلْمَ التّصوريَّ؛ سُمِّيَ ذلكَ الشَّيءُ دالا وإنْ كانَ مُفيداً لِلعِلْم اليقيني ؛ سُمِّي ذلكَ الشَّيءُ دليلاً ، وإِنْ كانَ مُفيداً لِلظَّنِّ؛ سُمّي ذلكَ الشَّيءُ دليلاً إقناعيًا وأمارة. واعلَمْ أَنَّ العِلْمَ غير اليقيني لا يُفيدُ عِلْماً يقينيا . (قَوْلُهُ : مِنَ الْعِلْمِ بِهِ) أي : بذلكَ الشَّيء، وقوله: (بشيء آخر) هو المدلول.
(His saying: 'it necessitates' (yalzamu)) i.e., after knowledge of that state, and after knowledge of the context (al-qarīna), to include the indication of words upon their metaphorical meanings. And what is intended is universal necessity: i.e., from knowledge of it in all times of that knowledge, knowledge of something else does not separate from it at any time. And what is intended by the first knowledge and the second is apprehension (idrāk), more general than being conceptual (taṣawwurī) or assertive (taṣdīqī), certain or otherwise. But if the knowledge of the thing yields conceptual knowledge, that thing is called an indicator (dāll). If it yields certain knowledge, that thing is called a proof (dalīl). And if it yields conjecture (ẓann), that thing is called a persuasive proof (dalīl iqnāʿī) and a sign (amāra). And know that non-certain knowledge does not yield certain knowledge. (His saying: 'from knowledge of it') i.e., from knowledge of that thing; and his saying: 'something else' is the indicated.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the definition of indication, specifically the condition 'it necessitates from knowledge of it knowledge of something else'. The commentator specifies that the necessity (luzūm) is universal (kullī), meaning it holds at all times when the first knowledge is present. The 'knowledge' here is broad: it is apprehension (idrāk), covering both conceptual (taṣawwur) and assertive (taṣdīq) cognition, certain or uncertain. The thing that produces conceptual knowledge is called an indicator (dāll). If it produces certain knowledge, it is called a proof (dalīl). If it produces only conjecture, it is called a persuasive proof or a sign (amāra). The distinction is based on the type of knowledge yielded, not on the indicator itself. The passage also notes that non-certain knowledge cannot produce certain knowledge. The remark about 'after knowledge of the state and after knowledge of the context' ensures the definition covers metaphorical usage where a context is needed.

Key Terms
اللزوم الكلي
دال
دليل
أمارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the term for a thing that yields certain knowledge (yaqīn)?
A) Dāll
B) Dalīl
C) Amāra
D) Qarīna

Key Takeaway
Indicators are classified by the type of knowledge they produce: dāll for conceptual, dalīl for certain, amāra for conjecture.
