The Concept of Luzūm: Impossibility of Separation
Super-commentator refines the meaning of necessary implication (luzūm) in the definition of indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of luzūm (existential, temporal, and cognitive) as defined by ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm in the context of indication.

Core Idea
Luzūm is not just logical entailment; it includes any inseparable connection between indicator and indicated.

In this passage, the super-commentator ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm refines the definition of 'necessary implication' (luzūm) used in the definition of indication. He explains that luzūm is the impossibility of separation between two things, and this can occur in existence, time, or knowledge. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing how words and meanings relate in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of luzūm
Locate the statement 'اللُّزومُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ امتناع الانفَكَاكِ' (luzūm is an expression for the impossibility of separation). Understand that this is the core definition.
The definition is unconditional; it covers all forms of inseparability.
2
Extract the three modes of luzūm
The text lists three modes: (1) in realization at one time, e.g. man and laughter; (2) in two successive times, e.g. correct reflection and knowledge of the result; (3) in knowledge, where two things are known together, e.g. correlatives (mutaḍāyifān).
These modes cover existential, temporal, and cognitive inseparability.
3
Note the epistemic nuance for indication
The text adds that in the context of indication by convention, the 'knowledge' required is mere attention (iltifāt), not full certainty. This lowers the epistemic bar for luzūm in conventional indication.
This refines the earlier understanding that indication requires definite knowledge.
4
Apply to a new Islamic example
Consider the relation between the word 'Hajj' and its meaning. This is a conventional luzūm based on the share of existence? Actually it is conventional, so it falls under the cognitive mode (known together by the linguistic community). Classify it accordingly.
Practice applying the three types to real examples from fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار (قَوْلُهُ: يَلْزَمُ مِنَ العِلْمِ بِهِ قالَ عبد الحكيم: أي في الجملةِ كَمَا هو المقرَّرُ مِنْ أَنَّ الحُكْمَ إذا أُطلِقَ عن الجهة؛ يتبادرُ منه الإطلاق العام، أعني : بعدَ العِلْمِ بوجه الدَّلالةِ؛ أعني : الوضع أو اقتضاءَ الطَّبع ، أو العِليَّةَ والمعلولية، أو بالعلم بالقرينة ليشمل دلالةَ اللَّفْظ على المعنى المجازي، واللُّزومُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ امتناع الانفَكَاكِ بينَ الشَّيئين بأن لا يتخلَّلَ بينَهُما أمرُ آخَرُ؛ سواء كانَ في التَّحقُّقِ في وقتٍ واحدٍ كالإنسانِ والضَّحكِ، أو في وَقتَيْنِ مُستَعقِبَاً لهُ كالنَّظَرِ الصَّحيح والعِلْمِ بالنتيجة أو في العِلْمِ بأنْ يعلما معاً بأن يكون إحدُهُما متعقلاً قصداً والثَّاني تبعاً، وإلا ؛ فإحضار أمرَيْنِ بِالبال مُحالٌ كما في المتضايفين والمدلول المطابقي والتضمني والالتزامي، أو يكونُ العِلْمُ بأحدِهِمَا مُستعقِباً لِلعِلْمِ بالآخَرِ بِلَا فَضْلٍ ؛ كَمَا في الدليل والمعرَّفِ واللَّفْظِ والمعنى، والمراد بالعِلْمَ هَهُنا ؛ مُجرَّدُ الالتفاتِ والتَّوحِهِ كما صرَّحَ بِهِ قُدَّسَ سِرُّهُ في حواشِي
al-ʿAṭṭār: (His saying: 'it necessitates from knowledge of it') ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm said: i.e., in general (fī l-jumla), as it is established that when a ruling is mentioned without qualification, the absolute general meaning is understood, meaning: after knowledge of the mode of indication, i.e., convention, natural requirement, or causality and effect, or by knowledge of the context (al-qarīna) to include the indication of a word upon its metaphorical meaning. And necessary implication (al-luzūm) is an expression for the impossibility of separation between two things, such that no other thing intervenes between them, whether in realization in one time, like man and laughter, or in two times where one follows the other, like correct reflection and knowledge of the result, or in knowledge in that they are known together such that one is intended primarily and the other secondarily—otherwise bringing two things to mind is impossible, as in mutual correlatives (al-mutaḍāyifān) and the indicated by correspondence, by inclusion, and by commitment—or that knowledge of one is followed by knowledge of the other without lag, as in the proof (dalīl) and the defined (muʿarraf), and the word and meaning. And what is meant by knowledge here is mere attention and direction (al-iltifāt wa-l-tawajjuh), as stated by the author (quddisa sirruhu) in his glosses.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the super-commentator ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm, quoted by al-ʿAṭṭār. It clarifies the nature of 'necessary implication' (luzūm) in the definition of indication. The implication is unconditional (bi-lā qayd) but depends on knowledge of the mode (wajh al-dalāla) and context (qarīna). Luzūm is defined as the impossibility of separation between the indicator and the indicated, which can be of several types: in existence (e.g., man and laughter inseparably co-exist), in time (e.g., reflection leads to knowledge), or in knowledge (e.g., correlatives like father and son are known together). In the case of indication by convention (dalāla waḍʿiyya), the knowledge is not full certainty but mere attention (iltifāt). This refines the previous commentary, showing that 'knowledge' in the definition is minimal: a mental turn toward something.

الالتفات

D) In knowledge

Key Takeaway
The super-commentator clarifies that luzūm in the definition of indication is the impossibility of separation in existence, time, or knowledge, and that the required knowledge is merely attention.

The Definition of Wad' (Positing) and Its Components
Definition of wad' and its commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of wad' and distinguish absolute wad' from wad' of words.

Core Idea
Wad' is the positing of any thing for another such that understanding one implies the other.

The definition of wad' is foundational in the Islamic science of language and logic. The author gives a concise definition; al-Dasuqi unpacks each part, clarifying that wad' is broader than just words. This lesson will dissect that definition into its logical components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find what is being defined. Here it is 'wad'' (الوضع). Note that the definition is for absolute wad', not just for words.
Al-Dasuqi explicitly states this is a definition of 'مطلق الوضع'.
2
Extract the genus (jins)
The genus is 'making a thing opposite another' (جعل الشيء بإزاء آخر). This places wad' in the category of relational acts of positing.
The word 'جعل' indicates an act of making or placing.
3
Extract the differentia (fasl)
The differentia is 'such that when the first is understood, the second is understood' (بحيث إذا فهم الأول فهم الثاني). This distinguishes wad' from mere juxtaposition.
The differentia includes a conditional relationship of understanding.
4
Classify the definition type
This is a complete definition (hadd tamm) because it has both genus and differentia covering all instances. Check: Does it include words and non-words? Yes, because 'thing' is general.
Al-Dasuqi confirms it covers both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوَضعُ : جَعْلُ الشَّيء بإزاء آخَرَ ، بحيثُ إذا فُهِمَ الأَوَّل ؛ فُهِمَ الثَّاني.
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : وَالْوَضْعُ) أي : ومطلقُ الوضع؛ كان وضع لفظ أو وضع غيره، فهو تعريف لمطلق الوضع؛ لا لوضع اللفظ ؛ إذْ هو جعل اللفظ بإزاء المعنى، فقوله: (جعل الشَّيء)؛ أي : لفظاً كان أو غيره، وقوله : (بإزاء) أي : بمقابلة آخر، وهو الموضوع له . وقوله : (بحيث إذا فهم الأول)؛ أي: بحيث إذا أُدرك الأوَّلُ وهو الموضوع؛ أي : وعُلِمَ وضعُهُ لِلشَّيءِ الَّذي جُعِلَ بإزائه، وقوله: (فهم الثاني)؛ أي: وهو الموضوع له ، واعترض بأنَّ «إذا» للإهمال، فتكون القضيَّةُ معها في حكم الجزئية، فتقتضي أَنَّهُ إذا فُهِمَ الأَوَّلُ ؛ يُفْهَمُ الثَّاني تارةً، وتارةٌ لا يُفهم معَ أَنَّهُ لا بُدَّ في الوضع مِن فَهُم الثَّاني عند فهم الأوَّلِ في جميع الأحوال والأوقات، فكان المناسبُ إبدال إذا بـ «كُلَّما»؛ الَّتي هي من سور الإيجاب الكُلِّيِّ، فتأمل.
And wad' (positing) is: making a thing opposite another, such that when the first is understood, the second is understood. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'And wad'') means: absolute wad', whether it be positing of a word or something else, so it is a definition of absolute wad', not of wad' of a word; for that is making a word opposite a meaning. So his saying: 'making a thing' means: whether a word or something else. His saying: 'opposite' means: corresponding to another, which is the thing posited for. His saying: 'such that when the first is understood' means: such that when the first, i.e., the posited thing, is perceived and its positing for the thing it is placed opposite is known. His saying: 'the second is understood' means: which is the thing posited for. And it is objected that 'idha' is for contingency, so the proposition with it is in the position of a particular, so it implies that sometimes when the first is understood the second is understood, and sometimes not, whereas in wad' it is necessary that the second is understood upon understanding the first in all states and times. So the appropriate thing is to replace 'idha' with 'kullama', which is a quantifier of universal affirmative. So reflect.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'wad'' (positing) and then clarifies its scope with a technical objection. The definition states that wad' is placing one thing in correspondence with another such that whenever the first is understood, the second is understood. Al-Dasuqi immediately explains that this is a definition of absolute wad', not specifically the positing of words (which is a subtype). He unpacks each part: 'making a thing' includes words and non-words; 'opposite another' refers to the thing posited for; 'such that when the first is understood' includes knowledge of the fact that it is placed for that thing. Then an objection is raised: the word 'idha' (when) in Arabic indicates contingency, making the proposition particular (i.e., not universal). This would imply that sometimes understanding the first yields understanding the second, sometimes not. But wad' requires that understanding the first always (in all cases) brings the second to mind. Therefore, the critic suggests replacing 'idha' with 'kullama' (whenever), which is a universal affirmative quantifier. The commentator invites the reader to reflect on this. The objection is a logical point about the strength of the condition in the definition.

Key Terms
الوضع
جعل
بإزاء
الموضوع
الموضوع له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the genus in the definition of wad' as given by the author?
A) Making a word opposite a meaning
B) Making a thing opposite another
C) Understanding the first upon the second
D) The condition of universal understanding

Key Takeaway
The definition of wad' consists of a relational genus and a differentia of mutual understanding, making it a complete logical definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the genus and differentia in the definition of 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning) in Usul al-Fiqh: 'qiyas is the application of a ruling of an original case to a new case due to a shared effective cause ('illah).' Is this a complete definition?
?
Consider the definition of 'zikr' (remembrance) in Tasawwuf: 'zikr is the presence of the heart with Allah.' What is the genus and differentia? Is it a complete definition or a description?

Why 'Kullama' Is More Precise Than 'Idha' in the Definition of Wad'
Objection regarding the particle 'idha' in the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'kullama' (whenever) is preferable to 'idha' (when) in the definition of wad' based on the logical requirement of universality.

Core Idea
In definitions, a particular conditional particle like 'idha' weakens the universal force required.

Al-Dasuqi raises an objection: the particle 'idha' in the definition suggests contingency, implying that understanding the first sometimes does not lead to the second. But wad' requires that it always does. This lesson examines the logical nuance and the substitution of 'kullama' for universal quantification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic particle
Find 'idha' in the definition. The objector argues that 'idha' expresses a conditional that is particular (جزئية), not universal. Check the logical implication: 'if P then Q' with 'idha' often implies only some cases.
In Arabic logic, 'idha' is considered a particle of contingency (إهمال).
2
Understand the requirement of wad'
Wad' demands that whenever the posited thing (the first) is understood, the posited-for meaning (the second) is necessarily understood. This must hold for all instances, not just some.
Think of a word: every time you hear the word 'kitab', you understand 'book'.
3
Evaluate the objector's replacement
The objector suggests replacing 'idha' with 'kullama' (whenever). 'Kullama' is a universal quantifier (سور الإيجاب الكلي), affirming that in every instance, understanding the first entails understanding the second.
This correction makes the definition logically precise, satisfying the requirement of universality.
4
Assess the validity of the objection
Is the objection valid? Linguistically, 'idha' can indeed imply particularity, but sometimes it is used universally. However, in logical definitions, precision is paramount. The commentator says 'فتأمل' (so reflect), inviting further thought.
This reflects the meticulousness of logicians in handling particles.

Source Text

Distinguishing the More Specific Evident Implication
Commentators define and refine the more specific evident implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the more specific evident implication from the more general evident implication by analyzing whether conceiving the antecedent alone suffices for certainty.

Core Idea
The more specific sense requires only the antecedent; the more general requires both.

In this passage from the Tadhheeb commentary, al-Dasouqi and al-Attar refine the definition of 'evident implication' (al-bayyin). They distinguish two senses: a more specific one where merely understanding the antecedent (malzum) yields certainty of the consequent, and a more general one where both must be conceived. This distinction is essential for evaluating the strength of logical connections in Islamic reasoning, such as in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses
Locate the terms 'al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass' (more specific) and 'al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'am' (more general). Note each is defined by a condition about what suffices for the mind's certainty.
The more specific is defined first; the more general is referenced later in contrast.
2
Extract the defining conditions
For the specific sense: 'yukfi fihi tasawwur al-malzum faqat li-jazm al-aql bi al-luzum' (conceiving only the antecedent suffices). For the general sense: both antecedent and consequent must be conceived. Write each condition in your own words.
The general sense is implied in the discussion about the example of evenness.
3
Analyze the example of blindness and sight
Al-Dasouqi gives the example that blindness implies sight. Why does this fit the specific sense? Because conceiving blindness necessarily entails conceiving sight (sight is part of the definition of blindness).
This shows how the antecedent alone triggers certainty of the consequent.
4
Understand the refinement by the commentators
Al-Dasouqi notes a problem: if the specific and general are defined by different conditions, they might seem mutually exclusive, but in reality they relate as general and specific in individuals. Al-Attar adds that the antecedent necessitates the consequent, so the former is intended and the latter follows derivatively.
The key is that the more specific is a subset of the more general in terms of instances, not concept.
5
Apply the distinction to a new case
Consider the implication: 'intoxication implies prohibition'. Does conceiving intoxication alone suffice to infer prohibition? Or must you also conceive the connection? Decide which sense applies based on the conditions.
This tests your ability to operationalize the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البين بالمعنى الأخص، وهو الذي يكفي فيه تصور الملزوم فقط لجزم العقل باللزوم. قال الدسوقي: (قوله: بالمعنى الأخص) أي: كلزوم البصر للعمى، فإنه لازم له؛ لأنه متى تصور العمى الذي هو الملزوم؛ تصور اللازم الذي هو البصر؛ لأنه مأخوذ في تعريفه، ولا بد من معرفة كل جزء من أجزاء التعريف حتى يعلم المعرف. (قوله: يكفي فيه) أي: في جزم العقل باللزوم، وهذا الجار متعلق بقوله: يكفي، وكذلك قوله: في جزم.. إلخ، لكن الثاني تعلق به بعد التقييد، والأول تعلق به مطلقا، فلا يرد أن فيه تعلق حرفي جر متحدين معنى بعامل واحد. واعلم أنه على ما ذكره في تعريف الأخص والأعم؛ لا يتأتى خصوص ولا عموم، بل عليه يكونان متباينين، وأما ما ذكرناه؛ فالخصوص والعموم عليه ظاهر كما علم، فالأعمية في الأفراد لا في المفهوم، فإذا كان تصور الاثنينية كافيا في جزم العقل باللزوم بين الاثنين والزوجية على ما قاله؛ فمن باب أولى في الكفاية إذا تصورهما، لكن حينئذ؛ يكون اللزوم أعم، وأما في القابلية؛ فلا بد في جزم العقل باللزوم من تصور الأمرين، ولا يكفي تصور الملزوم في اللزوم، فقد وجد الأعم بدون الأخص. قال العطار: (قوله: تصور الملزوم فقط) أي: ولا يحتاج لتصور اللازم بل يكون تصور الملزوم مقتضيا لتصور اللازم ومستدعيا له، فيحصل الأول قصداً والثاني تبعاً، ولا يمكن الانفكاك بينهما على ما تقدم شرحه. وعليه فالتحقيق مذهب الإمام من اعتبار اللزوم البين بالمعنى الأعم من باب أولى حيث اعتبر اللزوم العرفي. (قوله المحشي: فالأعمية في الأفراد... إلخ) أي: فكلما تحقق اللازم البين بالمعنى الأخص تحقق اللازم البين بالمعنى الأعم، وهذا لا ينافي اختلافهما في المفهوم شأن كل عام وخاص، وقوله على ما قاله يريد أن التمثيل بالزوجية لا يلائم الممثل له إذ هو من قبيل البين بالمعنى الأعم كما سيوضحه. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
The evident in the more specific sense is that for which conceiving only the antecedent suffices for the mind's certainty of the implication. Al-Dasouqi said: (His statement: by the more specific sense) i.e., like the implication of sight for blindness, for it is necessary to it; because whenever one conceives blindness which is the antecedent, one conceives the consequent which is sight, because it is taken in its definition, and one must know every part of the parts of the definition until the definiendum is known. (His statement: suffices in it) i.e., in the mind's certainty of the implication, and this prepositional phrase is attached to his word 'suffices', and likewise his statement: in certainty... etc., but the second is attached to it after restriction, and the first is attached to it absolutely, so it is not objected that it involves two prepositions identical in meaning attached to one operator. And know that according to what he mentioned in the definition of the more specific and the more general, neither particularity nor generality obtains; rather, according to him they are mutually exclusive. As for what we mentioned, particularity and generality are apparent as known, so the generality is in the individuals, not in the concept. And if conceiving twoness suffices for the mind's certainty of the implication between two and evenness according to what he said, then a fortiori it suffices if one conceives both, but in that case the implication is more general. As for possibility, there must be in the mind's certainty of the implication conceiving both things, and conceiving only the antecedent does not suffice for the implication, so the more general occurs without the more specific. Al-Attar said: (His statement: conceiving only the antecedent) i.e., it does not need conceiving the consequent, rather conceiving the antecedent requires conceiving the consequent and calls for it, so the first is obtained intentionally and the second derivatively, and separation between them is impossible as explained previously. According to this, the verifier's doctrine is that of the Imam regarding considering the evident implication in the more general sense a fortiori, since he considered customary implication. (The glossator's statement: So the generality is in the individuals... etc.) i.e., whenever the evident in the more specific sense is realized, the evident in the more general sense is realized, and this does not contradict their difference in concept, as is the case with every general and specific. And his statement 'according to what he said' means that the example of evenness is not suitable for what it exemplifies because it is of the type of evident in the more general sense as he will clarify. End of al-Sharnubi's note.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the commentators al-Dasouqi and al-Attar on the definition of the "evident implication in the more specific sense" (al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass). The text first defines it: it is the implication where merely conceiving the antecedent (al-malzum) is sufficient for the mind to be certain of the logical connection (luzum). Al-Dasouqi explains this with an example: the implication of sight for blindness. When one conceives blindness, one necessarily conceives sight as well, because sight is part of the definition of blindness. He then clarifies a grammatical point: the prepositional phrase "in certainty" is attached to "suffices" after restriction, while the first phrase is attached absolutely. He then points out a problem: if one defines the more specific and more general as mutually exclusive based on concept, then no generality or particularity holds between them; but in reality the more general is more inclusive in individuals, not in concept. He argues that if the example of evenness (zawjiyya) for two (ithnayn) is used, then conceiving twoness alone suffices for certainty, but then the implication is more general, not specific. The implication in the "specific" sense requires that the antecedent alone guarantees the consequent, while in the "general" sense one must conceive both. Al-Attar adds that the antecedent necessitates the consequent, so the first is intended and the second follows. The verifier's doctrine aligns with the Imam's (likely al-Razi or al-Ghazali) that customary implication (al-luzum al-'urfi) is considered for the more general. The glossator (al-Muhashi, possibly al-Sharnubi) clarifies that the more specific implies the more general, but they differ in concept. The example of evenness is not suitable because it is a case of the more general, not the more specific; a better example is blindness and sight.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of the more specific evident implication?
A) It requires conceiving both the antecedent and the consequent.
B) It is limited to examples from the physical world.
C) Conceiving only the antecedent suffices for certainty of the implication.
D) It is identical to customary implication (al-luzum al-'urfi).

Key Takeaway
The more specific evident implication is distinguished by the sufficiency of the antecedent alone; the more general requires both antecedent and consequent, and they differ in concept but not in individual instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the implication: 'Smoke implies fire'. Does conceiving smoke alone suffice to infer fire? Or must you also conceive the connection between smoke and fire? Determine which sense of evident implication applies, and justify using the conditions from the passage.
?
In usul al-fiqh, the implication of a command (amr) for obligation (wujub) is often debated. Does the specific sense apply here? Use the definition to analyze whether conceiving the command alone suffices for certainty of obligation.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
The Conditional Syllogism Proves Words Do Not Signify Everything
Author reviews schools on what words signify and proves words do not signify every external thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) and apply it to arguments about the limits of linguistic signification.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism refutes the claim that words signify everything external.

In this passage, the commentator builds a rigorous logical proof that the scope of a word's signification is limited. By presenting the argument as a conditional syllogism (if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P), he demonstrates that if words signified every external thing, they would have to signify an infinite number of meanings—which is impossible. This lesson walks you through the syllogistic structure so you can recognise and deploy such arguments yourself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional claim
Find the 'if...then' statement in the passage. The conditional is: 'If the utterance signifies every external thing, then it signifies infinite meanings.'
The Arabic uses 'لو...لزم' (if...then necessarily).
2
Locate the antecedent and consequent
The antecedent (P) is 'the utterance signifies every external thing'. The consequent (Q) is 'the utterance signifies infinite meanings'.
The passage explicitly mentions both parts.
3
Identify the exception (istithna')
The consequence (Q) is denied: 'The consequent is false' – i.e., it is not the case that utterances signify infinite meanings.
The denial is based on the obvious fact that speakers do not attend to infinite meanings.
4
Determine the conclusion
Since Q is false, the antecedent P must also be false. Therefore, the utterance does NOT signify every external thing. This is modus tollens.
The conclusion is the contradictory of the antecedent.
5
Check validity
The argument is valid modus tollens: if P implies Q, and not Q, then not P. No formal fallacy occurs.
The passage confirms the validity by noting that the consequent is false, so the antecedent must be false.

الخاص، وإِنْ أريدَ بِهِ أنَّه يلتفتُ إليهِ بالذات؛ فَيجوزُ أن يكون الأمرُ الخارجي أيضاً كذلك، وذهب المحقِّقُ الطُّوسِيُّ والقُطْبُ الشَّيرازي والتفتازاني والدُّوَانيُّ وغيرُهُم مِنَ المحققين إلى أنَّ الألفاظ موضوعةٌ بِإِزاءِ الأمورِ الخارجيَّةِ ؛ لأنَّها الملتفّتُ إليها بالذَّاتِ، وهُوَ مِن ضَروريَّاتِ الموضوع لَهُ بِخلافِ الصُّوَرِ الذهنيَّةِ؛ فإنَّها مِرأَةٌ لِمُشاهدتها، وذهب بعضُ الأفاضِلِ إِلى أَنَّ الألفاظ موضوعةٌ للمعاني من حيثُ هِيَ هِيَ لَا لِلصُّوَرِ الدّهنية أو الخارجيَّةِ؛ لمَّا أنَّ مناط التّعلّمِ والتَّعليم المحتاج إليهِمَا في التَّمدُّنِ إِنَّما هو المعاني مطلقاً؛ لا الخصوصيَّاتُ الذهنيَّةُ أو الخارجيَّةُ ؛ فإنَّها مُلغاةٌ، والحقُّ هو هذا ؛ لأنَّ الموضوع له في الحقيقة نَفْسُ الشَّيء مِن حيثُ هو عينيا كان أو ذهنيا ؛ سواء كان حاصلا في الذهن بِنَفْسِهِ أو بوجه ما لا لشيءٍ مِن حيثُ الاكتنافُ بالعوارض الذهنيَّةِ أو الخارجيَّةِ؛ فإنَّ كثيراً من معاني الألفاظ ليست بموجودة في الخارج، وكثيراً مِنها لَيسَتْ في الأذهانِ كَلَفْظِ اللَّهِ سبحانه وتعالى، وليسَ في وَضْعِ الألفاظ تفاوت، وفي حاشية مير زاهد: القولُ بأنَّ الألفاظ موضوعةٌ بإزاء الأمور الخارجيَّةِ ؛ ظَاهرُ البطلان؛ لأنَّ كَثيراً من معاني الألفاظ لَيسَتْ مَوجودَةٌ في الخارج، وليسَ في وَضْع الألفاظ تفاوت، وأنَّ الموضوعَ لَهُ يجب أنْ يكونَ مَعلُوماً بالذَّاتِ والغَيرَ الخارج معلوم بالعرض؛ لَا بالذَّاتِ، وإلا ؛ ينتفي العِلْمُ بانتفائه، فيصرفُ هذا القولُ عَن الظَّاهِرِ بأنَّ المراد بالمعنى الخارجيّ نَفْسُ الشَّيء؛ مَعَ قَطْع النَّظَرِ عَن كَوْنِهِ فِي الذِّهْنِ ا.هـ (قَوْلُهُ : وَاللَّفْظُ لَا يَدُلُّ) أي : اللَّفظُ الموضوع ؛ لأنَّهُ المحدَّثُ عنه. • وإلا ؛ لَزِمَ أن يكون كلُّ لفظ موضوع لمعنى دالا على معانٍ غير متناهية؛ وهو باطل. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِلَّا ، لَزِمَ ... إلخ) أي : وإلا بأن دلَّ اللفظ الموضوع لمعنى على كلِّ أمرٍ خارج، والحالُ : أنَّ الألفاظ الموضوعةً مُتساوية في كونها موضوعةٌ؛ لزمَ أنْ يكونَ كلَّ لفظ موضوع دالا على معانٍ غيرِ مُتناهية؛ لِشمول المعاني للموجودات والمعدومات. (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ بَاطِلٌ) أي : أنَّ هذا اللازم باطل، وهو دلالة اللفظ على معانٍ غيرِ متناهية؛ أي: وإذا بطل اللازم ؛ بطل المقدَّم، وهو دلالة اللفظ على كلِّ خارج، وإذا بطل هذا ثبت نقيضُه، وهو أنَّه لا يدلُّ على كلِّ خارج، بل لا بُدَّ مِن شرط، ؛ فَقولُهُ: (فَلَا بُدَّ ... إلخ)؛ تفريع على بطلان اللازم ؛ ليرتب عليه بطلانُ المقدَّمِ المترتب عليه ثبوت نقيض المقدَّم ؛ أي: لأنَّه ليس عندنا لفظ يدلُّ على معانٍ غيرِ متناهية ؛ أي : لعدم الالتفاتِ عند إطلاق لفظ منها إلى المعاني الغير المتناهية؛ لا إجمالاً وَلَا تفصيلاً. المطار دالا جميع على (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِلَّا ؛ لَزِمَ قياس استثنائي تقريرُهُ :هكذا : لَوْ دلَّ اللَّفْظُ على كُلِّ خارج؛ لَزِمَ أَنَّ كُلَّ لَفْظ موضوع لمعنى دَالّ على معانٍ غير متناهية، والتّالي باطل، فبطل المقدَّمُ؛ فثبت نَقيضُهُ وهو المدَّعَى، وكُلُّ مِنَ اللازم وبطلانِ التَّالي ظَاهِرُ، قَالَ الفَاضِلُ عبد الحكيمِ: لَو دلَّ اللَّفْظُ الموضوع على كُلِّ أمرٍ خارج ؛ والحالُ أَنَّ الألفاظ الموضوعةِ مُتساويةٌ في كَوْنِهَا مَوضوعةٌ ؛ لَزِمَ أنْ يكون كُلُّ لفظ معانٍ غيرِ مُتناهيةٍ ؛ لِشُمولها الموجودات والمعدوماتِ تفصيلاً وإجمالاً لخروجها عَنِ الموضوع لَهُ، وهوَ ظَاهِرُ البطلانِ؛ لِعَدَم الالتفاتِ عند إطلاق لفظ منها إلى المعاني الغير المتناهية؛ لا إجمالاً ولا تفصيلاً .ا.هـ. ، وبِهَذا؛ ظهرَ سِرُّ عُدول الشَّارِح عَنِ الظَّاهِيرِ وهو قَولُهُ: وإلا ؛ لَزِمَ أنْ يكونَ دالا إلى ما ذكرَهُ لَمَّا أَنَّ الألفاظ مُتساوية في ذلك؛ فنيَّة بالعدول عليه. جميعَ
Chapter: On the definition of the three significations and their rulings. The utterance does not signify every external thing. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'And the utterance' means: The state is that the utterance does not signify everything external to the meaning coined for. Al-Attar: Al-Farabi and Qutb al-Razi hold that utterances are coined for mental images insofar as they are mental, because they are the known per se, not the external thing qua external; otherwise knowledge would cease with its cessation. And in it there is discussion because if what is meant by 'known per se' is that it is imprinted in the mind in itself, then it is not necessary, neither at the time of coinage nor at the time of use; it suffices that it occurs in some manner, as you see in the general coinage for a specific object. And if what is meant is that it is attended to per se, then it is possible that the external thing is also such. And al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi, Qutb al-Shirazi, al-Taftazani, al-Dawani, and others among the investigators hold that utterances are coined for external realities, because they are attended to per se, and that is among the necessities of the object of coinage, unlike mental images, for they are a mirror for observing them. And some of the distinguished scholars hold that utterances are coined for meanings in themselves, not for mental images or external things, because the basis of learning and teaching needed in civilizational life is meanings absolutely, not mental or external particularities, for they are disregarded. The truth is this, because the object of coinage in reality is the thing itself insofar as it is either external or mental, whether it is present in the mind by itself or in some manner, not something insofar as it is clothed with mental or external accidents. For many meanings of utterances are not existent externally, and many of them are not in minds, like the word 'Allah' (glorified and exalted). And there is no difference in the coinage of utterances. And in the gloss of Mir Zahid: The statement that utterances are coined for external realities is apparently false, because many meanings of utterances are not existent externally, and there is no difference in the coinage of utterances, and the object of coinage must be known per se, and the external other is known by accident, not per se; otherwise knowledge would cease with its cessation. So this statement is diverted from the apparent meaning by interpreting 'external meaning' as the thing itself, regardless of its being in the mind. End quote. (His statement: 'And the utterance does not signify') i.e., the coined utterance, because it is what is being discussed. • Otherwise, it would necessitate that every utterance coined for a meaning signifies infinite meanings; and that is false. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'Otherwise, it would necessitate... etc.') i.e., Otherwise, if the utterance coined for a meaning signified every external thing, while the state is that coined utterances are equal in their being coined, then it would necessitate that every coined utterance signifies infinite meanings, because the meanings include existents and non-existents. (His statement: 'And it is false') i.e., this consequent is false, namely the signification of the utterance for infinite meanings; i.e., when the consequent is false, the antecedent is false, namely the signification of the utterance for every external thing, and when this is proven false, its contradictory is established, namely that it does not signify every external thing, but rather a condition is necessary. So his statement: 'Then it is necessary... etc.' is a branching from the falsity of the consequent to establish the falsity of the antecedent, upon which the establishment of the contradictory of the antecedent follows. i.e., because we do not have a utterance that signifies infinite meanings, i.e., due to the lack of attention when uttering a word to the infinite meanings, neither in summary nor in detail. Al-Attar: ...all... (His statement: 'Otherwise, it would necessitate: a conditional syllogism, its exposition is thus: If the utterance signified every external thing, it would necessitate that every utterance coined for a meaning signifies infinite meanings, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false; so its contradictory is established, which is the claim. Both the consequent and the falsity of the consequent are apparent. Al-Fadil Abd al-Hakim said: If the coined utterance signified every external thing, while the state is that coined utterances are equal in their being coined, then it would necessitate that every utterance be [for] infinite meanings, because they include existents and non-existents in detail and in summary, because they are outside the object of coinage, and that is apparently false, due to the lack of attention when uttering a word to the infinite meanings, neither in summary nor in detail. End quote. And with this, the secret of the commentator's departure from the apparent meaning is revealed, which is his statement: 'Otherwise, it would necessitate that it be signifying' to what he mentioned, because utterances are equal in that; so the intention in the departure from it is [clear].
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the proposition 'The utterance does not signify every external thing,' which is part of a chapter on the three types of signification. The commentary first reviews three major positions on what utterances signify: (1) mental images (al-Farabi, Qutb al-Razi), (2) external realities (al-Tusi, Qutb al-Shirazi, al-Taftazani, al-Dawani), and (3) meanings in themselves (some distinguished scholars). The author endorses the third view as the truth: the object of coinage is the thing itself, whether mental or external, without regard to accidents. He supports this by noting that many words (like 'Allah') have meanings that are not external or mental. He then proceeds to the main argument: if an utterance signified every external thing, then any word would signify infinite meanings (since existents and non-existents are infinite), which is patently false because when we utter a word we do not attend to an infinite number of meanings. This is a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i): if antecedent (word signifies every external) then consequent (word signifies infinite meanings); consequent is false, so antecedent is false; thus the contradictory is established: the word does not signify every external. The commentator al-Dasuqi and then al-Attar and others elaborate on the reasoning, emphasizing that the falsehood of the consequent forces the falsehood of the antecedent, and the argument is sound. The point is to show that signification is restricted to a specific object, not everything external.

Key Terms
اللفظ (al-lafẓ): the uttered word or expression, considered as a signifier
الموضوع له (al-mawḍūʿ lahu): the object or meaning for which a word is coined; the intended referent
الصور الذهنية (al-ṣuwar al-dhihniyya): mental images or concepts that exist in the mind
الأمور الخارجية (al-umūr al-khārijiyya): external realities that exist outside the mind
المعاني (al-maʿānī): meanings considered abstractly, without regard to mental or external existence [Note: In some later texts, 'ma'ani' may be used synonymously with 'concepts', but here it is distinguished from mental and external particulars.]
قياس استثنائي (qiyās istithnā'ī): conditional syllogism where one premise is a conditional and the other is the assertion or denial of one part

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the conditional syllogism presented in the passage, what is the antecedent that is shown to be false?
A) The word signifies infinite meanings.
B) The word signifies every external thing.
C) The word signifies its coined meaning only.
D) The word signifies mental images.

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) is a powerful tool to refute a claim by showing that it leads to an absurd or impossible consequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

But it is not categorical because there is a contextual indicator (qarīna) showing it is restricted. Therefore, it does not imply obligation.' Identify the antecedent, consequent, and the exception. What Is Mantiq?

The Composite Concept of Blindness: Material and Formal Parts
Commentary analyzes the definition of blindness as composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the material and formal parts of a composite concept and determine which part is included in the definition.

Core Idea
Blindness is not mere absence; it is absence specifically related to sight.

The commentator explains that the definition of blindness as 'absence of sight' does not mean absolute absence, but absence that is added to sight. This makes the concept of blindness composite: it has a material part (the absence itself) and a formal part (the relation to sight). Understanding this structure is essential for correctly analyzing definitions and their significations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term is 'blindness' (al-'ama). Locate how the commentator defines it: 'absence of sight from what is disposed to be seeing'.
2
Extract the material part
The material part is absence (al-'adam). But note: it is not absolute absence; it is a specific absence.
3
Extract the formal part
The formal part is the addition or relation (al-idafa) of that absence to sight.
The addition gives form to the concept.
4
Distinguish between mudaf and mudaf ilayhi
The 'added thing' (al-mudaf) is absence, and it is considered insofar as it is added, so the addition is included and the thing added to (sight, al-mudaf ilayhi) is excluded.
This distinction determines what counts as part of the concept.
5
Determine whether sight is part of the concept
Sight is outside the concept of blindness because the definition takes absence as added to sight, not sight itself.
This has implications for how blindness signifies sight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّه عدمُ البَصَرِ عمَّا من شأنه أن يكونَ بصيراً. (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّهُ عَدَمُ الْبَصَرِ ) أي : العدمُ المضافُ للبصر؛ لا مُطلق العدم، وحينئذٍ؛ فَمفهوم العمى مُركَّب من جزأين جزء مادّيّ، وهو العدم، وجزء صوري: وهو الإضافة، ويكون البصر خارجاً عن مفهوم العمى؛ لأنَّ المضاف إذا أخذ في المفهوم من حيثُ إِنَّهُ مُضاف؛ كانَتِ الإضافة داخلة فيه، والمضاف إليه خارجاً. وإذا أخذ من حيث ذاتُه ؛ كانَتِ الإضافةُ خارجة عن المفهومِ كَالمضاف إليه . وقد علمتَ أنَّ مفهوم العمى : هو العدمُ المضافُ للبصرِ مِن حيثُ إِنَّه مضاف، فتكونُ الإضافة للبصر داخلةً في مفهوم العمى، والبصر خارجاً عنه . وعلى هذا؛ فَدلالة العمى على كلِّ من العدمِ والإضافة؛ تَضمُّنيَّةٌ، وعلى البصر التزامية. (قَوْلُهُ : عَمَّا مِنْ شَأْنِهِ ... إلخ) أي شأن شخصه، فدخل فيه زيد الأعمى، وزيد الأكمه، والعقرب، فيتّصفُ جميعها بالعمى؛ لأنَّ شأنَ أشخاصِهَا أن تكونَ بصيرة، وخرج الحائط مثلاً ، فلا يتّصفُ بالعمى؛ لأنَّهُ ليسَ شأنُ شخصِها أن يكونَ بصيراً. العطار تتحقق دلالة الالتزام بدونه؛ فليس شرطاً فيها، ينتج : اللُّزوم الخارجي ليسَ شَرْطاً في دلالة الالتزام، لا يُقَال : العَمَى عدمُ البَصَرِ ، فيكونُ البَصَرُ جزء المفهوم؛ فتكونُ الدَّلالة تضمُّنيَّةٌ، وحاصِلُ الجوابِ ما حقَّقَهُ السَّيِّدُ أَنَّ المضاف إذا أُخِذَ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ مُضَافٌ؛ كانَتِ الإضافةُ داخِلَةٌ فيه، والمضافُ إليه خارِجَاً عنه، وإذا أُخِذَ مِن حيثُ ذاتُه ؛ كانَتِ الإضافة أيضاً خارجةٌ عَنْهُ ، ومفهومُ العَمّى هو العَدَمُ المضَافُ إلى البَصرِ مِنْ حيثُ هو مُضَافٌ، فتكونُ الإضافة إلى البصرِ داخلة في مفهوم العمى، ويكونُ البَصَرُ خَارِجَاً عنه .ا.هـ. وقد استدلَّ الدَّوَّاني على خروج البصرِ عَن مُسمَّى العمى بأنَّ إسناده إلى البصر شَائِعٌ بِدونِ قرينةٍ مَجازيَّةٍ ، قال تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَرُ وَلَكِن تَعْمَى الْقُلُوبُ الَّتِي فِي الصُّدُورِ ﴾ [الحج : ٤٦]، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَأَعْمَىٰ أَبْصَرَهُمْ ﴾ [محمد : ۲۳] إلى غيرِ ذَلِكَ مِنَ النَّظائرِ الشَّائعة، والأصْلُ الحقيقةُ ا.هـ. ، وقَوْلُهُ : بدون قرينة مجازيَّةٍ ؛ أي: بدونِ قرينة تدلُّ على أنَّ العَمَى المسند للبصر مجاز لغوي؛ بأن يذكر. فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها فيكون البصر لازماً للعمى في الذهن مع المعاندة بينهما في الخارج. (قَوْلُهُ: الْمُعَانَدَةِ) أي : المنافاة بينهما في الخارج، وحينئذٍ ; فَلَا مُلازمةً بينَهُما فيه . العطار لفظ العَمَى الموضوعُ لِلعَدَمِ مَع التَّقييدِ بِالبصرِ ، ويُرادُ مُطلَقُ العدم، ونقض دليلَهُ أبو الفتح بأنَّه لَو تمَّ؛ لَدَلَّ على أن يكونَ التَّقييدُ بالبصر أيضاً خارجاً عَنِ العَمَى؛ لأنَّه لو كانَ دَاخِلاً فيهِ؛ لم يصح إسنادُهُ لِلبَصَرِ بدونِ قرينةٍ مجازيَّةٍ؛ ضرورةً أن المسند إلى البصر هو العدمُ المطلقُ ; لا المقيَّدُ بِالبصرِ ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ العَمَى عبارةً عَن مُطلَقِ العَدَم ، وهو بَاطِلٌ قَطْعاً، والحقُّ أنَّا لا نسلّمُ صحة إسناده إلى البصر بدونِ قرينة مجازيَّة; إذ الأمثلة المذكورة مشتملة على القرينة وهِيَ نَفْسُ إسناده إلى البصر، وأمَّا قَوْلُهُ : والأصلُ الحقيقةُ ; فَفيهِ أنَّ الصَّارفَ عَنِ الحقيقةِ مَوجودٌ هَهُنَا ، وهُوَ لُزوم المجازيَّةِ باعتبارِ التَّقييدِ بالبصر ; سواء كانَ نَفْسُ البصر داخلاً فيه أو خارجاً عنه ١ هـ وأجاب مير زاهد بأنَّ المسند إلى البصرِ هُوَ نَفْسُ العَمَى، والنِّسبةُ لَيْسَتْ دَاخِلَةٌ فيهِ; بَل فيما يعبّر عنه، وإلا ; لَكانَ العَمَى أمراً نسبيًّا ، وقد اشتهر بينَهُم الفرقُ بينَ جُزء الشَّيء وجُزءٍ مَفهومه ; فالعَمَى صِفَةٌ بسيطةٌ قائمة بالأعمى، وحقيقتُهُ عَدَمٌ خاص يعبر عنهُ بِعَدَم البصر، فالتَّقييدُ بِهِ داخل في هذا المفهوم العنواني وخارج عن حقيقتِهِ البسيطةِ، وَلَمَّا كانَتِ الألفاظ موضوعةً لِلحقائقِ دونَ عنوانها ; كانَ دلالةُ العَمَى على البَصَرِ دَلالة على خارج عَنِ الموضوعِ لَهُ ، وكانَ إسناده إليه على سبيل الحقيقة من غَيْرِ تجريدٍ ومَجاز . .ا.هـ. وقال عبد الحكيم : تركُ ذِكْرِ البَصَرِ مَعَهُ فَي نَحْوِ قَولِهِ تعالى: ﴿هُم بُكُمُ عُمّى ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸]، وفي قوله تعالى : وبَلْ هُم مِّنْهَا عَمُونَ ﴾ [النمل: يدلُّ على دخول البصر في مفهومه، وذِكْرُهُ معه في نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ [الحج: ٤٦] ; يدلُّ على خُروجِهِ عَنْهُ كَي لا يحتاج إلى تجريد ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : مَعَ الْمُعَانَدَةِ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي الْخَارِجِ فَإِنَّهما متقابلانِ تَقابُلَ العَدَمِ والملكة.
Because it is the absence of sight from what is disposed to be seeing. His saying: 'Because it is the absence of sight' means: the absence added to sight, not absolute absence. Then the concept of blindness is composed of two parts: a material part, which is absence, and a formal part, which is the addition. And sight is outside the concept of blindness, because when the added thing is taken in the concept insofar as it is added, the addition is included in it and the thing to which it is added is outside. And if it is taken in terms of its essence, the addition is outside the concept like the thing to which it is added. And you have known that the concept of blindness is: the absence added to sight insofar as it is added, so the addition to sight is included in the concept of blindness, and sight is outside it. Based on this, the signification of blindness upon both absence and addition is by inclusion, and upon sight is by implication. His saying: 'from what is disposed' etc. means the disposition of its individual, so it includes Zayd the blind, Zayd the born blind, and the scorpion, all of them are described by blindness because the disposition of their individuals is to be seeing, and the wall, for example, is excluded, so it is not described by blindness because the disposition of its individual is not to be seeing. Al-'Attar: The signification by implication is realized without it; so it is not a condition in it. It results: external necessary concomitance is not a condition in signification by implication. It cannot be said: blindness is the absence of sight, so sight is part of the concept; then signification would be by inclusion. The summary of the answer is what al-Sayyid verified: that when the added thing is taken insofar as it is added, the addition is included in it and the thing added to it is outside it; and when taken in terms of its essence, the addition is also outside it. And the concept of blindness is the absence added to sight insofar as it is added, so the addition to sight is included in the concept of blindness, and sight is outside it. End. And al-Dawani argued for the exclusion of sight from the meaning of blindness by the fact that its attribution to sight is common without a metaphorical indicator. He said, the Exalted: 'For indeed they are not the eyes that become blind, but it is the hearts in the chests that become blind' (al-Hajj 46), and the Exalted: 'And He made their eyes blind' (Muhammad 23), and other common examples, and the principle is literalness. End. And his saying: without a metaphorical indicator means: without an indicator indicating that blindness attributed to sight is a linguistic metaphor, by mentioning. Section: On the definition of the three significations and their rulings. Then sight becomes a necessary concomitant of blindness in the mind while there is opposition between them in reality. His saying: 'opposition' meaning: mutual exclusion between them in reality, and then there is no necessary connection between them in reality. Al-'Attar: The word 'blindness' is placed for absence with restriction to sight, and absolute absence is intended. Abu al-Fath refuted his proof by saying that if it were valid, it would indicate that the restriction to sight is also outside of blindness; because if it were included in it, its attribution to sight would not be correct without a metaphorical indicator, necessarily because what is attributed to sight is absolute absence, not the absence restricted to sight. So it would follow that blindness is absolute absence, which is absolutely false. The truth is that we do not concede the correctness of its attribution to sight without a metaphorical indicator; because the mentioned examples include the indicator, which is the very attribution to sight. As for his saying: the principle is literalness; it is responded that the deterrent from literalness exists here, which is the necessity of metaphoricity due to the restriction to sight, whether sight itself is included in it or outside it. End. And Mir Zahid answered that what is attributed to sight is the very blindness, and the relation is not included in it; rather in what it expresses. Otherwise, blindness would be a relative matter. And the difference between the part of the thing and the part of its concept is well-known among them. So blindness is a simple quality subsisting in the blind person, and its reality is a specific absence expressed by 'absence of sight'. So the restriction to it is included in this conceptual designation and outside its simple reality. And since words are placed for realities, not for their designations, the signification of blindness upon sight is signification upon something outside the subject matter, and its attribution to it is literal without abstraction or metaphor. End. And 'Abd al-Hakim said: The omission of the mention of sight with it in verses like His saying: 'They are deaf, dumb, blind' (al-Baqarah 18) and His saying: 'Nay, they are blind from it' (al-Naml) indicates the inclusion of sight in its concept; and its mention with it in verses like His saying: 'For indeed they are not the eyes that become blind' (al-Hajj 46) indicates its exclusion from it so that abstraction is not needed. End. His saying: with opposition between them in reality, for they are two opposites in the opposition of privation and possession.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) on the definition of blindness (al-'ama) and its implications for the three types of signification: mutabaqa (correspondence), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication). The commentator begins by explaining the phrase 'because it is the absence of sight from what is disposed to be seeing.' He clarifies that 'absence of sight' does not mean absolute absence, but rather absence that is specifically related to sight (al-'adam al-mudaf li al-basar). Thus the concept of blindness is composite: it has a material part (the absence itself) and a formal part (the relation of that absence to sight). Importantly, sight itself is not part of the concept of blindness; it is the object of the relation. This is because when we consider the 'added thing' (al-mudaf) in terms of its being added, the addition is included but the thing to which it is added (sight) is excluded. The signification of blindness upon the absence and the addition is by inclusion (tadammun), since each of these is a part of the concept, while its signification upon sight is by implication (iltizam), because sight is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the concept in the mind, even though in external reality blindness and sight are opposite (opposition of privation and possession). The passage then addresses a potential objection: someone might say that the common definition 'blindness is the absence of sight' implies that sight is part of the concept, which would make its signification by inclusion. The commentator refutes this by reiterating the analysis of the 'added thing' according to al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani. Next, al-Dawani is cited as providing a proof from common linguistic usage that sight is not part of the meaning of blindness, because we can attribute blindness to sight without a metaphorical indicator, as in Quranic verses: 'Their eyes are not blind' and 'He made their eyes blind.' The principle is that words are used literally, so if sight were part of the meaning, such attribution would require metaphor. The passage then transitions to a section heading 'On the definition of the three significations and their rulings,' but the discussion continues with the same example. Abu al-Fath objects that if al-Dawani's proof were correct, it would actually show that the restriction to sight is also outside the concept, making blindness mean absolute absence, which is false. He argues that the Quranic examples themselves contain an indicator (the attribution to sight) that shows the word is being used metonymically. Mir Zaid answers by distinguishing between the reality (haqiqa) of blindness (a simple quality consisting of a specific absence) and the conceptual designation ('unwan) that includes the restriction. Words are placed for realities, not designations, so the attribution is literal without metaphor. 'Abd al-Hakim supports this by noting that in some Quranic contexts 'blind' is used alone (implying sight is included) and in others it is explicitly linked to the eyes (implying sight is excluded). The passage concludes by noting the opposition between blindness and sight in reality, which is the opposition of privation and possession.

Key Terms

الإضافة
المضاف
المفهوم

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is sight not part of the concept of blindness?
A) Because blindness is absolute absence, and sight is unrelated.
B) Because when the added thing (absence) is taken as added, the addition is included but the thing added to (sight) is excluded.
C) Because sight is a necessary concomitant in the mind, not in reality.
D) Because the definition explicitly excludes sight.

Key Takeaway
The concept of blindness is composite: it includes absence and the relation to sight, but not sight itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the definition of 'sawm' (fasting) as 'abstaining from food, drink, and marital relations from dawn to sunset with intention'. Identify its material part (abstaining) and formal part (specific conditions of time and intention).
?
In the definition of 'zakat' as 'a specific portion of wealth given to specific categories at a specific time', determine whether the category of recipients is part of the concept (formal) or external (like sight in blindness).

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate

The Three Significations Applied to Blindness
Commentary analyzes the three significations using blindness as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the signification of a word as mutabaqa, tadammun, or iltizam using the example of blindness.

Core Idea
Blindness signifies its parts by inclusion and its opposite by implication.

After establishing the composite nature of blindness, the commentator explains how the word 'blindness' signifies its parts (absence and addition) by inclusion (tadammun) and sight by implication (iltizam). This is because sight is a necessary concomitant in the mind, even though in reality they are opposites. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing how words convey meaning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three types of signification
Signification by correspondence (mutabaqa) is the whole meaning; by inclusion (tadammun) is a part; by implication (iltizam) is something external but necessarily connected.
2
Identify the concept of blindness
Blindness is a composite concept: absence + addition to sight. It is the 'whole' meaning.
3
Determine signification upon absence and addition
Each is a part of the concept, so blindness signifies them by inclusion (tadammun).
4
Determine signification upon sight
Sight is not part of the concept, but it is necessarily concomitant in the mind—when we think of blindness, we think of sight. So signification is by implication (iltizam).
This is despite the fact that in reality they are opposites.
5
Understand the condition of opposition (mu'anada)
Blindness and sight are opposite in reality (privation vs. possession). Yet in the mind, sight is still a necessary concomitant of blindness. This shows that signification by implication does not require real connection, only mental association.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّه عدمُ البَصَرِ عمَّا من شأنه أن يكونَ بصيراً. (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّهُ عَدَمُ الْبَصَرِ ) أي : العدمُ المضافُ للبصر؛ لا مُطلق العدم، وحينئذٍ؛ فَمفهوم العمى مُركَّب من جزأين جزء مادّيّ، وهو العدم، وجزء صوري: وهو الإضافة، ويكون البصر خارجاً عن مفهوم العمى؛ لأنَّ المضاف إذا أخذ في المفهوم من حيثُ إِنَّهُ مُضاف؛ كانَتِ الإضافة داخلة فيه، والمضاف إليه خارجاً. وإذا أخذ من حيث ذاتُه ؛ كانَتِ الإضافةُ خارجة عن المفهومِ كَالمضاف إليه . وقد علمتَ أنَّ مفهوم العمى : هو العدمُ المضافُ للبصرِ مِن حيثُ إِنَّه مضاف، فتكونُ الإضافة للبصر داخلةً في مفهوم العمى، والبصر خارجاً عنه . وعلى هذا؛ فَدلالة العمى على كلِّ من العدمِ والإضافة؛ تَضمُّنيَّةٌ، وعلى البصر التزامية. (قَوْلُهُ : عَمَّا مِنْ شَأْنِهِ ... إلخ) أي شأن شخصه، فدخل فيه زيد الأعمى، وزيد الأكمه، والعقرب، فيتّصفُ جميعها بالعمى؛ لأنَّ شأنَ أشخاصِهَا أن تكونَ بصيرة، وخرج الحائط مثلاً ، فلا يتّصفُ بالعمى؛ لأنَّهُ ليسَ شأنُ شخصِها أن يكونَ بصيراً. العطار تتحقق دلالة الالتزام بدونه؛ فليس شرطاً فيها، ينتج : اللُّزوم الخارجي ليسَ شَرْطاً في دلالة الالتزام، لا يُقَال : العَمَى عدمُ البَصَرِ ، فيكونُ البَصَرُ جزء المفهوم؛ فتكونُ الدَّلالة تضمُّنيَّةٌ، وحاصِلُ الجوابِ ما حقَّقَهُ السَّيِّدُ أَنَّ المضاف إذا أُخِذَ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ مُضَافٌ؛ كانَتِ الإضافةُ داخِلَةٌ فيه، والمضافُ إليه خارِجَاً عنه، وإذا أُخِذَ مِن حيثُ ذاتُه ؛ كانَتِ الإضافة أيضاً خارجةٌ عَنْهُ ، ومفهومُ العَمّى هو العَدَمُ المضَافُ إلى البَصرِ مِنْ حيثُ هو مُضَافٌ، فتكونُ الإضافة إلى البصرِ داخلة في مفهوم العمى، ويكونُ البَصَرُ خَارِجَاً عنه .ا.هـ. وقد استدلَّ الدَّوَّاني على خروج البصرِ عَن مُسمَّى العمى بأنَّ إسناده إلى البصر شَائِعٌ بِدونِ قرينةٍ مَجازيَّةٍ ، قال تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَرُ وَلَكِن تَعْمَى الْقُلُوبُ الَّتِي فِي الصُّدُورِ ﴾ [الحج : ٤٦]، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَأَعْمَىٰ أَبْصَرَهُمْ ﴾ [محمد : ۲۳] إلى غيرِ ذَلِكَ مِنَ النَّظائرِ الشَّائعة، والأصْلُ الحقيقةُ ا.هـ. ، وقَوْلُهُ : بدون قرينة مجازيَّةٍ ؛ أي: بدونِ قرينة تدلُّ على أنَّ العَمَى المسند للبصر مجاز لغوي؛ بأن يذكر. فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها فيكون البصر لازماً للعمى في الذهن مع المعاندة بينهما في الخارج. (قَوْلُهُ: الْمُعَانَدَةِ) أي : المنافاة بينهما في الخارج، وحينئذٍ ; فَلَا مُلازمةً بينَهُما فيه . العطار لفظ العَمَى الموضوعُ لِلعَدَمِ مَع التَّقييدِ بِالبصرِ ، ويُرادُ مُطلَقُ العدم، ونقض دليلَهُ أبو الفتح بأنَّه لَو تمَّ؛ لَدَلَّ على أن يكونَ التَّقييدُ بالبصر أيضاً خارجاً عَنِ العَمَى؛ لأنَّه لو كانَ دَاخِلاً فيهِ؛ لم يصح إسنادُهُ لِلبَصَرِ بدونِ قرينةٍ مجازيَّةٍ؛ ضرورةً أن المسند إلى البصر هو العدمُ المطلقُ ; لا المقيَّدُ بِالبصرِ ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ العَمَى عبارةً عَن مُطلَقِ العَدَم ، وهو بَاطِلٌ قَطْعاً، والحقُّ أنَّا لا نسلّمُ صحة إسناده إلى البصر بدونِ قرينة مجازيَّة; إذ الأمثلة المذكورة مشتملة على القرينة وهِيَ نَفْسُ إسناده إلى البصر، وأمَّا قَوْلُهُ : والأصلُ الحقيقةُ ; فَفيهِ أنَّ الصَّارفَ عَنِ الحقيقةِ مَوجودٌ هَهُنَا ، وهُوَ لُزوم المجازيَّةِ باعتبارِ التَّقييدِ بالبصر ; سواء كانَ نَفْسُ البصر داخلاً فيه أو خارجاً عنه ١ هـ وأجاب مير زاهد بأنَّ المسند إلى البصرِ هُوَ نَفْسُ العَمَى، والنِّسبةُ لَيْسَتْ دَاخِلَةٌ فيهِ; بَل فيما يعبّر عنه، وإلا ; لَكانَ العَمَى أمراً نسبيًّا ، وقد اشتهر بينَهُم الفرقُ بينَ جُزء الشَّيء وجُزءٍ مَفهومه ; فالعَمَى صِفَةٌ بسيطةٌ قائمة بالأعمى، وحقيقتُهُ عَدَمٌ خاص يعبر عنهُ بِعَدَم البصر، فالتَّقييدُ بِهِ داخل في هذا المفهوم العنواني وخارج عن حقيقتِهِ البسيطةِ، وَلَمَّا كانَتِ الألفاظ موضوعةً لِلحقائقِ دونَ عنوانها ; كانَ دلالةُ العَمَى على البَصَرِ دَلالة على خارج عَنِ الموضوعِ لَهُ ، وكانَ إسناده إليه على سبيل الحقيقة من غَيْرِ تجريدٍ ومَجاز . .ا.هـ. وقال عبد الحكيم : تركُ ذِكْرِ البَصَرِ مَعَهُ فَي نَحْوِ قَولِهِ تعالى: ﴿هُم بُكُمُ عُمّى ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸]، وفي قوله تعالى : وبَلْ هُم مِّنْهَا عَمُونَ ﴾ [النمل: يدلُّ على دخول البصر في مفهومه، وذِكْرُهُ معه في نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ [الحج: ٤٦] ; يدلُّ على خُروجِهِ عَنْهُ كَي لا يحتاج إلى تجريد ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : مَعَ الْمُعَانَدَةِ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي الْخَارِجِ فَإِنَّهما متقابلانِ تَقابُلَ العَدَمِ والملكة.
Because it is the absence of sight from what is disposed to be seeing. His saying: 'Because it is the absence of sight' means: the absence added to sight, not absolute absence. Then the concept of blindness is composed of two parts: a material part, which is absence, and a formal part, which is the addition. And sight is outside the concept of blindness, because when the added thing is taken in the concept insofar as it is added, the addition is included in it and the thing to which it is added is outside. And if it is taken in terms of its essence, the addition is outside the concept like the thing to which it is added. And you have known that the concept of blindness is: the absence added to sight insofar as it is added, so the addition to sight is included in the concept of blindness, and sight is outside it. Based on this, the signification of blindness upon both absence and addition is by inclusion, and upon sight is by implication. His saying: 'from what is disposed' etc. means the disposition of its individual, so it includes Zayd the blind, Zayd the born blind, and the scorpion, all of them are described by blindness because the disposition of their individuals is to be seeing, and the wall, for example, is excluded, so it is not described by blindness because the disposition of its individual is not to be seeing. Al-'Attar: The signification by implication is realized without it; so it is not a condition in it. It results: external necessary concomitance is not a condition in signification by implication. It cannot be said: blindness is the absence of sight, so sight is part of the concept; then signification would be by inclusion. The summary of the answer is what al-Sayyid verified: that when the added thing is taken insofar as it is added, the addition is included in it and the thing added to it is outside it; and when taken in terms of its essence, the addition is also outside it. And the concept of blindness is the absence added to sight insofar as it is added, so the addition to sight is included in the concept of blindness, and sight is outside it. End. And al-Dawani argued for the exclusion of sight from the meaning of blindness by the fact that its attribution to sight is common without a metaphorical indicator. He said, the Exalted: 'For indeed they are not the eyes that become blind, but it is the hearts in the chests that become blind' (al-Hajj 46), and the Exalted: 'And He made their eyes blind' (Muhammad 23), and other common examples, and the principle is literalness. End. And his saying: without a metaphorical indicator means: without an indicator indicating that blindness attributed to sight is a linguistic metaphor, by mentioning. Section: On the definition of the three significations and their rulings. Then sight becomes a necessary concomitant of blindness in the mind while there is opposition between them in reality. His saying: 'opposition' meaning: mutual exclusion between them in reality, and then there is no necessary connection between them in reality. Al-'Attar: The word 'blindness' is placed for absence with restriction to sight, and absolute absence is intended. Abu al-Fath refuted his proof by saying that if it were valid, it would indicate that the restriction to sight is also outside of blindness; because if it were included in it, its attribution to sight would not be correct without a metaphorical indicator, necessarily because what is attributed to sight is absolute absence, not the absence restricted to sight. So it would follow that blindness is absolute absence, which is absolutely false. The truth is that we do not concede the correctness of its attribution to sight without a metaphorical indicator; because the mentioned examples include the indicator, which is the very attribution to sight. As for his saying: the principle is literalness; it is responded that the deterrent from literalness exists here, which is the necessity of metaphoricity due to the restriction to sight, whether sight itself is included in it or outside it. End. And Mir Zahid answered that what is attributed to sight is the very blindness, and the relation is not included in it; rather in what it expresses. Otherwise, blindness would be a relative matter. And the difference between the part of the thing and the part of its concept is well-known among them. So blindness is a simple quality subsisting in the blind person, and its reality is a specific absence expressed by 'absence of sight'. So the restriction to it is included in this conceptual designation and outside its simple reality. And since words are placed for realities, not for their designations, the signification of blindness upon sight is signification upon something outside the subject matter, and its attribution to it is literal without abstraction or metaphor. End. And 'Abd al-Hakim said: The omission of the mention of sight with it in verses like His saying: 'They are deaf, dumb, blind' (al-Baqarah 18) and His saying: 'Nay, they are blind from it' (al-Naml) indicates the inclusion of sight in its concept; and its mention with it in verses like His saying: 'For indeed they are not the eyes that become blind' (al-Hajj 46) indicates its exclusion from it so that abstraction is not needed. End. His saying: with opposition between them in reality, for they are two opposites in the opposition of privation and possession.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) on the definition of blindness (al-'ama) and its implications for the three types of signification: mutabaqa (correspondence), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication). The commentator begins by explaining the phrase 'because it is the absence of sight from what is disposed to be seeing.' He clarifies that 'absence of sight' does not mean absolute absence, but rather absence that is specifically related to sight (al-'adam al-mudaf li al-basar). Thus the concept of blindness is composite: it has a material part (the absence itself) and a formal part (the relation of that absence to sight). Importantly, sight itself is not part of the concept of blindness; it is the object of the relation. This is because when we consider the 'added thing' (al-mudaf) in terms of its being added, the addition is included but the thing to which it is added (sight) is excluded. The signification of blindness upon the absence and the addition is by inclusion (tadammun), since each of these is a part of the concept, while its signification upon sight is by implication (iltizam), because sight is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the concept in the mind, even though in external reality blindness and sight are opposite (opposition of privation and possession). The passage then addresses a potential objection: someone might say that the common definition 'blindness is the absence of sight' implies that sight is part of the concept, which would make its signification by inclusion. The commentator refutes this by reiterating the analysis of the 'added thing' according to al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani. Next, al-Dawani is cited as providing a proof from common linguistic usage that sight is not part of the meaning of blindness, because we can attribute blindness to sight without a metaphorical indicator, as in Quranic verses: 'Their eyes are not blind' and 'He made their eyes blind.' The principle is that words are used literally, so if sight were part of the meaning, such attribution would require metaphor. The passage then transitions to a section heading 'On the definition of the three significations and their rulings,' but the discussion continues with the same example. Abu al-Fath objects that if al-Dawani's proof were correct, it would actually show that the restriction to sight is also outside the concept, making blindness mean absolute absence, which is false. He argues that the Quranic examples themselves contain an indicator (the attribution to sight) that shows the word is being used metonymically. Mir Zaid answers by distinguishing between the reality (haqiqa) of blindness (a simple quality consisting of a specific absence) and the conceptual designation ('unwan) that includes the restriction. Words are placed for realities, not designations, so the attribution is literal without metaphor. 'Abd al-Hakim supports this by noting that in some Quranic contexts 'blind' is used alone (implying sight is included) and in others it is explicitly linked to the eyes (implying sight is excluded). The passage concludes by noting the opposition between blindness and sight in reality, which is the opposition of privation and possession.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does blindness signify sight by implication despite their opposition in reality?
A) Because blindness and sight are always found together.
B) Because the mind necessarily relates blindness to sight, even though they are opposites externally.
C) Because the definition includes sight.
D) Because signification by implication requires external connection, and they are connected in reality.

Key Takeaway
Signification by implication depends on mental connection, not real-world coexistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the word 'zakat' in the Quran. Identify what it signifies by correspondence (the obligatory charity), by inclusion (the specific amount or categories), and by implication (the purification of wealth).
?
For the word 'iman' (faith), determine its significations: by correspondence (belief in heart, speech, action), by inclusion (each pillar), and by implication (paradise).

What Is Mantiq?

12 min
The Linguistic Debate: Is Sight Part of the Meaning of Blindness?
Commentary includes a debate on whether sight is part of the meaning of blindness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the arguments for and against including sight in the concept of blindness, and evaluate the solution based on the distinction between reality and conceptual designation.

Core Idea
Al-Dawani's proof from common usage is countered by distinguishing the word's reality from its conceptual designation.

The passage includes a detailed debate among later scholars about whether sight is part of the literal meaning of 'blindness'. Al-Dawani uses Quranic verses to argue that sight is not part of the meaning. Abu al-Fath objects that his proof leads to absurdity. Mir Zahid resolves the issue by distinguishing the simple reality of blindness from its conceptual designation. This discussion demonstrates how linguistic analysis and logical principles interact in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
State al-Dawani's argument
Al-Dawani argues that sight is not part of the meaning of 'blindness' because we can correctly say 'the eyes are blind' without metaphor. If sight were part of the meaning, such attribution would be redundant or metaphorical.
He cites Quranic verses like 'their eyes are not blind'.
2
State Abu al-Fath's objection
Abu al-Fath claims that if al-Dawani's proof were valid, it would actually show that the restriction to sight is also outside the meaning, reducing blindness to absolute absence, which is false.
He argues that the Quranic examples themselves contain an indicator (the attribution itself) that shows metaphor.
3
Explain Mir Zahid's resolution
Mir Zahid distinguishes the reality (haqiqa) of blindness (a simple quality of specific absence) from its conceptual designation ('unwan) that includes the restriction. Words are placed for realities, not designations, so the attribution is literal.
Thus, sight is external to the reality but included in the conceptual analysis.
4
Understand 'Abd al-Hakim's support
'Abd al-Hakim notes that in some Quranic contexts, 'blind' is used alone (implying sight is included in the concept) and in others with 'eyes' (implying sight is excluded to avoid abstraction).
This shows that the inclusion or exclusion depends on context.
