لأنَّه عدمُ البَصَرِ عمَّا من شأنه أن يكونَ بصيراً. (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّهُ عَدَمُ الْبَصَرِ ) أي : العدمُ المضافُ للبصر؛ لا مُطلق العدم، وحينئذٍ؛ فَمفهوم العمى مُركَّب من جزأين جزء مادّيّ، وهو العدم، وجزء صوري: وهو الإضافة، ويكون البصر خارجاً عن مفهوم العمى؛ لأنَّ المضاف إذا أخذ في المفهوم من حيثُ إِنَّهُ مُضاف؛ كانَتِ الإضافة داخلة فيه، والمضاف إليه خارجاً. وإذا أخذ من حيث ذاتُه ؛ كانَتِ الإضافةُ خارجة عن المفهومِ كَالمضاف إليه . وقد علمتَ أنَّ مفهوم العمى : هو العدمُ المضافُ للبصرِ مِن حيثُ إِنَّه مضاف، فتكونُ الإضافة للبصر داخلةً في مفهوم العمى، والبصر خارجاً عنه . وعلى هذا؛ فَدلالة العمى على كلِّ من العدمِ والإضافة؛ تَضمُّنيَّةٌ، وعلى البصر التزامية. (قَوْلُهُ : عَمَّا مِنْ شَأْنِهِ ... إلخ) أي شأن شخصه، فدخل فيه زيد الأعمى، وزيد الأكمه، والعقرب، فيتّصفُ جميعها بالعمى؛ لأنَّ شأنَ أشخاصِهَا أن تكونَ بصيرة، وخرج الحائط مثلاً ، فلا يتّصفُ بالعمى؛ لأنَّهُ ليسَ شأنُ شخصِها أن يكونَ بصيراً. العطار تتحقق دلالة الالتزام بدونه؛ فليس شرطاً فيها، ينتج : اللُّزوم الخارجي ليسَ شَرْطاً في دلالة الالتزام، لا يُقَال : العَمَى عدمُ البَصَرِ ، فيكونُ البَصَرُ جزء المفهوم؛ فتكونُ الدَّلالة تضمُّنيَّةٌ، وحاصِلُ الجوابِ ما حقَّقَهُ السَّيِّدُ أَنَّ المضاف إذا أُخِذَ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ مُضَافٌ؛ كانَتِ الإضافةُ داخِلَةٌ فيه، والمضافُ إليه خارِجَاً عنه، وإذا أُخِذَ مِن حيثُ ذاتُه ؛ كانَتِ الإضافة أيضاً خارجةٌ عَنْهُ ، ومفهومُ العَمّى هو العَدَمُ المضَافُ إلى البَصرِ مِنْ حيثُ هو مُضَافٌ، فتكونُ الإضافة إلى البصرِ داخلة في مفهوم العمى، ويكونُ البَصَرُ خَارِجَاً عنه .ا.هـ. وقد استدلَّ الدَّوَّاني على خروج البصرِ عَن مُسمَّى العمى بأنَّ إسناده إلى البصر شَائِعٌ بِدونِ قرينةٍ مَجازيَّةٍ ، قال تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَرُ وَلَكِن تَعْمَى الْقُلُوبُ الَّتِي فِي الصُّدُورِ ﴾ [الحج : ٤٦]، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَأَعْمَىٰ أَبْصَرَهُمْ ﴾ [محمد : ۲۳] إلى غيرِ ذَلِكَ مِنَ النَّظائرِ الشَّائعة، والأصْلُ الحقيقةُ ا.هـ. ، وقَوْلُهُ : بدون قرينة مجازيَّةٍ ؛ أي: بدونِ قرينة تدلُّ على أنَّ العَمَى المسند للبصر مجاز لغوي؛ بأن يذكر. فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها فيكون البصر لازماً للعمى في الذهن مع المعاندة بينهما في الخارج. (قَوْلُهُ: الْمُعَانَدَةِ) أي : المنافاة بينهما في الخارج، وحينئذٍ ; فَلَا مُلازمةً بينَهُما فيه . العطار لفظ العَمَى الموضوعُ لِلعَدَمِ مَع التَّقييدِ بِالبصرِ ، ويُرادُ مُطلَقُ العدم، ونقض دليلَهُ أبو الفتح بأنَّه لَو تمَّ؛ لَدَلَّ على أن يكونَ التَّقييدُ بالبصر أيضاً خارجاً عَنِ العَمَى؛ لأنَّه لو كانَ دَاخِلاً فيهِ؛ لم يصح إسنادُهُ لِلبَصَرِ بدونِ قرينةٍ مجازيَّةٍ؛ ضرورةً أن المسند إلى البصر هو العدمُ المطلقُ ; لا المقيَّدُ بِالبصرِ ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ العَمَى عبارةً عَن مُطلَقِ العَدَم ، وهو بَاطِلٌ قَطْعاً، والحقُّ أنَّا لا نسلّمُ صحة إسناده إلى البصر بدونِ قرينة مجازيَّة; إذ الأمثلة المذكورة مشتملة على القرينة وهِيَ نَفْسُ إسناده إلى البصر، وأمَّا قَوْلُهُ : والأصلُ الحقيقةُ ; فَفيهِ أنَّ الصَّارفَ عَنِ الحقيقةِ مَوجودٌ هَهُنَا ، وهُوَ لُزوم المجازيَّةِ باعتبارِ التَّقييدِ بالبصر ; سواء كانَ نَفْسُ البصر داخلاً فيه أو خارجاً عنه ١ هـ وأجاب مير زاهد بأنَّ المسند إلى البصرِ هُوَ نَفْسُ العَمَى، والنِّسبةُ لَيْسَتْ دَاخِلَةٌ فيهِ; بَل فيما يعبّر عنه، وإلا ; لَكانَ العَمَى أمراً نسبيًّا ، وقد اشتهر بينَهُم الفرقُ بينَ جُزء الشَّيء وجُزءٍ مَفهومه ; فالعَمَى صِفَةٌ بسيطةٌ قائمة بالأعمى، وحقيقتُهُ عَدَمٌ خاص يعبر عنهُ بِعَدَم البصر، فالتَّقييدُ بِهِ داخل في هذا المفهوم العنواني وخارج عن حقيقتِهِ البسيطةِ، وَلَمَّا كانَتِ الألفاظ موضوعةً لِلحقائقِ دونَ عنوانها ; كانَ دلالةُ العَمَى على البَصَرِ دَلالة على خارج عَنِ الموضوعِ لَهُ ، وكانَ إسناده إليه على سبيل الحقيقة من غَيْرِ تجريدٍ ومَجاز . .ا.هـ. وقال عبد الحكيم : تركُ ذِكْرِ البَصَرِ مَعَهُ فَي نَحْوِ قَولِهِ تعالى: ﴿هُم بُكُمُ عُمّى ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸]، وفي قوله تعالى : وبَلْ هُم مِّنْهَا عَمُونَ ﴾ [النمل: يدلُّ على دخول البصر في مفهومه، وذِكْرُهُ معه في نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ [الحج: ٤٦] ; يدلُّ على خُروجِهِ عَنْهُ كَي لا يحتاج إلى تجريد ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : مَعَ الْمُعَانَدَةِ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي الْخَارِجِ فَإِنَّهما متقابلانِ تَقابُلَ العَدَمِ والملكة.
Because it is the absence of sight from what is disposed to be seeing. His saying: 'Because it is the absence of sight' means: the absence added to sight, not absolute absence. Then the concept of blindness is composed of two parts: a material part, which is absence, and a formal part, which is the addition. And sight is outside the concept of blindness, because when the added thing is taken in the concept insofar as it is added, the addition is included in it and the thing to which it is added is outside. And if it is taken in terms of its essence, the addition is outside the concept like the thing to which it is added. And you have known that the concept of blindness is: the absence added to sight insofar as it is added, so the addition to sight is included in the concept of blindness, and sight is outside it. Based on this, the signification of blindness upon both absence and addition is by inclusion, and upon sight is by implication. His saying: 'from what is disposed' etc. means the disposition of its individual, so it includes Zayd the blind, Zayd the born blind, and the scorpion, all of them are described by blindness because the disposition of their individuals is to be seeing, and the wall, for example, is excluded, so it is not described by blindness because the disposition of its individual is not to be seeing. Al-'Attar: The signification by implication is realized without it; so it is not a condition in it. It results: external necessary concomitance is not a condition in signification by implication. It cannot be said: blindness is the absence of sight, so sight is part of the concept; then signification would be by inclusion. The summary of the answer is what al-Sayyid verified: that when the added thing is taken insofar as it is added, the addition is included in it and the thing added to it is outside it; and when taken in terms of its essence, the addition is also outside it. And the concept of blindness is the absence added to sight insofar as it is added, so the addition to sight is included in the concept of blindness, and sight is outside it. End. And al-Dawani argued for the exclusion of sight from the meaning of blindness by the fact that its attribution to sight is common without a metaphorical indicator. He said, the Exalted: 'For indeed they are not the eyes that become blind, but it is the hearts in the chests that become blind' (al-Hajj 46), and the Exalted: 'And He made their eyes blind' (Muhammad 23), and other common examples, and the principle is literalness. End. And his saying: without a metaphorical indicator means: without an indicator indicating that blindness attributed to sight is a linguistic metaphor, by mentioning. Section: On the definition of the three significations and their rulings. Then sight becomes a necessary concomitant of blindness in the mind while there is opposition between them in reality. His saying: 'opposition' meaning: mutual exclusion between them in reality, and then there is no necessary connection between them in reality. Al-'Attar: The word 'blindness' is placed for absence with restriction to sight, and absolute absence is intended. Abu al-Fath refuted his proof by saying that if it were valid, it would indicate that the restriction to sight is also outside of blindness; because if it were included in it, its attribution to sight would not be correct without a metaphorical indicator, necessarily because what is attributed to sight is absolute absence, not the absence restricted to sight. So it would follow that blindness is absolute absence, which is absolutely false. The truth is that we do not concede the correctness of its attribution to sight without a metaphorical indicator; because the mentioned examples include the indicator, which is the very attribution to sight. As for his saying: the principle is literalness; it is responded that the deterrent from literalness exists here, which is the necessity of metaphoricity due to the restriction to sight, whether sight itself is included in it or outside it. End. And Mir Zahid answered that what is attributed to sight is the very blindness, and the relation is not included in it; rather in what it expresses. Otherwise, blindness would be a relative matter. And the difference between the part of the thing and the part of its concept is well-known among them. So blindness is a simple quality subsisting in the blind person, and its reality is a specific absence expressed by 'absence of sight'. So the restriction to it is included in this conceptual designation and outside its simple reality. And since words are placed for realities, not for their designations, the signification of blindness upon sight is signification upon something outside the subject matter, and its attribution to it is literal without abstraction or metaphor. End. And 'Abd al-Hakim said: The omission of the mention of sight with it in verses like His saying: 'They are deaf, dumb, blind' (al-Baqarah 18) and His saying: 'Nay, they are blind from it' (al-Naml) indicates the inclusion of sight in its concept; and its mention with it in verses like His saying: 'For indeed they are not the eyes that become blind' (al-Hajj 46) indicates its exclusion from it so that abstraction is not needed. End. His saying: with opposition between them in reality, for they are two opposites in the opposition of privation and possession.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) on the definition of blindness (al-'ama) and its implications for the three types of signification: mutabaqa (correspondence), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication). The commentator begins by explaining the phrase 'because it is the absence of sight from what is disposed to be seeing.' He clarifies that 'absence of sight' does not mean absolute absence, but rather absence that is specifically related to sight (al-'adam al-mudaf li al-basar). Thus the concept of blindness is composite: it has a material part (the absence itself) and a formal part (the relation of that absence to sight). Importantly, sight itself is not part of the concept of blindness; it is the object of the relation. This is because when we consider the 'added thing' (al-mudaf) in terms of its being added, the addition is included but the thing to which it is added (sight) is excluded. The signification of blindness upon the absence and the addition is by inclusion (tadammun), since each of these is a part of the concept, while its signification upon sight is by implication (iltizam), because sight is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the concept in the mind, even though in external reality blindness and sight are opposite (opposition of privation and possession). The passage then addresses a potential objection: someone might say that the common definition 'blindness is the absence of sight' implies that sight is part of the concept, which would make its signification by inclusion. The commentator refutes this by reiterating the analysis of the 'added thing' according to al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani. Next, al-Dawani is cited as providing a proof from common linguistic usage that sight is not part of the meaning of blindness, because we can attribute blindness to sight without a metaphorical indicator, as in Quranic verses: 'Their eyes are not blind' and 'He made their eyes blind.' The principle is that words are used literally, so if sight were part of the meaning, such attribution would require metaphor. The passage then transitions to a section heading 'On the definition of the three significations and their rulings,' but the discussion continues with the same example. Abu al-Fath objects that if al-Dawani's proof were correct, it would actually show that the restriction to sight is also outside the concept, making blindness mean absolute absence, which is false. He argues that the Quranic examples themselves contain an indicator (the attribution to sight) that shows the word is being used metonymically. Mir Zaid answers by distinguishing between the reality (haqiqa) of blindness (a simple quality consisting of a specific absence) and the conceptual designation ('unwan) that includes the restriction. Words are placed for realities, not designations, so the attribution is literal without metaphor. 'Abd al-Hakim supports this by noting that in some Quranic contexts 'blind' is used alone (implying sight is included) and in others it is explicitly linked to the eyes (implying sight is excluded). The passage concludes by noting the opposition between blindness and sight in reality, which is the opposition of privation and possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Mir Zahid, why is the attribution of blindness to eyes literal even though sight is not part of the simple reality of blindness?
A) Because words are placed for conceptual designations, and the designation includes sight.
B) Because blindness is a relative matter that includes sight by definition.
C) Because words are placed for realities, and the reality of blindness is a simple quality of specific absence, which can be attributed to eyes literally.
D) Because the Quranic examples force a metaphorical reading.

Key Takeaway
The simple reality of blindness (specific absence) is literal; the conceptual restriction to sight is part of the designation, not the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar debate for the term 'iman' (faith). Some argue that 'iman' in the Quran includes actions (based on verses like 'those who believe and do righteous deeds'), while others say actions are external. Use the reality/designation distinction to propose a resolution.
?
Analyze the phrase 'the hand of Allah is above their hands' (Quran 48:10). Using the methodology from this lesson, determine whether 'hand' should be taken literally as a part of Allah or as a metaphorical attribution.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
Customary Implication: Between Logic and Rhetoric
Commentary debates whether customary implication should be recognized as a type of signifier alongside rational implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the debate over customary implication and analyze how commentators refine the classification of signification.

Core Idea
Customary implication is a fault line between logical and rhetorical traditions.

In this passage, the commentator Al-Dasuqi and others debate whether customary implication (al-luzum al-'urfi) should be recognized as a valid type of signification in logic. The musannif appears to follow the scholars of Arabic rhetoric, leading to a critical refinement by later commentators who argue that the logicians reduce customary implication to rational implication. This lesson guides you through the key positions and objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Review the standard classification: correspondence (mutabaqah), inclusion (tadammun), and implication (iltizam). Implication is typically understood as rational (luzum 'aqli).
2
Locate the claim about customary implication
Find where the musannif appears to accept customary implication as a valid signifier, following the scholars of meanings (uluma' al-ma'ani).
The commentator says 'فَكَأَنَّ الْمُصَنِّفَ تَبِعَهُمْ' (so it is as if the author followed them).
3
Analyze the objections
Examine al-Dasuqi's note about negation by greater reason, and al-Attar's point about mixing logical and rhetorical terminologies (خلط اصطلاح باصطلاح).
Al-Attar suggests customary implication reduces to rational for logicians.
4
Evaluate the resolutions
Consider the alternative interpretations: al-Attar's reduction to rational implication, and al-Jalal's defense based on the stability of custom. Which resolution do you find most convincing?
Al-Jalal argues that differences in custom do not invalidate customary implication, just as differences in convention do not invalidate conventional signification.

Source Text

فَصْلٌ: في تعريف الدَّلالاتِ الثَّلاثِ وأحكامها
نَعَمْ؛ اعتبارُ اللُّزوم العرفي عند علماء المعاني، فكأنَّ المصنف تَبِعَهم . الدسوقي
زائد على عدم اعتبارِ اللُّزومِ العُرفيّ، وَلَا خفاءَ أَنَّه إذا انتفى اعتبارُ اللُّزوم بالمعنى الأعمّ ؛ انتفى اعتبارُ اللَّزومِ بالمعنى العُرفي بطريق الأولى (قَوْلُهُ : اعْتِبَارُ) مبتدأ، خبره قوله : (عند علماء . . . إلخ ) . (قَوْلُهُ: عِنْدَ عُلَمَاءِ الْمَعَانِي) أي: فَهُمْ يعتبرونه كما يعتبرونَ اللُّزوم العقلي؛ إذ لو لم يعتبر اللزوم العرفي؛ لخرج كثير من المجازات والكنايات المعتبرة في المخاطبات، وهو ما كانَ اللَّزومُ فِيهِ عُرفيًّا كَرَعَيْنَا الغيثَ ؛ أي: النبات في المجاز، وزيدٌ كثيرُ الرَّماد ؛ أي: كريم في الكناية .
(قَوْلُهُ : فَكَأَنَّ الْمُصَنِّفَ تَبِعَهُمْ فيه: أنَّ في تبعيَّتِهِ لهم خلط اصطلاح باصطلاح، فكيف يحملُ المصنِّفُ على تبعيَّته لهم؟ فالأَوْلَى أن يُقالَ: إِنَّ هذا الفنَّ في الأصل للفلاسفة، واللُّزوم العرفي عندَهُم راجع للعقلي؛ لأنَّ الأمور العادية مؤثرة عندَهُم، فيكون المصنِّفُ جارياً على هذا . العطار
صَرَّحَ بِهِ المصنِّفُ في المطوَّلِ، فَكَأَنَّهُ أرادَ بالامتناع في مجرى العادة؛ الامتناع في الجملة وفي بعض الأوقاتِ، وَلَو حُمِلَ الكَلامُ على ظَاهِرِهِ؛ يخرجُ كثيرٌ مِنَ الدَّلالاتِ المجازيَّةِ عَن الدَّلالةِ التّضمنيَّةِ والالتزاميَّةِ مَعَ تَصريحهم بانحصارها فيهما ا.هـ.
(قَوْلُهُ: فَكَأَنَّ الْمُصَنِّفَ تَبِعَهُمْ قالَ الجَلالُ : اختار المصنِّفُ مَذهب أهل العربيَّة؛ لأنَّه لا ريبة في فهم هذا المعنى، فَإسقاطهُ مِن درجةِ الاعتبارِ غير والعُذْرُ بالاختلافِ بِحَسبِ العادةِ غير مسموع، فإنَّ الوضعية أيضاً تَختَلِفُ باختلافِ الأوضاع ا.هـ.، يعني لو كان الاختِلافُ بِحسب العادةِ مُوجِبَاً لإسقاط اللُّزومِ العادي عن درجةِ الاعتبار ؛ لَكَانَ اختلافُ الأوضاع مُوجِباً لإسقاط الدَّلالةِ الوضعيَّةِ عَنْ درجةِ الاعتبار ا.هـ.، وقَالَ الهرويُّ : لَوِ اعتُبِرَ اللزومُ العَقِليُّ فَقَطْ ؛ لخرج المجازات والكنايات المعتبرة في المحاورات والمخاطباتِ، وَلَا شَكٍّ (قَوْلُهُ : فالأَوْلَى ... إلخ) سبق لك تحقيقه بما يغنيك عن هذا الجواب فراجعه ا . هـ . الشَّرنوبي .
Section: On the definition of the three types of signification and their rules. Yes; consideration of customary implication according to the scholars of meanings, so it is as if the author followed them. Al-Dasuqi: Additional to not considering customary implication, and it is not hidden that if consideration of implication in the broader sense is negated, then consideration of implication in the customary sense is negated by even greater reason. (His saying: 'Consideration') is a subject, its predicate is his saying: 'according to the scholars... etc.'. (His saying: 'according to the scholars of meanings') i.e., they consider it just as they consider rational implication; for if customary implication were not considered, many of the metaphors and metonymies considered in discourse would be excluded—that in which the implication is customary, like 'we grazed the rain' meaning the vegetation in metaphor, and 'Zayd has much ash' meaning generous in metonymy. (His saying: 'so it is as if the author followed them') there is in his following them a mixing of technical term with technical term; so how can the author be attributed to following them? The preferable is that it be said: This art originally belongs to the philosophers, and customary implication for them reduces to rational implication, because customary matters are effective for them, so the author is proceeding according to this. Al-Attar: The author explicitly stated in al-Mutawwal that it is as if he intended by 'impossibility in the usual course' impossibility in general and in some times, and if the words are taken literally, many metaphorical significations would be excluded from inclusive and implicative signification, despite their declaration that they are restricted to them. (His saying: 'so it is as if the author followed them') Al-Jalal said: The author chose the school of the Arab grammarians because there is no doubt in understanding this meaning; so dropping it from the degree of consideration is, and the excuse of difference according to custom is not heard, for conventional (wad'iyyah) signification also differs with difference of conventions. Meaning: if difference according to custom necessitates dropping customary implication from the degree of consideration, then difference of conventions would necessitate dropping conventional signification from the degree of consideration. Al-Harawi said: If only rational implication were considered, then the metaphors and metonymies considered in dialogues and discourses would be excluded, and there is no doubt. (His saying: 'the preferable... etc.') Your investigation has already preceded that which suffices you from this answer, so refer to it. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the issue of whether customary implication (al-luzum al-'urfi) should be accepted as a valid type of signification in logic. The author (musannif) is said to have followed the scholars of Arabic rhetoric (uluma' al-ma'ani) in considering customary implication, which the commentator(s) critique as mixing logical and rhetorical terminologies. The passage begins by affirming that customary implication is considered by the scholars of meanings, as it is needed for metaphors and metonymies. Al-Dasuqi notes that if the broader sense of implication is negated, then the narrower customary sense is negated a fortiori. Then the commentary explains that if customary implication were not recognized, many valid figures of speech would be excluded from the categories of inclusive and implicative signification, which contradicts the logicians' claim that all signification is either inclusive or implicative. Al-Attar suggests that the author's position can be interpreted as reducing customary implication to rational implication, since the philosophers consider customary matters as effectively necessary. Al-Jalal defends the author by arguing that the Arab grammarians' position is sound and that differences in custom do not justify discarding customary implication, just as differences in convention do not discard conventional signification. Al-Harawi adds that rejecting customary implication would exclude many valid metaphors and metonymies. The passage shows the tension between logical and rhetorical perspectives on signification.

اللزوم العرفي
اللزوم العقلي
الدلالات الثلاث

Key Takeaway
The debate over customary implication reveals the ongoing negotiation between logical exactitude and rhetorical flexibility in the Islamic scholarly tradition.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
The Three Significations and Non-Implication between Tadamun and Iltizam
Author defines three significations and refutes mutual implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of signification and demonstrate why tadamun does not imply iltizam and vice versa using examples from Islamic concepts.

Core Idea
Tadamun and iltizam are independent: a part does not necessitate a mental attendant, and a simple can have an external concomitant.

In this lesson, we delve into the three classical divisions of signification: correspondence, inclusion, and implication. The passage establishes a critical distinction: tadamun (inclusion) does not necessitate iltizam (implication) in the mind, and vice versa. We will unpack this claim by examining definitions, examples from the tradition, and the reasoning behind the non-implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Find the definitions in the passage: mutabaqa (correspondence to the whole meaning), tadamun (signifying a part), and iltizam (signifying an external necessary concomitant). Note that iltizam is defined as a necessary concomitant such that from conceiving the meaning, its conception follows without stipulation.
The commentator specifies that this is a 'lazim bayyin bi-l-ma'na al-akhass' (evident concomitant in the specific sense).
2
Grasp the key claim of non-implication
The passage asserts: 'tadamun does not necessitate iltizam, and vice versa.' This means that just because a word signifies a part of its meaning (tadamun), it does not automatically carry an external implication; similarly, a word may have an external implication without including that implication as a part of its meaning.
The commentator clarifies this is about mental conception, not real existence. In reality, everything has some necessary concomitant, but that may not be of the specific type required for iltizam.
3
Analyze the supporting examples
Examine the example of the dot (nuqta): a simple concept that has external concomitants like indivisibility and being posited, which are not part of its essence. This shows iltizam without tadamun. Conversely, a composite meaning like a specific whole may have parts (tadamun) but no external mental concomitant, showing tadamun without iltizam.
The passage also notes that definitions of simples are descriptions (rusum), not real definitions, due to lack of composition.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic concept
Choose an Islamic concept, e.g., 'ṣalāh' (prayer). Mutabaqa: the word signifies the entire act of prayer. Tadamun: it signifies specific components like bowing (rukū'). Iltizam: the word may imply the intention (niyyah) as a necessary condition external to the literal meaning.
Think about whether the part (tadamun) necessarily implies the external condition (iltizam). According to the passage, it does not in the mind.

(قوله: واعلم أن التضمن... إلخ) تبرع من الشارح على ما في المثن، والمصنف تركهما؛ لعلمهما بالمقايسة، كما قال الجلال: لم يتعرض لحال التضمن والالتزام في الاستلزام وعدم إحالته إلى فهم المتعلم، فإنه كما يجوز بسيط لا لازم له؛ يجوز مركب كذلك، ويجوز أيضاً بسيط له لازم، قال أبو الفتح بعد أن قدح في أدلة الاستلزام بينهما: والحق أن استلزام شيء من التضمن والالتزام للآخر غير معلوم وجوداً وعدماً، كما أن استلزام المطابقة للالتزام غير معلوم؛ فالأولى توجيه الاقتصار على بيان حال المطابقة مع التضمن والالتزام من اللزوم، وعدم الاستلزام وترك التعرض لحال أحدهما مع الآخر لعدم الاهتمام بشأنهما لفرعيتهما؛ بخلاف المطابقة لأصالتها أو لكونهما مهجورين في الجملة، كما اشتهر فيما بينهم بخلافها ا.هـ. Chapter on the definition of the three significations and their rulings. A necessary concomitant such that from the conception of the meaning its conception follows without stipulation. And know that tadamun (inclusion) does not necessitate iltizam (implication), and vice versa. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'A necessary concomitant such that... etc.') meaning: a necessary concomitant characterized by this state; i.e., an evident necessary concomitant in the specific sense. (His saying: 'does not necessitate iltizam') meaning: in the mind, but in reality; there must be for every thing a necessary concomitant; because it is either necessary existence, and His attributes of power... etc., or al-'Attar: what we transmitted in the commentary of the original is the lack of knowledge of the implication, in agreement with the people; not the knowledge of the lack of implication derived from the context of the commentator; reflect. Al-Sayyid said: Among them is one who argued for the lack of implication that we are absolutely certain of the permissibility of conceiving some meanings while abstracting from all others, so there occurs correspondence without implication. If that is correct, then the claim of lack of implication is established. He only said 'if that is correct' because it is an argument by intuition; the fair person acknowledges it when he returns to his intuition, but the obstinate denies it and says: we do not concede the occurrence of abstraction from all others; what occurs is abstraction from awareness, and that does not necessitate the absence of awareness. Abd al-Hakim said: One may argue for the lack of implication: when taken such that nothing escapes it; then there is correspondence and no mental concomitant, otherwise the contrary of the assumption would follow. But there is in it that that expression is characterized by infinitude and by that nothing escapes it, and each of these is external to it due to its being characterized by them, so the signification of the expression put for it upon it is that all quiddities are mental implications, and its entrance into it does not contradict considering that it is a concept among concepts; so ponder. (His saying: And know that tadamun... etc.) is a gratuity from the commentator on what is in the text, and the author left them out due to their knowledge of the analogy, as al-Jalal said: He did not address the state of tadamun and iltizam in implication and its lack, leaving it to the understanding of the learner. For just as a simple [concept] without a necessary concomitant is possible, so too a composite, and also a simple with a necessary concomitant is possible. Abu al-Fath said, after criticizing the proofs of implication between them: The truth is that the implication of one of tadamun and iltizam to the other is unknown in existence and non-existence, just as the implication of correspondence to iltizam is unknown. So it is better to restrict the explanation to the state of correspondence with tadamun and iltizam regarding necessity and non-necessity, and leave addressing the state of one with the other due to lack of interest in their matter due to their being subsidiary, unlike correspondence due to its originality or because they are generally abandoned, as is well-known among them unlike it. (His saying: 'For it is possible that... etc.') This is a mental possibility based on what we established earlier, and what follows it is actual, for the dot is a simple meaning, and indivisibility is external to its quiddity, otherwise it would be a non-existent thing and it would have no evident necessary concomitant in the specific sense, so they took it in its definition and also its being a posited thing, and similar is said about unity and all simples, hence they said: their definitions are descriptions, not definitions, due to the lack of composition in them. (His saying: 'For it is possible that among composite meanings there is what has no mental necessary concomitant, so there is tadamun without iltizam. And as for the second: it is possible that a simple meaning has a mental necessary concomitant, so there is iltizam without tadamun.' Al-Dasuqi: Created, and it is either an accident; it necessitates being in a subject, or a substance; it necessitates occupying space, or a mental consideration; it necessitates that it is other than other things. This is based on the assumption that the general necessity is considered, otherwise there may be no specific necessary concomitant for anything. (His saying: 'For it is possible... etc.') He expressed with possibility to indicate that this is something mentally possible even if no example exists for it, because mere possibility does not necessitate existence. And the statement of al-Fakhr: The existence of correspondence without implicative signification cannot be achieved, because the meaning of every expression necessitates otherness from others. So in it: That mentioned otherness is an evident necessary concomitant in the general sense, and it is not considered in implicative signification, yes on the view of its consideration, what he said holds. Al-'Attar: And the implication to the other is unknown in existence and non-existence, just as the implication of correspondence to iltizam is unknown. So it is better to restrict the explanation to the state of correspondence with tadamun and iltizam regarding necessity and non-necessity, and leave addressing the state of one with the other due to lack of interest in their matter due to their being subsidiary, unlike correspondence due to its originality or because they are generally abandoned, as is well-known among them unlike it. (His saying: 'For it is possible that... etc.') This is a mental possibility based on what we established earlier, and what follows it is actual, for the dot is a simple meaning, and indivisibility is external to its quiddity, otherwise it would be a non-existent thing and it would have no evident necessary concomitant in the specific sense, so they took it in its definition and also its being a posited thing, and similar is said about unity and all simples, hence they said: their definitions are descriptions, not definitions, due to the lack of composition in them.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative commentary on the three types of signification: correspondence (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadamun), and implication (iltizam). The author begins by defining the third type, iltizam, as a necessary concomitant such that from the mere conception of the meaning, the conception of the concomitant follows without the need to stipulate it separately. He then asserts the key claim that tadamun does not entail iltizam and vice versa. The commentator, al-Dasuqi, explains that this non-implication is in the mind; in reality, everything has some necessary concomitant, but not necessarily of the specific kind required for iltizam. He then engages with various scholars: al-'Attar clarifies that the claim is about lack of knowledge of implication, not knowledge of lack of implication. Al-Sayyid argues that we can conceive a meaning while abstracting from all others, thus correspondence occurs without implication, but this is contested by the obstinate who claim we only abstract from awareness, not from existence. Abd al-Hakim adds that if we consider a concept that encompasses everything, then that concept has correspondence but no mental concomitant, because any concomitant would be external. However, this leads to the point that all quiddities are mental implications. The commentator then notes that the author of the matn left out discussion of the relationship between tadamun and iltizam, assuming the learner would infer it by analogy. Abu al-Fath argues that the implication between tadamun and iltizam is unknown, so it is better to focus on the primary relationship of correspondence with the other two. The passage then gives concrete examples: a composite meaning may have no mental concomitant (tadamun without iltizam), and a simple meaning like the dot 'nuqta' has necessary concomitants like indivisibility and being a posited thing, which are external to its essence, thus iltizam without tadamun. Al-Dasuqi expands on the dot example, noting that its definition is a description (rasm) not a real definition (hadd) because it has no composition. Finally, al-Fakhr al-Razi's objection is addressed: he claimed correspondence cannot occur without implication because every meaning entails otherness; but the commentator replies that otherness is only a necessary concomitant in the general sense, not the specific sense required for iltizam. Thus, the passage systematically establishes the independence of tadamun and iltizam through conceptual analysis and examples.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the example of the 'dot' (nuqta) illustrate iltizam without tadamun?
A) The dot is a composite meaning with many parts, so it includes tadamun.
B) The dot is a simple concept; its indivisibility is external to its quiddity, so it has an external concomitant but no part-signification.
C) The dot implies its definition, which includes tadamun.
D) The dot has no necessary concomitant at all.

Key Takeaway
Tadamun and iltizam are logically independent: a part of a meaning need not have an external mental concomitant, and a simple meaning can have an external concomitant without containing it as a part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the Islamic term 'ṣiyām' (fasting). Identify an instance of mutabaqa, tadamun, and iltizam for this term. Then explain whether the tadamun (e.g., abstaining from food) implies iltizam (e.g., intention) in the mind, according to the passage's claim.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner

Compound and Simple Words: The Foundational Division
The author defines compound and simple words, and discusses the conditions for a word to be considered compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between compound and simple words according to the logicians' criteria, and identify which expressions count as true compounds.

Core Idea
A compound word's parts each contribute meaning; a simple word's parts do not.

In logic, before analyzing definitions and arguments, we must understand how words signify. The fundamental division of words into compound (murakkab) and simple (mufrad) is the first step. This lesson unpacks the criteria for compound words as explained by the commentators Dusuqi and Attar on a classic logic text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core division
The author divides all words placed for meaning into compound or simple. Note that 'placed' (موضوع) means intentionally assigned to a meaning.
2
Understand 'placement by conformance'
Placement by conformance (وضع بالمطابقة) means the whole word indicates its whole meaning. Only such words are subject to this division.
3
Learn the condition for a true compound
A compound's parts must each be placed for a meaning, and the whole meaning is a combination of these part-meanings. Example: 'رامي الحجارة' (thrower of stones) where each word has its own meaning.
4
Recognize exclusions
Expressions like verbal repetition ('زيد زيد'), synonymous pairs, and composites with meaningless parts are not true compounds because they lack the requisite combination of independently meaningful parts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مباحثِ الألفاظ
(وَ) اللَّفظ (الموْضُوعُ) للمعنى بالمطابقة إِمَّا مركَّب، أو مُفرد.
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: وَاللَّفْظُ الْمَوْضُوْعُ قدَّرَ اللَّفظَ دونَ الدَّالِّ؛ لأَنَّهُ هُوَ الَّذِي يوصفُ بالمركَبِ والمفرد، ولأنَّ الكلام في دلالة الألفاظ ، وقوله : الموضوع؛ أي: وضعاً شخصيًّا (١) أو نوعيا كالمجاز .
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : الْمَوْضُوْعُ لِلْمَعْنَى) وذلكَ بِأنْ يوضَعَ عينُ اللَّفْظِ لِعَينِ المعنى كَمَا فِي وَضْعِ الإنسانِ لِلحيوانِ النَّاطِقِ ؛ شخصيًّا كانَ الوضعُ كالمثال المذكور، أو نوعيًّا كَمَا في المشتقَاتِ، أو بِوَضْع الأجزاء للأجزاء كَمَا في رامي الحجارة وزيدٌ قائم ؛ فإِنَّ الجزء الأَوَّلَ مَوضوع لمعنى، والجزء الثاني لمعنى آخر، فإِذا أُخِذَ مَجموع المعنَيَيْنِ كانَ مجموعُ اللَّفْظِ موضوعاً لمجموع المعنى ؛ لا وضعُ عينِ اللَّفْظِ لِعَينِ المعنى ؛ بل وَضْعُ أجزائِهِ لأجزائِهِ، فَلِلمركَبِ مِن حيثُ التَّركيبُ وَضْعُ بِاعتبارِهِ يدخلُ في الدَّالِّ بالمطابقة، وهو وَضْعُ أجزائِهِ لِمعناه، وأمَّا الوضعُ النَّوعيُّ لِلمركَّب باعتبارِ الهيئةِ ؛ فَلَا مدخلَ لَهُ في التَّركيب والإفراد، فإنَّ المعتبر فيهما الأجزاء المرتَّبَةُ في السَّمع، فخرج التقسيم الموضوعات الغير اللفظية والألفاظ المهملة والمركَّبُ مِنَ الموضوع والمهملُ ، فَإِنَّ ذلكَ ليسَ مُركَّباً ؛ بَلْ ضَمُّ مُهمل إلى مُستعمل، وخرجَ أيضاً اللَّفْظَانِ المترادِفَانِ ؛ لأنَّ كُلَّ واحدٍ منهما يدلُّ على ما يدلُّ عليهِ الآخَرُ ، وعطفُ البيانِ مَعَ مَعطوفِهِ والتَّأكيدُ اللَّفظيُّ؛ كَزيدٍ ،زيدٍ وقرأتُ الكتابَ بَاباً بَاباً ؛ لانتفاء التركيب، فَمَا ذُكِرَ مِن حيثُ المعنى إِنَّمَا التَّركيب مِن حيثُ اللَّفْظُ لِفائدةِ التَّأكيد أو التَّفصيل أو
عَنِ
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : وضعاً شخصيَّاً ... إلخ) الفرق بين الوضع الشخصي والنوعي أن الأوَّل يقصد به تشخص الموضوع سواء كان الموضوع له مشخصاً كزيد أو كلياً كإنسان. والثاني لا يقصد به تشخص الموضوع بل يعمد الواضع إلى أمر كلي يندرج تحته أشياء كثيرة كقوله : وضعت كلّ ما كان على وزن فاعل أو مفعول للدلالة على ذات وقع منها أو عليها الحدث، ويندرج تحت تلك القاعدة ضارب ومضروب وقاتل و مقتول وهكذا . ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Chapter: On discussions of words
And the word placed for the meaning by conformance is either compound or simple.
Dusuqi: His statement: 'and the word placed' he estimated 'word' without 'indicating' because it is what is described as compound and simple, and because the discussion is about the signification of words. And his statement: 'placed' means personally or typically, like metaphor.
Attar: His statement: 'placed for the meaning' that is by placing the exact word for the exact meaning, as in placing 'human' for 'rational animal'; personally as in the mentioned example, or typically as in derived words, or by placing parts for parts as in 'thrower of stones' and 'Zaid standing'; for the first part is placed for a meaning, and the second part for another meaning, so if the two meanings are taken together, the total word is placed for the total meaning – not the placing of the exact word for the exact meaning, but the placing of its parts for its parts. So the compound has a placing from the aspect of composition, by which it enters into the indicating by conformance, and it is the placing of its parts for its meaning. As for the typical placing for the compound by consideration of the form, it has no entry in composition and singularity, for what is considered in them is the parts arranged in hearing. So the division excludes non-linguistic objects, meaningless words, and the composite of a placed word and a meaningless one, for that is not compound; rather it is an appendage of a meaningless to a used word. Also excluded are two synonymous words, because each one indicates what the other indicates. And explanatory apposition with its apposited, and verbal emphasis like 'Zaid, Zaid' and 'I read the book, chapter by chapter' due to lack of composition. For what is mentioned is in terms of meaning, the composition is in terms of the word for the benefit of emphasis or detail or...
Footnote: (His statement: 'personally or typically...') The difference between personal and typical placement is that the first intends the specification of the placed thing, whether the thing placed for is particular like Zaid or universal like human. The second does not intend the specification of the placed thing; rather the positer intends a universal thing under which many things fall, like his saying: 'I placed everything on the pattern of fā‘il or maf‘ūl to indicate an entity from which or on which the event occurs,' and under that rule fall ḍārib, maḍrūb, qātil, maqtūl, etc. – Sharunubi.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section (فصل) on discussions of words. It states the fundamental division: every word that is placed for a meaning by conformance (مطابقة) is either compound (مركب) or simple (مفرد). Dusuqi explains that the author used 'word' rather than 'indicator' because it is the word itself that is described as compound or simple. He also clarifies that 'placed' can be either personal (وضع شخصي) or typical (وضع نوعي), with metaphor as an example of typical placement. Attar then expands on what it means for a word to be placed for a meaning. He gives examples: personal placement like 'human' for 'rational animal', typical placement like derived words (مشتقات), and placement by parts for parts (وضع الأجزاء للأجزاء). In this last type, the compound word's total meaning arises from the combination of its parts' meanings, as in 'thrower of stones' (رامي الحجارة) where each part contributes a meaning. Importantly, he clarifies that for a word to be considered compound, the placement must be of its parts for their respective meanings; typical placement by form (هيئة) does not enter into the division. Attar then excludes various things that are not truly compound: non-linguistic objects, meaningless words, composites of a meaningful and a meaningless word (since not all parts are placed), synonymous words (since each indicates the same meaning, no combination), explanatory apposition, and verbal repetition. These are not true composition because the combination does not produce a new meaning from parts. The footnote elaborates on the difference between personal and typical placement: personal placement specifies a particular word for a particular meaning (e.g., Zaid for that individual), while typical placement sets a rule for a class of words (e.g., all words on the pattern fā‘il indicate a doer). This is foundational for understanding how words signify in logic.

Key Terms
مركب
مفرد
وضع شخصي
وضع نوعي
دلالة المطابقة

Key Takeaway
The fundamental division of words into compound and simple, with the crucial condition that a compound's parts each carry their own meaning, is the first step in analyzing logical expressions and definitions.

Conditions for a Word to Be Compound
Commentators define compound word and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given Arabic word qualifies as compound (murakkab) by applying the conditions of intention, conventional usage, and sequential hearing.

Core Idea
A word is compound only if the speaker intends a part to indicate part of the meaning, in line with conventional usage, and the parts are heard sequentially.

In Islamic logic, distinguishing between simple and compound words is foundational for understanding definitions and the structure of propositions. This lesson unpacks the precise conditions that make a word compound, as explained by the classical commentators. You will learn to apply these conditions to real examples from Arabic and Islamic terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of murakkab
Find in the passage the core definition: a word where part of the word indicates part of the intended meaning, with specific conditions. Note that the commentators are expanding on a matn (base text).
2
Understand the intention condition
The intention (qasd) must be according to conventional usage (wad‘). The example of using the letter 'z' from 'Zayd' to mean his head violates this because it is not in line with the established language.
3
Understand the sequential hearing condition
The parts must be heard sequentially in time, not simultaneously. The example 'daraba' has two aspects (material and formal) but they are heard at once, so it is not a compound word.
4
Apply to proper nouns like 'Abdullah'
When 'Abdullah' is used as a proper name, the speaker intends it as a single unit, not as parts. Thus, despite its etymological composition, it is treated as simple (mufrad) because the intention is not for parts to indicate separate meanings.
This shows the importance of the intention condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مباحثِ الألفاظِ. (قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ قُصِدَ . . . إلخ) أي : قصداً جارياً على قانون الوضع، فخرج ما إذا قصد بالزاي من زيدٍ الدَّلالة على عضو من أعضائِهِ كَرَأسِهِ؛ فلا يكونُ مُركَّباً بهذا القصد؛ لأنَّهُ مُخالفٌ لِقانون الوضع. (قَوْلُهُ : بِجُزءٍ مِنْهُ). . . ثمَّ قَالَ : وَلَا تُصْعَ إلى مَا قِيْلَ. والمراد القصد الجاري على قانونِ الوضْعِ كَمَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ شارحُ المطالع، فَلَا يردُ أنَّ نحو زيدٍ إذا قُصِدَ بِجزء منهُ الدَّلالة على جزء معناه؛ لأنَّهُ على خلافِ قانون الوضع. والمراد بقصدِ الدَّلالةِ أنْ تُعتبر تلكَ الدَّلالة في إفادة المعنى المقصودِ مِنَ اللَّفْظ؛ سواء كانَ المفاد صحيحاً أو بَاطِلاً. . . ثمَّ المراد الأجزاء المترتبةٌ في السَّمْعِ؛ بأن يُسمع أحد الجزأين قبلَ الآخَرِ، فَلَا ينتقضُ التَّعريفُ بِنَحوِ ضربَ؛ فَإِنَّهُ يَدلُّ باعتبار جزيْهِ المادّيّ على الحدثِ، والصُّوري على الزمان والنِّسبة، فإنَّ الجزأين يُسمَعانِ معاً. ولكَ أنْ تقول : إنَّ المقصود من نحو ضرب دلالة المادَّةِ والصُّورةِ على مجموع المعنى؛ لا دلالة الجزء على الجزء، فَلا نقض. (قَوْلُهُ: المَقْصُوْدِ) هذا القيد أشعرَ بِهِ كلامُ المصنّفِ، ومخرج لمثل عبدِ اللهِ علماً. وأما الكلام في أن القصد ضروري، والاكتفاء بالحيثية لا يدفع اجتماع الإفراد والتركيب في مثل عبد الله.
Section: On Investigations of Words. (His saying: 'If it is intended...' etc.) Meaning: an intention proceeding according to the law of conventional usage. Thus excluded is the case where by the letter 'z' in 'Zayd' one intends to indicate a part of his body like his head; it would not be compound with this intention because it violates the law of conventional usage. (His saying: 'with a part of it'.) Then he said: 'And do not pay heed to what was said'. The intended meaning is the intention proceeding according to the law of conventional usage, as the commentator on al-Mutala' stated. So the objection that something like 'Zayd' if part of it is intended to indicate part of its meaning is not valid because it is contrary to the law of conventional usage. The meaning of 'intention of indication' is that that indication is considered in conveying the intended meaning from the word, whether the conveyed meaning is correct or false. Then the intended parts are those that are sequential in hearing; such that one part is heard before another, so the definition is not undermined by something like 'daraba' (he hit); for it indicates by its material part the event, and by its formal part the tense and relation, [but] the two parts are heard simultaneously. And you may say: The intended from something like 'daraba' is the indication of the material and form on the total meaning; not the indication of the part on the part; so no objection. (His saying: 'the intended') This condition was indicated by the author's speech, and it excludes something like 'Abdullah' as a proper name. As for the discussion that intention is necessary, and relying solely on the aspect does not prevent the simultaneous occurrence of being simple and compound in something like 'Abdullah'.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of a compound word (murakkab) as presented in a text on logic. The commentators, likely al-Dasuqi and al-‘Attar, are expanding on the conditions that make a word compound. They begin by stating that for a word to be compound, the speaker must intend to indicate a part of the meaning by a part of the word, and this intention must be in accordance with conventional usage (wad‘). An example violating this: if one uses the letter ‘z’ from the word ‘Zayd’ to refer to Zayd’s head, that is not legitimate compounding because conventional usage does not assign that meaning to that letter. They then address a potential objection: that the definition might incorrectly include words like ‘Zayd’ if one intends a part of it to indicate a part of the meaning. But they clarify that such an intention would be contrary to conventional usage, so it is excluded. Next, they discuss the condition that the parts of the compound must be heard sequentially in time. The example ‘daraba’ (he hit) is given: it has two parts (the root ‘d-r-b’ and the pattern indicating tense and person), but these parts are heard simultaneously, not one after another, so it is not a compound. Alternatively, one can argue that in ‘daraba’, the parts are not intended to indicate separate parts of the meaning; rather, the whole indicates the entire event. Finally, they discuss the condition of the intention (qasd) itself, showing its necessity to exclude proper nouns like ‘Abdullah’ (lit. ‘servant of Allah’) which, if considered etymologically, would be compound, but as a proper name, it is intended as a simple unit referring to a person. The commentators argue that without the intention condition, one cannot resolve the fact that such a word is both simple (as a name) and compound (from its components) simultaneously. The differing views on whether one can rely solely on the aspect (al-haythiyya) or need the intention condition are presented, with the conclusion that the intention condition is necessary.

قصد
جزء
دلالة
وضع

Key Takeaway
A word is compound only if the speaker intends a part to indicate part of the meaning, the intention follows conventional usage, and the parts are heard sequentially.

The Four Types of Singular Expressions
Author defines four types of singular expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any singular expression into one of the four divisions based on its parts and their signification.

Core Idea
A singular expression is defined by meaning, not word count.

In logic, the classification of expressions is based on meaning, not just grammar. The author divides singular expressions into four types depending on whether they have parts and whether those parts signify something related to the intended meaning. This distinction is critical for analyzing definitions and understanding how proper names work in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Memorize the four divisions: (1) has no parts, (2) has parts but no signification, (3) parts signify something other than the intended meaning, (4) parts signify part of the intended meaning but not intentionally.
Categories 3 and 4 are the most nuanced.
2
Determine if the expression has parts
Ask: Is the expression composed of smaller units (e.g., words or meaningful syllables)? If not, it is category 1.
3
Check if the parts signify anything
If it has parts, do any of them carry independent meanings? If not, category 2.
4
Evaluate what the parts signify relative to the whole
If parts signify something, is that something part of the intended meaning of the whole expression? If not, category 3; if yes, category 4.
Intended meaning is what the expression primarily denotes.
5
Apply to examples from the passage
Test with 'Abdullah' and 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq'. In 'Abdullah', 'abd' and 'Allah' signify servant and God, but the name refers to a person, so category 3. In 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq', 'hayawan' signifies animal, which is part of the human essence, so category 4.
The difference lies in whether the part's meaning is part of the essence of the referent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مباحثِ الألفاظ. فالمفرد أربعة أقسام: ما لا جزء له أصلاً، وما له جزء لا دلالة له، وما له جزء يدل على غير جزء المعنى المقصودِ، وما له جزء يدل على جزء المعنى المقصودِ لكن دلالته عليه غير مقصودة. فإن قلتَ: ما الفرق بين القسمين الأخيرين؟ قلتُ: الفرق أن «عبد الله» العَلم لا يدل جزء لفظه على جزء المعنى المقصود؛ إذ ليس شيء من الجزأين دالا على شيء من الذات المشخصة. وأما «الحيوان الناطق» عَلَماً فيدل جزء لفظه على جزء المعنى المقصود، لكن تلك الدلالة ليست بمقصودة. بيانه: أن الحيوان الذي هو جزء اللفظ دال على مفهومه، ومفهومه جزء الماهية الإنسانية.
Chapter: On discussions of expressions. The singular has four divisions: that which has no part at all, that which has a part with no signification, that which has a part signifying [something] other than the intended part of the meaning, and that which has a part signifying [something] of the intended part of the meaning but its signification is not intended. If you say: What is the difference between the last two divisions? I say: The difference is that 'Abdullah' as a proper name does not have its part signify the intended part of the meaning, since neither of the two parts signifies anything of the specific essence. As for 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq' as a proper name, its part signifies the intended part of the meaning, but that signification is not intended. Its explanation: that 'al-Hayawan' which is part of the expression signifies its concept, and its concept is part of the human essence.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage defines the four types of singular expressions (المفرد) in logic. The classification is based on whether the expression has parts and whether those parts signify the intended meaning. The first type has no parts at all, e.g., a simple letter or word. The second has parts but none of them signify anything, like proper names consisting of meaningless syllables. The third has parts that signify something, but what they signify is not part of the intended meaning of the whole expression. The fourth has parts that signify some part of the intended meaning, but that signification is not the primary intention. The passage then clarifies the crucial difference between the third and fourth types using two proper name examples: 'Abdullah' as a proper name exemplifies the third division because its parts 'Abd' and 'Allah' individually signify 'servant' and 'God', but those meanings are not part of the intended meaning (the specific person). In contrast, 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq' (the Rational Animal) as a proper name for a specific human exemplifies the fourth division because 'al-Hayawan' signifies 'animal', which is part of the definition of 'human', but when used as a proper name, that signification is not intended; rather, the name refers to the individual. This distinction is important in logic to analyze how meaning is conveyed.

Key Terms
مفرد
جزء
دلالة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Division 4: parts signify part of intended meaning but not intended

Key Takeaway
The four divisions of singular expressions hinge on whether parts exist, whether they signify, and whether that signification matches the intended meaning.

Distinguishing the Third and Fourth Divisions
Author distinguishes last two divisions using proper name examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the third and fourth divisions of singular expressions by analyzing proper name examples like 'Abdullah' and 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq'.

Core Idea
A proper name may have parts that signify part of the essence or not, but neither intends the generic meaning.

The author anticipates confusion between the last two divisions. Both involve parts that signify something, but the key difference is whether what the parts signify is part of the intended meaning of the whole expression. This lesson walks through the two proper name examples to cement this distinction and show how it applies to familiar Islamic names.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proper names
The examples are 'Abdullah' and 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq'. Both are proper names (أعلام) that refer to specific entities—'Abdullah' to a person, 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq' to a specific human being.
2
Analyze 'Abdullah'
Break it into parts: 'Abd' and 'Allah'. Each part has clear signification: 'abd' = servant, 'Allah' = God. However, when used as a name for a person, the intended meaning is that specific individual, not the concepts 'servant' or 'God'. Therefore, the parts signify something other than the intended part of meaning.
This places 'Abdullah' in the third division.
3
Analyze 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq'
Break it into parts: 'al-Hayawan' (the animal) and 'al-Natiq' (the rational). The part 'al-Hayawan' signifies the concept 'animal', which is indeed part of the human essence (the intended meaning of the name refers to a specific human). However, when used as a name, the speaker does not intend to convey the generic meaning 'animal'; the name simply refers to that individual. Thus, the part signifies part of the intended meaning, but that signification is not intended.
This places 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq' in the fourth division.
4
Compare the two
The crucial test: Is what the part signifies part of the essence of the referent? For 'Abdullah', 'servant' and 'God' are not part of the person's essence (his specific identity). For 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq', 'animal' is part of the human essence. Thus, division 3: parts signify something outside the intended essence; division 4: parts signify something inside the intended essence but not intended.
Intention here refers to the primary purpose of the utterance—to refer, not to describe.
5
Apply to a new example
Consider the name 'Abū Bakr' (father of a young camel) as a name for a companion. The part 'Bakr' means 'young camel', which is not part of the person's essence. So 'Abū Bakr' belongs to division 3.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مباحثِ الألفاظ. فالمفرد أربعة أقسام: ما لا جزء له أصلاً، وما له جزء لا دلالة له، وما له جزء يدل على غير جزء المعنى المقصودِ، وما له جزء يدل على جزء المعنى المقصودِ لكن دلالته عليه غير مقصودة. فإن قلتَ: ما الفرق بين القسمين الأخيرين؟ قلتُ: الفرق أن «عبد الله» العَلم لا يدل جزء لفظه على جزء المعنى المقصود؛ إذ ليس شيء من الجزأين دالا على شيء من الذات المشخصة. وأما «الحيوان الناطق» عَلَماً فيدل جزء لفظه على جزء المعنى المقصود، لكن تلك الدلالة ليست بمقصودة. بيانه: أن الحيوان الذي هو جزء اللفظ دال على مفهومه، ومفهومه جزء الماهية الإنسانية.
Chapter: On discussions of expressions. The singular has four divisions: that which has no part at all, that which has a part with no signification, that which has a part signifying [something] other than the intended part of the meaning, and that which has a part signifying [something] of the intended part of the meaning but its signification is not intended. If you say: What is the difference between the last two divisions? I say: The difference is that 'Abdullah' as a proper name does not have its part signify the intended part of the meaning, since neither of the two parts signifies anything of the specific essence. As for 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq' as a proper name, its part signifies the intended part of the meaning, but that signification is not intended. Its explanation: that 'al-Hayawan' which is part of the expression signifies its concept, and its concept is part of the human essence.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage defines the four types of singular expressions (المفرد) in logic. The classification is based on whether the expression has parts and whether those parts signify the intended meaning. The first type has no parts at all, e.g., a simple letter or word. The second has parts but none of them signify anything, like proper names consisting of meaningless syllables. The third has parts that signify something, but what they signify is not part of the intended meaning of the whole expression. The fourth has parts that signify some part of the intended meaning, but that signification is not the primary intention. The passage then clarifies the crucial difference between the third and fourth types using two proper name examples: 'Abdullah' as a proper name exemplifies the third division because its parts 'Abd' and 'Allah' individually signify 'servant' and 'God', but those meanings are not part of the intended meaning (the specific person). In contrast, 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq' (the Rational Animal) as a proper name for a specific human exemplifies the fourth division because 'al-Hayawan' signifies 'animal', which is part of the definition of 'human', but when used as a proper name, that signification is not intended; rather, the name refers to the individual. This distinction is important in logic to analyze how meaning is conveyed.

Key Terms
مفرد
جزء
دلالة
علم

C) Because it has only one part
D) Because it is not a proper name

Key Takeaway
The crucial difference between the third and fourth divisions is whether the part's signification is part of the essence of the referent.

What Is Mantiq?

Two Types of Proper Names: Do Their Parts Indicate Parts of the Meaning?
Author distinguishes two types of proper names based on part-whole indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between proper names whose parts indicate parts of the intended meaning (unintentionally) and those that do not, and explain the logical significance of this distinction.

Core Idea
A proper name can have parts that indicate parts of its meaning, but that indication may be unintended.

In logic, proper names (أعلام) are considered simple terms, but not all are alike. Some proper names, like 'Abdullah', have parts that do not indicate anything about the specific individual named. Others, like 'the rational animal' used as a proper name, do have parts that indicate components of the individual's essence, but this indication is not the point of the name. This lesson unpacks that subtle distinction, which is crucial for understanding how definitions and terms relate to their referents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of proper names
The passage contrasts two types: one where no part of the word indicates any part of the intended meaning (e.g., 'Abdullah'), and one where a part does indicate a part of the intended meaning, but the indication is not the purpose of the name (e.g., 'the rational animal' used as a proper name).
2
Understand the criterion
The criterion is whether any part of the word (e.g., 'Abd' or 'Allah' in 'Abdullah') indicates a part of the specific individual's essence or identity. In 'Abdullah', neither 'Abd' nor 'Allah' indicates the particular person; they are just parts of the name.
This is about the intended meaning (المعنى المقصود), which is the specific individual, not any general concept.
3
Analyze 'Abdullah' as a proper name
The name 'Abdullah' is composed of two parts, but neither part indicates anything about the specific person named. For example, 'Abd' means 'slave' and 'Allah' means 'God', but these do not refer to components of that particular individual's identity. Thus, the name is considered simple in its reference, even though it has parts.
The glossators clarify that the parts may indicate other things, but not the intended meaning.
4
Analyze 'the rational animal' as a proper name
If someone uses 'the rational animal' as a proper name for a specific human, the word 'animal' indicates the concept 'animal', which is part of the human essence (jins). The human essence is part of the intended meaning (the individual human). So 'animal' indicates a part of the intended meaning. However, this indication is not the purpose of the name; the name is used just to refer to the person, not to describe him.
The passage uses the principle 'a part of a part is a part' to show the chain of indication.
5
Consider the glossators' refinement on particularization
Later glosses (especially from Yasin and al-Attar) discuss whether particularization (التشخص) is part of the intended meaning. They conclude that particularization is part of the individual's essence but not of the universal species essence. This nuance helps explain why the indication is considered unintended—the name primarily identifies the individual, not his universal properties.
This refinement is important for understanding how proper names relate to definitions and the five universals.

(قوله: لا يدل جزء لفظه على جزء المعنى المقصود) أي: وإن دل جزؤه على غير جزء المعنى المقصود. (قوله: بيانه) أي: بيان كون الحيوان الناطق علماً يدل جزوه على جزء المعنى المقصود دلالة غير مقصودة. Chapter: On Discussions of Words
If you say: What is the difference between the last two divisions? I say: The difference is that 'Abdullah as a proper name does not indicate by any part of its word a part of the intended meaning, since none of its two parts indicates anything of the particularized essence. As for 'the rational animal as a proper name', it indicates by a part of its word a part of the intended meaning, but that indication is not intended. Its explanation: That 'animal', which is part of the word, indicates its concept, and its concept is part of the human essence, and the human essence is part of the intended meaning, which is the human individual. Thus 'animal' indicates a part of the intended meaning, because a part of a part is a part.

(His statement: 'does not indicate by a part of its word a part of the intended meaning') meaning: even if its part indicates something other than the intended meaning. (His statement: 'its explanation') meaning: explanation of the fact that 'the rational animal as a proper name' indicates by its parts a part of the intended meaning with an unintended indication. (His statement: 'indicates its concept') which is a body that is sentient, growing, and moving by will. (His statement: 'and its concept') meaning: the concept of the preceding 'animal' is a part of the essence, i.e., the animality and rationality. And his statement: ('the human') meaning: because it is composed of it and of another, which is the rational.

Al-Attar: The meaning did not distinguish between that which has a part like Zayd and that which does not, like the letters of the alphabet, because the restrictions mentioned in the definition do not indicate it, neither explicitly nor necessarily, because what is mentioned is the restriction of indication, which entails the meaning. As for the generality of that meaning in being with a part or without, there is no indication upon it, because the absolute does not entail the general. End. By this you know the non-occurrence of the first. As for what was said that there remains the converse of the first in his statement, i.e., the converse of the first example... so in it: whether the pronoun is for the commentator or the glossator, this converse is mere estimation and assumption with no existence, and the like is not harmful because the discussion is about existing divisions. And in the 'Imadi glosses: that which is predicated of 'the point' does not have a part, not the concept of the point. As for the second, because he did not mention in the definition of the compound the restriction of order in hearing, and the commentator only dealt with the exclusions of the mentioned restrictions, it is not said:

Chapter: On Discussions of Words
(His statement: 'the human individual') meaning: attributed to human, because it is a part of him, because the individual is the essence with particularizers. (His statement: 'upon a part of the intended meaning') meaning: and the other part is particularization, this is his intended meaning, and there is consideration in it because particularization is outside the subject matter as will come in the discussion of species. End.

Yasin: And this is the reason for his command to reflect, and perhaps its reason also: that if 'the rational animal' is a proper name, its part does not indicate a part of the meaning at all. And there is consideration in Yasin's statement because we do not concede that particularization is outside the subject matter; yes, it is outside the human essence as will come, and this does not negate that it is a part of the subject matter, which is the human individual. (His statement: 'because a part') which is 'animal', and his statement: ('the part') meaning: the human essence, and his statement: ('a part') meaning: of the intended meaning.

Al-Attar: The intended meaning is that two divisions of the divisions of the simple remain in reality, even if they are not included under the negation of the mentioned restrictions, because we say: they are three, not two as Abul-Fath counted them. (His statement: 'and the human essence is a part of the intended meaning') meaning: and the other part is the individual. And what is in al-Muhaqqi of consideration that particularization is outside the subject matter due to what will come in the discussion of species, that it is the complete reality, is refuted by the fact that what comes in the species is that particularization is outside the reality of the individual that is common between it and other individuals sharing the same species reality. And here the individual is made a part of the identity named by that, which is the essence with particularization, i.e., the external individual. The outcome is that particularization not being a part of the essence of the universal concept that is the species does not negate its being a part of the essence of the individual. And Abd al-Hakim mentioned in the discussion of the species that particularization is an accident of the species; its relation to it is like the relation of the differentia to the genus; it is a part of the individual.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between two types of proper names (علم) regarding whether their word-parts indicate parts of the intended meaning. The author begins by answering a hypothetical question: 'What is the difference between the last two divisions?' The answer identifies two kinds. The first, exemplified by 'Abdullah' as a proper name, is such that no part of its word indicates any part of the intended meaning, because neither 'Abd' nor 'Allah' indicates the specific individual. The second, exemplified by 'the rational animal' used as a proper name, has its parts indicating parts of the intended meaning (e.g., 'animal' indicates a part of the human essence), but this indication is not the intended purpose of the name; it is incidental. The author provides a brief explanation: 'animal' as a word-part indicates the concept 'animal', which is part of the human essence, which is part of the intended meaning (the human individual). Thus, it indicates a part of the intended meaning because a part of a part is a part.

Then the glossators comment. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that the first type may indicate something else not intended. Al-Attar adds that the definition's restrictions do not cover all cases, and discusses the concept of 'compound' and 'simple'. Yasin raises an objection about particularization (التشخص) being outside the subject matter, but al-Attar refutes it by distinguishing between the universal concept of species and the individual. The passage concludes by affirming that particularization is part of the individual's essence but not of the species' essence.

What Is Mantiq?

The Division of Single Words by Independence in Predication
The author states the division of المفرد into اسم, فعل, حرف and explains the criterion of independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any single Arabic word as noun, verb, or particle based on whether it can independently convey a meaning that can be predicated alone, and identify exceptions like deficient verbs.

Core Idea
A single word's independence in conveying a concept determines whether it is a noun, verb, or particle.

This passage from the Tadhheeb introduces the foundational division of words into three categories. Understanding this division is crucial for anyone working with Arabic texts, especially in logic and usul. The criterion of independence in predication may seem straightforward, but the commentators show it has subtle implications, particularly for deficient verbs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the passage, find the three types: noun (اسم), verb (فعل), and particle (حرف). Notice that the division is based on the single word (المفرد).
These categories are standard in Arabic grammar.
2
Understand the criterion: independence in predication
The key phrase is 'إِنِ اسْتَقَلَّ بِالْإِخْبَارِ بِهِ وَحْدَهُ'. al-Dasuqi explains that independence here means conceptual independence: the word conveys a meaning by itself without needing another word. Predication alone then follows as a necessary consequence.
This criterion distinguishes nouns and verbs from particles.
3
Recognize the exception: deficient verbs
al-Razi raises the objection that deficient verbs (الأفعال الناقصة) like كان and ليس cannot be predicated alone. The answer accepts that they are not independent, implying they could be considered particles. This shows the division is not absolute but based on common usage.
This nuance guards against overgeneralization.
4
Apply the criterion to new examples
Practice classifying words like 'في' (particle, dependent), 'يكتب' (verb, independent), 'كتاب' (noun, independent). Consider deficient verbs like 'كان' – is it independent?
Apply the rule while being aware of exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام المفرد (وَهُوَ) أي: المفرد : (إِنِ اسْتَقَلَّ) بالإخبار به وحدَه الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : إِنِ اسْتَقَلَّ ) أي : بالمفهوميَّة؛ أي بإفادة المفهومِ بِنَفْسِهِ مِن غير احتياج لشيء آخر، وحينئذ ؛ فيلزمُ الإخبارُ بِهِ ،وحدَه ، فما فسَّر بِهِ الشَّارحُ الاستقلال؛ تفسير باللازم، وهذا بخلافِ الحرف، فإِنَّ فَهُم مَعناهُ يتوقَّفُ على ذكرِ المتعلِّقِ، فَمعنى العطار (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ إِنِ اسْتَقَلَّ) قدَّمَ هذا القسمَ لِكَوْنِ مَفهومِهِ وُجوديا، والقِسْمُ الثَّاني سلبٌ لِذلِكَ المفهوم الوجودي ، وسَلْبُ الشَّيء مُتأخّرُ عَنْ تَعقُلِ وجودِهِ، فهو كتقديم المركَّب على المفردِ، ومُحصِّلُ هذا التقسيم تقسيمُ اللَّفْظِ إلى كلمة واسم وأداة ووجه التّسمية، إمَّا بالأداةُ فَلِأَنَّها آلةٌ في تركيب الألفاظ بعضها مع بعض، وإما بالكلمة؛ فَلِأَنَّها مِنَ الكَلْمِ وهو الجرح ؛ كأنَّها لَمَّا دَلَّتْ على الزَّمَانِ وَهُوَ مُتجدِّدٌ ومتصرّم؛ تكلَّمَ الخاطرُ بِتغيرِ مَعنَاها ، وأمَّا بالاسمِ؛ فَلِأَنَّهُ أَعلى رتبةٌ مِن سائرِ الألفاظ ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ مُسْتَمِلاً على معنى الشمو. ; (قَوْلُهُ : بِالْإِخْبَارِ بِهِ وَحْدَهُ) قُيّدَ بِهِ؛ لأنَّ الأداةَ يخبرُ بِهَا مَعَ غَيْرِها كَزيدٍ هُوَ لا حجر ، فإنَّ لا جزء مِنَ المخبّرِ بِهِ قالَ الرَّازِيُّ : وَلَعَلَّكَ تقولُ : الأَفَعالُ النَّاقصةُ لَا تصلحُ لِأَنْ يُخبَّرَ بِهَا فيلزمُ أنْ تكون أدواتٍ، فَنقولُ : لَا بُعدَ في ذلكَ حَتَّى إِنَّهُم قَسَمُوا
Sections of the single word: (وَهُوَ) i.e. the single word: (إِنِ اسْتَقَلَّ) by predicating with it alone. al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'is independent') i.e. in terms of conceptualization; meaning by itself conveying the concept without need for anything else, and then predication with it alone necessarily follows; so what the expositor interpreted independence with is an interpretation by necessary consequence. This is unlike the particle, for understanding its meaning depends on mentioning the related. So the meaning. al-Attar: (His statement: 'it is independent') He presented this section because its concept is existential, and the second section is the negation of that existential concept, and the negation of a thing is posterior to conceptualizing its existence, thus it is like presenting the compound over the single word. The result of this division is the division of the word into noun, verb, and particle, and the reason for naming: the particle because it is a tool in composing words with each other; the verb because it comes from 'kalm' which is wound, as if when it indicates time which is renewing and passing, the mind speaks of the change of its meaning; the noun because it is higher in rank than other words because it is comprehensive of the meaning of 'shumu'. (His statement: 'by predicating with it alone') is restricted by this because the particle is predicated with other than it, like 'Zaid is not a stone', for 'la' is part of the predicate. al-Razi said: Perhaps you say: Deficient verbs are not suitable for predication with them, so they would be particles. We say: No impossibility in that, even until they divided.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines the division of single words (مفرد) into three types: noun (اسم), verb (فعل), and particle (حرف). The criterion is independence in conveying a meaning that can be predicated alone. al-Dasuqi explains that 'independence' here means conceptual independence, not necessarily predicability by itself, but predicability follows as a necessary consequence. Particles are dependent on other words. al-Attar adds a logical ordering: the independent part (اسم/فعل) is presented first because its concept is existential, while the dependent part (حرف) is a negation of that existential concept, which is posterior. Then he gives the etymological reasons for each name: particle (أداة) is a tool for composition; verb (كلمة) comes from 'wounding' because it indicates time that changes; noun (اسم) is higher rank. The final part restricts the condition 'predicating with it alone' because particles can be part of a predicate like 'لا' in 'Zaid is not a stone'. al-Razi anticipates an objection: deficient verbs (e.g., كان) are not independent for predication, so they would be particles. The answer accepts that, showing that the division is not absolute but based on common usage. This passage guards against the misunderstanding that verbs are always independent for predication.

Key Terms
المفرد
الاستقلال
الحرف
